Universals Do Not Entail Particulars or Opposites
Author argues that universal knowledge does not entail particular or opposite knowledge, and refutes a redefinition of opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's argument that universal knowledge does not entail knowledge of every particular or its opposite, and evaluate the rebuttal to the objector's redefinition of 'opposite'.

Core Idea
Knowing a universal gives you no guarantee of knowing every instance or its negation.

This passage tackles a common logical mistake: assuming that understanding a general concept gives complete knowledge of all its instances or its contradictory. The author illustrates this with clear examples and then refutes an objector who attempts to redefine the opposite in a way that would make such knowledge necessary. By the end, you will see how careful conceptual analysis protects against unwarranted entailments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
Find the opening claim: knowing a universal does not entail knowing every individual particular nor knowing its opposite (whether contradictory or contrary).
The author states this as a general rule.
2
Examine the examples
Notice the two examples: 'human' and 'the world is originated'. The author says you can know the universal without knowing every individual instance.
These are concrete cases from everyday and theological discourse.
3
Understand the objector's challenge
The objector grants the first point but objects to the claim about the opposite: he defines the opposite as the removal (raf‘) of the thing, and argues that knowing the thing implies knowing its removal.
The objector thinks removal is part of what is known.
4
Analyze the author's rebuttal
The author rejects the definition: the opposite is not removal but the 'non-thing' (al-la shay'), which is pure non-existence without mental identity, thus unknowable as a quiddity.
The author distinguishes between removal (which is relational) and non-existence (which is absolute).
5
Reflect on the epistemological limit
Understand the broader lesson: conceptual knowledge does not entail exhaustive knowledge of particulars or of pure negations; this protects against overextending what we claim to know.
This is crucial for theology and jurisprudence when dealing with universal terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالكلي المعرفة بكل فرد من أفراد جزئياته ولا بما يقابله أيضًا نقيضا كان ذلك أو ضدا. ألا ترى أنك تعرف الإنسان من حيث هو الإنسان؛ ولا تعرف كل فرد من أفراد الإنسان، وتعرف العالم حادث من حيث هو العالم؛ ولا تعرف كل فرد من أفراد العالم. وكذلك في كل شيء يمكن أن يكون مشتركا بين الأشياء فإنك إذا عرفته فلا يلزم من معرفته معرفة تلك الأشياء. ثم لقائل أن يقول: هب أنه كذلك لكن لما قلتم بأنه لا يلزم من معرفة الشيء معرفة نقيضه؛ ونقيض الشيء عبارة عن رفع ذلك الشيء في هذا الموضع، والمعرفة برفع الشيء من جملة ما يكون ضرورياً عند معرفة ذلك الشيء. لكنا نقول هذا في حيز المنع فإن نقيض الشيء عبارة عن اللا شيء، لا عن رفع ذلك الشيء وإلا لكان عدم الرفع عبارة عن ذلك الشيء. وذلك لا يمكن إذا كان ذلك الشيء أمرًا وجوديًا. ثم اللاشيء من هو اللاشيء عدم صرف، والعدم الصرف من قبيل ما لا هوية له في الذهن؛ وما يكون من هذا القبيل فلا يمكن معرفته بأنه ما هو.
Knowing the universal does not entail knowing every individual of its particulars, nor knowing what is opposite to it, whether a contradictory or a contrary. Do you not see that you know 'human' insofar as it is human, yet you do not know every individual human; and you know 'the world is originated' insofar as it is the world, yet you do not know every individual of the world. And similarly for everything that can be common among things, if you know it, knowledge of it does not necessitate knowledge of those things. Then someone may say: Suppose that is so, but since you said that knowledge of a thing does not necessitate knowledge of its opposite, and the opposite of a thing in this context is the removal of that thing, and knowledge of the removal of a thing is among what is necessary when knowing that thing. But we say this is subject to denial, for the opposite of a thing is the non-thing, not the removal of that thing; otherwise the non-removal would be that thing. And that is impossible if that thing is a positive entity. Moreover, the non-thing that is the non-thing is pure non-existence, and pure non-existence is of the kind that has no identity in the mind; and what is of this kind cannot be known as to what it is.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological argument about the limits of knowledge of universals. The author begins by stating a principle: knowing a universal (kulli) does not entail knowing every individual under it, nor does it entail knowing its opposite (whether contradictory or contrary). Two examples illustrate: knowing 'human' as a universal does not mean you know every individual human; knowing 'the world is originated' as a universal proposition does not mean you know every individual originated entity. The principle extends to any universal that is common among particulars. Then a hypothetical objector responds: Suppose we grant that, but you also claim that knowing a thing does not necessitate knowing its opposite (naqid). The objector argues that the opposite here is defined as the removal (raf‘) of that thing, and knowledge of removal is necessary when knowing the thing itself (since knowing what something is implies knowing what it is not). The author replies by rejecting the definition: the opposite of a thing is not its removal but rather the 'non-thing' (al-la shay'). If the opposite were the removal, then non-removal would be that thing, which is impossible for positive entities. Furthermore, the 'non-thing' is pure non-existence (al-‘adam al-sirf), which has no mental identity and thus cannot be known as a quiddity (ma huwa). The author concludes by cutting off the discussion as not fitting this context.

Key Terms
كلي
نقيض
عدم صرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can pure non-existence not be known as 'what it is'?
A) Because it is not a universal.
B) Because it has no mental identity.
C) Because it is a contradictory.
D) Because it is an opposite.

Key Takeaway
Universal knowledge does not entail knowledge of every particular or of pure negation; the opposite is not removal but unknowable non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task: Read the following claim: 'Whoever knows that Allah is Just knows that He does not create injustice.' Using the principle from the passage, evaluate whether knowledge of the universal 'Justice' entails knowledge of every particular act of justice or its opposite. Provide your reasoning.
?
Task: In a fiqh discussion, someone argues: 'If you know the general rule that every intoxicant is forbidden, then you know that this particular drink is forbidden.' Explain how the passage's principle applies to this statement.

The Difference Between Potential and Actual Knowledge
Author explains how potential knowledge coexists with actual ignorance or false belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between potential knowledge ('ilm bi'l-quwwa) and actual knowledge ('ilm bi'l-fi'l) and explain why potential knowledge does not automatically become actual knowledge without a productive arrangement in the mind.

Core Idea
Potential knowledge is not knowledge until the mind actively combines premises in a productive arrangement.

Have you ever known two facts but failed to draw the obvious conclusion? This common experience reveals a deep truth about how the mind works. In this lesson, we explore the Islamic logical distinction between potential and actual knowledge, using the example from the passage of knowing that a mule is barren and yet possibly thinking it pregnant.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of knowledge: 'ilm bi'l-fi'l (actual) and 'ilm bi'l-quwwa (potential)
Recognize that actual knowledge is consciously present and arranged in the mind, while potential knowledge consists of premises that could yield a conclusion but are not actively combined.
These terms are foundational in Islamic epistemology.
2
Understand the role of arrangement: tartib muntij
Actual knowledge requires premises to be combined in a specific productive order (major and minor premises arranged correctly). Without this arrangement, even if both premises are known, the conclusion remains potential.
This is why syllogisms must be properly formed.
3
Apply to the mule example
The passage gives the example: someone knows that 'this is a mule' and 'every mule is barren'. If these are not combined in the mind, he may still opine that the mule is pregnant. His knowledge of the premises is actual, but the conclusion (that this mule is barren) is only potential.
This shows that potential knowledge does not entail actual knowledge.
4
Distinguish from other cases
Note that the person may hold an opposite opinion (zann) if the premises are not combined. This is not a case of simultaneous actual knowledge and actual ignorance, but of actual knowledge of premises and potential knowledge of the conclusion, with an actual opinion contrary to that conclusion.
The author emphasizes that this is possible from different aspects (universal vs particular).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في أنه كيف يمكن أن يعلم الشيء ويجهل معا وإنه كيف يمكن أن يعلم ويظن به مقابل ما يعلم أنه كما أن المجيب قد تسلم مقدمات يلزم منها تبكيته ولا يشعر لأن تسليمها منه لا على الترتيب المنتج كذلك الإنسان فيما بينه وبين نفسه قد تكون عنده المقدمات التي يجب أن تعلم مع العلم بها شيء آخر. فيجهل ذلك الشيء لما أن المقدمات لا تكون حاضرة في ذهنه مرتبة في علمه بالفعل. وكذلك كيف يمكن أن يحهل الشيء ويعلم معا وأن يعلم ويظن به مقابل ما يعلم: فنقول إن السبب في أن يكون بالشيء علم وظن مقابلين بقياسين، أو أحدهما بقياس والآخر ليس بقياس؛ وذلك على جهتين إحديهما تستحيل أن يكون في إنسان واحد في وقت واحد بل قد يقع لإنسانين. وذلك إنه إذا كان مثلا كل «دب» و «ج ب» لا واسطة ثمَّ كل ب أ) وكل (ج) أ) أيضًا: فإن اعتقد واحدٌ أن «ب أ» وهو حق، واعتقد الآخر أن لا شيء من (ج) أ) وهو باطل؛ ثم قرن أن كل «دب» وأن كل «دج» من ذلك اعتقدا عقدين متقابلين. وأما الواحد فلا يمكن أن تنعقد عنده القياسات معا بالفعل فيرى شيئًا وضده من جهة إنتاجها إياهما معا. والجهة الثانية هي التي يمكن في واحد؛ وذلك أن ينعقد أن لا شيء من «ج أ»؛ ومع ذلك ينعقد في نفسه مقدمات قياس على هذه الصفة أن كل «دج» وكل «ج ب» وكل «ب» أ، فإنه ربما التفت فعلم من هذا بالفعل أن كل «د أ» وعلم أن كل ب أ) ولم يعلم أن كل «ج» «أب» الفعل. / وكان يجب أن تعلم ذلك إذا صرف إليه تأمله؛ إذ العلم بأن كل (ج) (ب) وكل ب أ يكون علما بالقوة أن كل «ج أ» قوة يصير بالفعل إذا أخطر مما على الترتيب قاصدا به أن يعلم لكنه لم يفعل ويظن أن لا شيء من «ج أ»، فالذي يعلمه ليس يعلمه إلا من جهة العلم بالكلي الذي يلزم منه أن يعلم وهو أن كل ب أ). وأما من جهة المخصوصة به فليس يعلمه كما أن إنسانا يعتقد أن الأجرام السماوية لا تشارك النار ثمَّ يحسب أن الكواكب نارية لأنها نيرة فهذه ظنه بالفعل مخصوص بالكواكب، وعلمه بها كلي توجد هي فيه بالقوة لأنه علم بالجملة أن كل جسم سماوي لا يشارك النار وأما أن الكواكب غير نارية فهو جزئي تحت هذا الحكم الكلي فليس من جهة واحدة علم وظن؛ وعلى هذا فإنه يفهم من بعد ما يكون من هذا القبيل. وكيف لا وقد علم أنه لا فرق بين أن يعلم الكبرى ولم يضع الأصغر تحت الأوسط بالفعل في أنه لا يجب أن تعلم النتيجة بالفعل، وبين أن يعلم الكبرى والصغرى معا، ولم يؤلف بيهما تأليفا منتجًا بالفعل : فإن وجودهما في النفس كيف اتفق لا يوجبان العلم بالنتيجة إلا وأن يكون بينهما تأليف مخصوص، وأن تكون النفس مراعية لذلك التأليف مثلا أن من يعلم أن هذه بغلة ويعلم أن كل بغلة عاقر ؛ فإذا لم يجمعهما في الذهن يمكن أن يظن مع ذلك أن هذه البغلة حامل. فالحاصل أنه لا يلزم من العلم بكل واحد منهما بالفعل شيء إلا وأن يحضرا في الذهن على الترتيب المذكور ونحو الغرض المقصود بل يلزم بالقوة؛ فعلى هذا ينبغي أن يفهم أن الشيء الواحد لا يمكن أن يعلم ويجهل في وقت واحد من جهة واحدة، وأن يزول شبهة دخل؛ قيل على سقراط الحكيم هل المطلوب عندك بالقياس معلوم أو مجهول ؟ فإن كان معلومًا فالطلب محال، وإن كان مجهولا فكيف تعرفه إذا وجدته؟ وهل يمكن أن يظفر الآبق إذا لم يعلمه بعينه فالحكيم لم يتعرض للمقدمات وما يتعلق بها بل عرفه بشكل هندسي أن المجهول كيف يضاد بالمعلوم. فأما تلميذه أفلاطون فقد تعرض لذلك كما أشار إليه الشيخ في الشفاء
Section: On how a thing can be known and unknown simultaneously, and how one can know and have an opinion opposite to what one knows. [He says:] Just as the respondent may accept premises from which his refutation follows without realizing it, because his acceptance is not in the productive order, similarly a person in himself may possess premises from which something else must be known along with knowledge of them. Yet he is ignorant of that other thing because the premises are not present in his mind arranged in actual knowledge. Likewise, how can one be ignorant of a thing and know it simultaneously, and know it and have an opinion opposite to what is known? We say: The cause for there being knowledge and an opposite opinion concerning a thing through two syllogisms, or one through a syllogism and the other not, is in two ways. One of them is impossible in a single person at a single time, but may occur in two persons. For example, if every D is B and every J is B without a middle term, then every B is A and every J is A as well: then if one believes that B is A, which is true, and another believes that no J is A, which is false; then they join that every D is B and every D is J, they hold two opposite beliefs. But a single person cannot have both syllogisms actually present in him so as to see a thing and its opposite from their both producing them simultaneously. The second way is possible in one person: that he hold that no J is A; yet in his soul there are premises of a syllogism of this form: every D is J, every J is B, and every B is A; then perhaps he turns his attention and actually knows from this that every D is A, and knows that every B is A, but does not actually know that every J is A. And it should be known when he directs his reflection to it; for knowledge that every J is B and every B is A is potential knowledge that every J is A, which becomes actual if he brings it to mind in the order, intending thereby to know; but he did not do so, and he opines that no J is A. So what he knows, he knows only from the aspect of the universal knowledge from which it follows that he knows, which is that every B is A. But from the particular aspect, he does not know it. Just as a person who believes that celestial bodies do not share the nature of fire, then supposes that the planets are fiery because they are luminous: this is his actual opinion specific to the planets, and his knowledge of them is universal, existing in them potentially, because he knows in general that every celestial body does not share the nature of fire; but that the planets are not fiery is a particular under this universal judgment; so it is not from one aspect knowledge and opinion. And thus what is of this kind will be understood afterwards. How not? It is known that there is no difference between knowing the major premise and not actually placing the minor under the middle, in that the conclusion need not actually be known, and between knowing both major and minor together but not combining them in a productive combination actually: for their existence in the soul however they occur does not necessitate knowledge of the conclusion unless there is between them a specific combination, and the soul observes that combination. For example, someone who knows that this is a mule and knows that every mule is barren; if he does not combine them in his mind, he can nevertheless opine that this mule is pregnant. So the upshot is that actual knowledge of each individual premise does not necessitate anything unless they are present in the mind in the aforementioned order, but rather necessitates potentially. So on this basis, one should understand that a single thing cannot be known and unknown at the same time from one aspect, and that a doubt that entered should be removed. It was said to the wise Socrates: 'Is the object sought by syllogism known or unknown? If it is known, the search is impossible; if it is unknown, how will you recognize it when you find it? And can the runaway slave be caught if he is not known by his essence?' So the wise man did not address the premises and what pertains to them, but defined for him through a geometric form how the unknown opposes the known. As for his student Plato, he addressed that, as the Shaykh indicated in al-Shifa.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological investigation into how a person can simultaneously know and not know the same thing, and how one can hold knowledge and an opposite opinion. The author begins by drawing an analogy with dialectical debate: just as a respondent may unknowingly accept premises that lead to his refutation because they are not arranged productively, so too a person may possess premises that entail another piece of knowledge but fail to actualize that knowledge because the premises are not arranged in the mind. The question is then posed: how can one be ignorant and know at the same time, or know and have an opposite opinion? The author explains that this occurs through two syllogisms leading to opposite conclusions, or one through a syllogism and the other not. He distinguishes two cases. The first case (impossible in one person) involves two different people each holding one syllogism that yields opposite conclusions, e.g., one believes every B is A, the other believes no J is A, and they both accept that every D is B and every D is J, leading to opposite beliefs about D. A single person cannot hold both actual syllogisms simultaneously. The second case (possible in one person) is when a person holds an opinion that no J is A, yet in his mind there are premises (every D is J, every J is B, every B is A) that potentially entail that every J is A. But he does not arrange them properly, so he remains ignorant of that conclusion and holds the opposite opinion. His knowledge of the premises is potential, not actual. The author illustrates with an example: someone believes celestial bodies are not fiery (a universal judgment), but then opines that particular planets are fiery because they are luminous. This person's knowledge of the universal (celestial bodies are not fiery) is potential regarding the particular planets, while his opinion about the planets is actual and specific. Thus, from one aspect he knows (the universal) and from another he opines (the particular). The author then emphasizes that simply knowing the premises individually does not entail actual knowledge of the conclusion; they must be combined in a specific way in the mind. He gives the example of knowing that this is a mule and that every mule is barren: if one does not combine these, one may still opine that this mule is pregnant. So actual knowledge of each premise does not necessitate the conclusion unless they are mentally arranged in the productive order. The passage concludes by recalling the Socratic paradox about inquiry: if the object sought is known, inquiry is futile; if unknown, how can we recognize it? The author notes that Socrates answered with a geometric demonstration, while his student Plato addressed the matter more fully, as indicated by Avicenna in al-Shifa.

Key Terms
علم بالفعل ('ilm bi'l-fi'l): Actual knowledge: the presence of a mental content explicitly and consciously held, such as when premises are arranged in a productive syllogism and the conclusion is immediately apprehended. [Note: No significant shift; this term is stable across the tradition.]
علم بالقوة ('ilm bi'l-quwwa): Potential knowledge: the possession of premises or principles that could lead to a conclusion but have not been explicitly combined in the mind to produce actual awareness of that conclusion. [Note: No significant shift; used consistently in Aristotelian and Islamic logic.]
ترتيب منتج (tartib muntij): Productive arrangement: the specific order of premises (major and minor) in a syllogism that yields a conclusion necessarily. [Note: In later works, the same concept is called 'hay'at al-qiyas'.]
ظن (zann): Conjecture or opinion: a belief that falls short of certainty, often contrary to what is known potentially or actually. [Note: In some later texts, zann is distinguished from wahm (estimation) as a separate faculty.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, why might a person who knows that every mule is barren and that this is a mule nevertheless think the mule is pregnant?
A) Because the person does not know the minor premise
B) Because the premises are not combined in the mind in a productive arrangement
C) Because the person is ignorant of the major premise
D) Because the conclusion is unknown in principle

Key Takeaway
Actual knowledge of premises does not automatically yield conclusion; a productive arrangement in the mind is required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider someone who knows that 'every intoxicant is forbidden' and knows that 'this beverage is an intoxicant', but does not combine them in his mind, and thus considers the beverage permissible. Identify which knowledge is potential and which is actual, and explain what is missing.

How Knowledge and Ignorance Can Coexist
Author explains how potential knowledge coexists with actual ignorance or false belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two scenarios described in the passage where knowledge and ignorance or opposite opinion coexist: the impossible scenario (two persons) and the possible scenario (one person with potential knowledge).

Core Idea
Knowledge and ignorance can coexist if one is potential and the other is actual, but never actual knowledge and actual ignorance of the same thing from the same aspect.

The author addresses a perplexing philosophical question: how can you know something and yet hold an opposite opinion? He provides two distinct scenarios: one involving two people who each have syllogisms leading to opposite conclusions, and another involving a single person who has potential knowledge but holds an actual contrary opinion. This lesson unpacks these scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first scenario (two persons)
The author describes two different people each having a syllogism that leads to opposite conclusions about the same subject. For example, one believes 'every B is A' (true) and another believes 'no J is A' (false), and they both also hold that 'every D is B' and 'every D is J'. This leads them to opposite beliefs about D. This cannot happen in one person because the mind cannot hold two opposite actual syllogisms simultaneously.
This scenario is impossible for a single individual at one time.
2
Identify the second scenario (one person)
A single person can hold an opinion (e.g., 'no J is A') while having in his mind premises that potentially entail the opposite (e.g., 'every J is B' and 'every B is A'). He fails to arrange these premises properly, so his knowledge remains potential, and he actualizes only the contrary opinion.
This scenario is possible because the knowledge is potential, not actual.
3
Analyze the celestial bodies example
The author gives an example: someone knows universally that 'every celestial body does not share the nature of fire', but opines particularly that 'the planets are fiery because they are luminous'. His knowledge about the planets is universal and potential (since it is a general rule), while his opinion about the planets is actual and specific. Thus, from the universal aspect he knows, from the particular aspect he opines.
This shows how knowledge and opinion can coexist from different aspects.
4
General principle
The key principle is that a single thing cannot be known and unknown from exactly the same aspect. However, knowledge from one aspect (e.g., universal) can coexist with ignorance or opinion from another aspect (e.g., particular) if the mind does not apply the universal to the particular.
This resolves the paradox of simultaneous knowledge and ignorance.

Source Text

فيما بينه وبين نفسه قد تكون عنده المقدمات التي يجب أن تعلم مع العلم بها شيء آخر. فيجهل ذلك الشيء لما أن المقدمات لا تكون حاضرة في ذهنه مرتبة في علمه بالفعل. وكذلك كيف يمكن أن يحهل الشيء ويعلم معا وأن يعلم ويظن به مقابل ما يعلم: فنقول إن السبب في أن يكون بالشيء علم وظن مقابلين بقياسين، أو أحدهما بقياس والآخر ليس بقياس؛ وذلك على جهتين إحديهما تستحيل أن يكون في إنسان واحد في وقت واحد بل قد يقع لإنسانين. وذلك إنه إذا كان مثلا كل «دب» و «ج ب» لا واسطة ثمَّ كل ب أ) وكل (ج) أ) أيضًا: فإن اعتقد واحدٌ أن «ب أ» وهو حق، واعتقد الآخر أن لا شيء من (ج) أ) وهو باطل؛ ثم قرن أن كل «دب» وأن كل «دج» من ذلك اعتقدا عقدين متقابلين. وأما الواحد فلا يمكن أن تنعقد عنده القياسات معا بالفعل فيرى شيئًا وضده من جهة إنتاجها إياهما معا. والجهة الثانية هي التي يمكن في واحد؛ وذلك أن ينعقد أن لا شيء من «ج أ»؛ ومع ذلك ينعقد في نفسه مقدمات قياس على هذه الصفة أن كل «دج» وكل «ج ب» وكل «ب» أ، فإنه ربما التفت فعلم من هذا بالفعل أن كل «د أ» وعلم أن كل ب أ) ولم يعلم أن كل «ج» «أب» الفعل. / وكان يجب أن تعلم ذلك إذا صرف إليه تأمله؛ إذ العلم بأن كل (ج) (ب) وكل ب أ يكون علما بالقوة أن كل «ج أ» قوة يصير بالفعل إذا أخطر مما على الترتيب قاصدا به أن يعلم لكنه لم يفعل ويظن أن لا شيء من «ج أ»، فالذي يعلمه ليس يعلمه إلا من جهة العلم بالكلي الذي يلزم منه أن يعلم وهو أن كل ب أ). وأما من جهة المخصوصة به فليس يعلمه كما أن إنسانا يعتقد أن الأجرام السماوية لا تشارك النار ثمَّ يحسب أن الكواكب نارية لأنها نيرة فهذه ظنه بالفعل مخصوص بالكواكب، وعلمه بها كلي توجد هي فيه بالقوة لأنه علم بالجملة أن كل جسم سماوي لا يشارك النار وأما أن الكواكب غير نارية فهو جزئي تحت هذا الحكم الكلي فليس من جهة واحدة علم وظن؛ وعلى هذا فإنه يفهم من بعد ما يكون من هذا القبيل. وكيف لا وقد علم أنه لا فرق بين أن يعلم الكبرى ولم يضع الأصغر تحت الأوسط بالفعل في أنه لا يجب أن تعلم النتيجة بالفعل، وبين أن يعلم الكبرى والصغرى معا، ولم يؤلف بيهما تأليفا منتجًا بالفعل : فإن وجودهما في النفس كيف اتفق لا يوجبان العلم بالنتيجة إلا وأن يكون بينهما تأليف مخصوص، وأن تكون النفس مراعية لذلك التأليف مثلا أن من يعلم أن هذه بغلة ويعلم أن كل بغلة عاقر ؛ فإذا لم يجمعهما في الذهن يمكن أن يظن مع ذلك أن هذه البغلة حامل. فالحاصل أنه لا يلزم من العلم بكل واحد منهما بالفعل شيء إلا وأن يحضرا في الذهن على الترتيب المذكور ونحو الغرض المقصود بل يلزم بالقوة؛ فعلى هذا ينبغي أن يفهم أن الشيء الواحد لا يمكن أن يعلم ويجهل في وقت واحد من جهة واحدة، وأن يزول شبهة دخل؛ قيل على سقراط الحكيم هل المطلوب عندك بالقياس معلوم أو مجهول ؟ فإن كان معلومًا فالطلب محال، وإن كان مجهولا فكيف تعرفه إذا وجدته؟ وهل يمكن أن يظفر الآبق إذا لم يعلمه بعينه فالحكيم لم يتعرض للمقدمات وما يتعلق بها بل عرفه بشكل هندسي أن المجهول كيف يضاد بالمعلوم. فأما تلميذه أفلاطون فقد تعرض لذلك كما أشار إليه الشيخ في الشفاء
Section: On how a thing can be known and unknown simultaneously, and how one can know and have an opinion opposite to what one knows. [He says:] Just as the respondent may accept premises from which his refutation follows without realizing it, because his acceptance is not in the productive order, similarly a person in himself may possess premises from which something else must be known along with knowledge of them. Yet he is ignorant of that other thing because the premises are not present in his mind arranged in actual knowledge. Likewise, how can one be ignorant of a thing and know it simultaneously, and know it and have an opinion opposite to what is known? We say: The cause for there being knowledge and an opposite opinion concerning a thing through two syllogisms, or one through a syllogism and the other not, is in two ways. One of them is impossible in a single person at a single time, but may occur in two persons. For example, if every D is B and every J is B without a middle term, then every B is A and every J is A as well: then if one believes that B is A, which is true, and another believes that no J is A, which is false; then they join that every D is B and every D is J, they hold two opposite beliefs. But a single person cannot have both syllogisms actually present in him so as to see a thing and its opposite from their both producing them simultaneously. The second way is possible in one person: that he hold that no J is A; yet in his soul there are premises of a syllogism of this form: every D is J, every J is B, and every B is A; then perhaps he turns his attention and actually knows from this that every D is A, and knows that every B is A, but does not actually know that every J is A. And it should be known when he directs his reflection to it; for knowledge that every J is B and every B is A is potential knowledge that every J is A, which becomes actual if he brings it to mind in the order, intending thereby to know; but he did not do so, and he opines that no J is A. So what he knows, he knows only from the aspect of the universal knowledge from which it follows that he knows, which is that every B is A. But from the particular aspect, he does not know it. Just as a person who believes that celestial bodies do not share the nature of fire, then supposes that the planets are fiery because they are luminous: this is his actual opinion specific to the planets, and his knowledge of them is universal, existing in them potentially, because he knows in general that every celestial body does not share the nature of fire; but that the planets are not fiery is a particular under this universal judgment; so it is not from one aspect knowledge and opinion. And thus what is of this kind will be understood afterwards. How not? It is known that there is no difference between knowing the major premise and not actually placing the minor under the middle, in that the conclusion need not actually be known, and between knowing both major and minor together but not combining them in a productive combination actually: for their existence in the soul however they occur does not necessitate knowledge of the conclusion unless there is between them a specific combination, and the soul observes that combination. For example, someone who knows that this is a mule and knows that every mule is barren; if he does not combine them in his mind, he can nevertheless opine that this mule is pregnant. So the upshot is that actual knowledge of each individual premise does not necessitate anything unless they are present in the mind in the aforementioned order, but rather necessitates potentially. So on this basis, one should understand that a single thing cannot be known and unknown at the same time from one aspect, and that a doubt that entered should be removed. It was said to the wise Socrates: 'Is the object sought by syllogism known or unknown? If it is known, the search is impossible; if it is unknown, how will you recognize it when you find it? And can the runaway slave be caught if he is not known by his essence?' So the wise man did not address the premises and what pertains to them, but defined for him through a geometric form how the unknown opposes the known. As for his student Plato, he addressed that, as the Shaykh indicated in al-Shifa.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological investigation into how a person can simultaneously know and not know the same thing, and how one can hold knowledge and an opposite opinion. The author begins by drawing an analogy with dialectical debate: just as a respondent may unknowingly accept premises that lead to his refutation because they are not arranged productively, so too a person may possess premises that entail another piece of knowledge but fail to actualize that knowledge because the premises are not arranged in the mind. The question is then posed: how can one be ignorant and know at the same time, or know and have an opposite opinion? The author explains that this occurs through two syllogisms leading to opposite conclusions, or one through a syllogism and the other not. He distinguishes two cases. The first case (impossible in one person) involves two different people each holding one syllogism that yields opposite conclusions, e.g., one believes every B is A, the other believes no J is A, and they both accept that every D is B and every D is J, leading to opposite beliefs about D. A single person cannot hold both actual syllogisms simultaneously. The second case (possible in one person) is when a person holds an opinion that no J is A, yet in his mind there are premises (every D is J, every J is B, every B is A) that potentially entail that every J is A. But he does not arrange them properly, so he remains ignorant of that conclusion and holds the opposite opinion. His knowledge of the premises is potential, not actual. The author illustrates with an example: someone believes celestial bodies are not fiery (a universal judgment), but then opines that particular planets are fiery because they are luminous. This person's knowledge of the universal (celestial bodies are not fiery) is potential regarding the particular planets, while his opinion about the planets is actual and specific. Thus, from one aspect he knows (the universal) and from another he opines (the particular). The author then emphasizes that simply knowing the premises individually does not entail actual knowledge of the conclusion; they must be combined in a specific way in the mind. He gives the example of knowing that this is a mule and that every mule is barren: if one does not combine these, one may still opine that this mule is pregnant. So actual knowledge of each premise does not necessitate the conclusion unless they are mentally arranged in the productive order. The passage concludes by recalling the Socratic paradox about inquiry: if the object sought is known, inquiry is futile; if unknown, how can we recognize it? The author notes that Socrates answered with a geometric demonstration, while his student Plato addressed the matter more fully, as indicated by Avicenna in al-Shifa.

Key Terms
علم بالفعل ('ilm bi'l-fi'l): Actual knowledge: the presence of a mental content explicitly and consciously held, such as when premises are arranged in a productive syllogism and the conclusion is immediately apprehended. [Note: No significant shift; this term is stable across the tradition.]
علم بالقوة ('ilm bi'l-quwwa): Potential knowledge: the possession of premises or principles that could lead to a conclusion but have not been explicitly combined in the mind to produce actual awareness of that conclusion. [Note: No significant shift; used consistently in Aristotelian and Islamic logic.]
ترتيب منتج (tartib muntij): Productive arrangement: the specific order of premises (major and minor) in a syllogism that yields a conclusion necessarily. [Note: In later works, the same concept is called 'hay'at al-qiyas'.]
ظن (zann): Conjecture or opinion: a belief that falls short of certainty, often contrary to what is known potentially or actually. [Note: In some later texts, zann is distinguished from wahm (estimation) as a separate faculty.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in the example of celestial bodies and planets, from which aspect does the person have knowledge of the planets?
A) From the particular aspect
B) From the universal aspect
C) From both aspects
D) From no aspect

Key Takeaway
A single person cannot have actual knowledge and actual ignorance of the same thing from the same aspect, but can have potential knowledge with actual ignorance or opposite opinion from a different aspect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
A scholar knows that 'every form of usury is forbidden' (universal) but thinks that a particular transaction is not usury because he has not combined the universal with the particular. Is this a case of knowledge coexisting with ignorance? Explain using the author's framework.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq

Resolving the Paradox of Inquiry: Distinguishing Aspects of Knowledge
Author resolves the Socratic paradox by distinguishing aspects of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the Socratic paradox of inquiry and apply the distinction of aspects (known vs unknown from different respects) to reconcile apparently contradictory knowledge states.

Core Idea
A thing can be known in one respect and unknown in another; the paradox dissolves when aspects are distinguished.

The Socratic paradox questions how we can search for what we do not know. The author resolves it by showing that the sought object is known from one aspect (e.g., its essence or a identifying mark) and unknown from another (e.g., its location or particular state). This distinction is essential for understanding how syllogistic inquiry works in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
State the paradox
The paradox: If the object of inquiry is known, search is unnecessary; if unknown, it cannot be recognized when found. Identify the dilemma in the passage.
The passage presents the paradox attributed to Socrates.
2
Extract the author's examples
Find the examples of the runaway slave and the celestial bodies. Note how each illustrates a distinction between universal knowledge and particular ignorance.
The slave is known 'who he is' but unknown 'where he is'.
3
Identify the two aspects
For each example, specify what is known and what is unknown. For the slave: known by a specific mark (essence/identity) but unknown by location. For celestial bodies: universal knowledge (celestial bodies are not fiery) but particular ignorance (planets might be fiery).
The author states that the sought is known from one aspect and unknown from another.
4
Connect to the middle term
The identifying mark of the runaway slave is the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) that connects the major and minor terms in a syllogism. Recognize how this resolves the paradox: the middle term provides partial knowledge.
The middle term is the link that allows the conclusion to be discovered.

وأما من جهة المخصوصة به فليس يعلمه كما أن إنسانا يعتقد أن الأجرام السماوية لا تشارك النار ثمَّ يحسب أن الكواكب نارية لأنها نيرة فهذه ظنه بالفعل مخصوص بالكواكب، وعلمه بها كلي توجد هي فيه بالقوة لأنه علم بالجملة أن كل جسم سماوي لا يشارك النار وأما أن الكواكب غير نارية فهو جزئي تحت هذا الحكم الكلي فليس من جهة واحدة علم وظن؛ وعلى هذا فإنه يفهم من بعد ما يكون من هذا القبيل. وكيف لا وقد علم أنه لا فرق بين أن يعلم الكبرى ولم يضع الأصغر تحت الأوسط بالفعل في أنه لا يجب أن تعلم النتيجة بالفعل، وبين أن يعلم الكبرى والصغرى معا، ولم يؤلف بينهما تأليفا منتجًا بالفعل : فإن وجودهما في النفس كيف اتفق لا يوجبان العلم بالنتيجة إلا وأن يكون بينهما تأليف مخصوص، وأن تكون النفس مراعية لذلك التأليف مثلا أن من يعلم أن هذه بغلة ويعلم أن كل بغلة عاقر ؛ فإذا لم يجمعهما في الذهن يمكن أن يظن مع ذلك أن هذه البغلة حامل. فالحاصل أنه لا يلزم من العلم بكل واحد منهما بالفعل شيء إلا وأن يحضرا في الذهن على الترتيب المذكور ونحو الغرض المقصود بل يلزم بالقوة؛ فعلى هذا ينبغي أن يفهم أن الشيء الواحد لا يمكن أن يعلم ويجهل في وقت واحد من جهة واحدة، وأن يزول شبهة دخل؛ قيل على سقراط الحكيم هل المطلوب عندك بالقياس معلوم أو مجهول ؟ فإن كان معلومًا فالطلب محال، وإن كان مجهولا فكيف تعرفه إذا وجدته؟ وهل يمكن أن يظفر الآبق إذا لم يعلمه بعينه فالحكيم لم يتعرض للمقدمات وما يتعلق بها بل عرفه بشكل هندسي أن المجهول كيف يضاد بالمعلوم. فأما تلميذه أفلاطون فقد تعرض لذلك كما أشار إليه الشيخ في الشفاء. وخلاصة الكلام في هذا المقام أن المطلوب يُعلم لا من الوجه الذي يجهل، ويُجهل لا من الوجه الذي يعلم؛ فليس إذن أن يجهل كل الجهل حتى إذا وجد فلا يُعلم أنه هو، ولا يعلم كل العلم حتى يكون غنيا عن الطلب. فالآبق مثلا يعرف بعلامة مخصوصة به أنه من هو ؟ لكن لا يعرف أنه «أين هو ؟»، فتلك العلامة هي الحد الأوسط عند الطرف. وبالجملة / فالمعرفة إما خاصة وإما عامة. وأيضًا فإن المعرفة إما معرفة بالقوة وإما معرفة بالفعل، ولهذا يمكن أن يكون الشخص عارفًا بالكلي وهو جاهل بالجزئي لا الجهل الذي هو عدم العلم بل الجهل المضاد للعلم مثلا إذا علمنا أن هذه النقلة فرطية ثم رأيناها فحسبنا أنها محققة لعلمنا أنها خير أو غير تلك النقلة. وعلى هذا فإنه قد يظن خيرًا ما كالشجاعة مثلا أنه شر؛ وقد علمنا أن الشر ليس بخير أي شيء كان فيكون طيبا أن خيرًا ما ليس بخير. وقد يعرض للانسان من جهة أخرى علم وظن لشيء واحد معا من جهة، وليس معا من جهة مثل أن الذهن قد يصدق من جهة القياس أن ليس خارج العالم خلاء ولا ملاء؛ ثم إنه إذا أعرض عن ذلك القياس ونظر في الأمر نفسه خاب الوهمية فرسمت وجوب أن يكون هناك خلاء أو ملاء فتتبعها النفس فتظن أن هناك خلاء أو ملاء على سبيل عقله. ثم إذا تذكَّرت عن ذلك القياس انفعلت عن طاعة الوهم فيكون هناك من جهة علم وظن معا، ومن جهة ليس: أما الجهة الأولى فلأن هذا العلم قد اكتسب وحصل وليس يحتاج أن يتألف طلبه كالمشكوك فيه بقياس يستفاد والظن طارئ على هذا العلم وهو مكتسب. وفرق بين هذا المكتسب الذي هو معرض عنه وبين المجهول مطلقا. وأما الجهة الثانية فلاستحالة أن يخطرا جميعًا بالبال أن ليس هناك خلاء أو ملاء بالقياس الموجب لذلك ؛ والنفس تظن أن هناك خلاء أو ملاء، وإن كانت القوة الوهمية مصمّمة على ذلك؛ إذ ليس الوهم والظن شيئًا واحدا على ما عرف.
As for what is specific to it, he does not know it, just as a man believes that the celestial bodies do not share nature with fire, then he supposes that the planets are fiery because they are luminous. This opinion of his is actually specific to the planets, but his knowledge of them is universal, existing in him potentially, because he knows in general that every celestial body does not share nature with fire. But that the planets are not fiery is a particular under this universal judgment, so it is not from one aspect knowledge and opinion. And thus one understands subsequently anything of this sort. How could it not be, since it is known that there is no difference between knowing the major premise and not actually placing the minor under the middle, so that the conclusion is not necessarily known actually, and between knowing both the major and minor, without composing them into a productive composition actually: for their existence in the soul however they happen does not necessitate knowledge of the conclusion unless there is a specific composition between them and the soul observes that composition. For example, one who knows that this is a mule and knows that every mule is barren: if he does not combine them in his mind, he may still suppose that this mule is pregnant. The upshot is that actual knowledge of each one individually does not entail anything unless they are present in the mind in the mentioned order and similar to the intended purpose; rather, it entails potentially. So on this basis, one should understand that a single thing cannot be known and unknown at the same time from one aspect, and that the doubt that entered should be removed. It was said to the wise Socrates: Is the object sought through syllogism known or unknown? If it is known, then the search is impossible; and if it is unknown, how will you recognize it when you find it? And can a runaway slave be captured if he is not known by his very self? The wise man did not address the premises and what pertains to them, but rather defined by a geometrical form how the unknown is opposed to the known. As for his student Plato, he did address that, as the Shaykh indicated in the Shifa'. The summary of the discourse in this context is that the sought is known from the aspect from which it is unknown, and unknown from the aspect from which it is known; it is not then completely unknown such that when found it is not known to be it, nor completely known such that it is in no need of search. For example, the runaway slave is known by a specific mark: who he is? But he does not know 'where is he?'. That mark is the middle term in relation to the end. In general, knowledge is either specific or general. Also, knowledge is either potential or actual. For this reason, a person can be knower of the universal while ignorant of the particular—not the ignorance which is absence of knowledge, but the ignorance contrary to knowledge. For example, if we know that this transfer is excessive, then we see it and we suppose it is verified because we know it is good or other than that transfer. On this basis, one may suppose something good, like courage, to be evil; and we have known that evil is not good, whatever it may be. So it is acceptable that a good thing is not good. And from another aspect, a person may have knowledge and opinion of a single thing together from one aspect, but not together from another aspect. For example, the mind may assent from the aspect of syllogism that there is neither void nor plenum outside the world; then if it turns away from that syllogism and looks into the matter itself, the estimative faculty fails and it depicts the necessity of there being void or plenum, so the soul follows it and supposes that there is void or plenum by way of its intellect. Then when it recalls that syllogism, it is affected by obedience to the estimative faculty. So there is from one aspect knowledge and opinion together, and from another aspect not: as for the first aspect, because this knowledge has been acquired and obtained and does not need its pursuit to be composed like that which is doubted through a syllogism that yields benefit—and opinion is incidental to this knowledge and it is acquired. There is a difference between this acquired knowledge that is ignored and the absolutely unknown. As for the second aspect, because it is impossible for both to occur in the mind: that there is no void or plenum through the syllogism necessitating that, while the soul supposes that there is void or plenum, even if the estimative faculty is determined on that; for the estimative faculty and opinion are not one and the same thing, as has been known.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion addressing the famous Socratic paradox of inquiry: how can we seek what we do not know, and how would we recognize it if found? The author begins by illustrating the distinction between universal knowledge and particular opinion. For example, someone may hold the universal belief that celestial bodies do not share the nature of fire, but mistakenly think that planets are fiery due to their brightness. Here, the person's opinion is particular to the planets, while his knowledge is universal and potential. This shows that from one aspect, the same thing can be known and opined differently. The author then emphasizes that knowledge of premises does not automatically yield the conclusion unless they are actually combined in the mind in the correct order. He gives the example of a mule: knowing both that 'this is a mule' and 'every mule is barren' does not prevent one from supposing the mule is pregnant if the premises are not actively composed. So, knowledge remains potential until actual composition. From this, the author asserts that a single thing cannot be known and unknown from the same aspect at the same time, thus resolving the doubt. He presents the Socratic paradox: if the object of inquiry is known, search is unnecessary; if unknown, it cannot be recognized. The author notes that Socrates did not resolve this, but Plato addressed it, as referenced in Avicenna's Shifa. The resolution is that the sought is known from one aspect and unknown from another. For example, a runaway slave is known by his identity (who he is) but unknown by his location (where he is). The identifying mark serves as the middle term in the syllogism. Knowledge is either particular or general, and either potential or actual. Thus, one can know the universal while being ignorant of the particular—not a total absence of knowledge, but a specific ignorance contrary to knowledge. For instance, if we know that a certain thing is excessive, we might mistakenly view it as verified. Similarly, one may suppose something good like courage to be evil, even though we know evil is not good; this is a case of opinion conflicting with knowledge. Finally, the author discusses how a person can have both knowledge and opinion of the same thing from different aspects. The example: from a demonstrative syllogism, the mind affirms that there is no void or plenum outside the world. But when the syllogism is set aside and the matter is considered by the estimative faculty, one may form the opinion that there is void or plenum. When the original syllogism is recalled, the soul is pulled between the two. In this case, from one aspect (the syllogistic proof) there is knowledge, and from another aspect (the estimative judgment) there is opinion. The two are not simultaneous in the same respect because the estimative faculty's opinion is incidental and the soul cannot hold both the proof and the opinion as active at the same moment. The author stresses that the estimative faculty and opinion are distinct, as previously established.

Key Terms
علم ('ilm): Certain knowledge, either universal or particular, actual or potential; contrasted with opinion (zann). [Note: No significant shift; this is standard usage in Islamic logic.]
ظن (zann): Opinion or supposition, often fallible; can coexist with knowledge when from different aspects. [Note: In later usul al-fiqh, zann often denotes probable knowledge; here it is merely opinion without certainty.]
الحد الأوسط (al-hadd al-awsat): Middle term in a syllogism; here it is the specific mark (e.g., the identity of the runaway slave) that connects the major and minor terms. [Note: Standard syllogistic term; no shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between the runaway slave's identifying mark and the middle term in a syllogism?
A) The mark is the conclusion of the syllogism.
B) The mark is the middle term that connects the known identity to the unknown location.
C) The mark is irrelevant to the syllogism.
D) The mark is the major term that stands for the slave's location.

Key Takeaway
The sought is known from one aspect (e.g., its mark) and unknown from another (e.g., its location), resolving the paradox that we cannot search for what is completely unknown.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Using the distinction of aspects, resolve the following apparent contradiction: A jurist knows that all intoxicants are forbidden (universal knowledge), but he is uncertain whether a particular substance (e.g., nabidh) is an intoxicant. Explain how this is not a contradiction according to the passage.
?
2. Take the classic Kalam example: We know that the world has a Creator (known in essence), but we do not know the exact nature of His creative act (unknown in modality). Identify the aspects of knowledge and ignorance, and show how this parallels the runaway slave example.

From Potential to Actual: The Necessity of Composing Premises
Author explains that premises must be composed actually to yield the conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why knowing individual premises does not automatically entail knowing the conclusion, and differentiate potential from actual knowledge using the example of the barren mule.

Core Idea
Knowing premises individually does not produce knowledge of the conclusion unless they are actually composed in the mind.

The author emphasizes that even if you know both the major and minor premises, the conclusion remains only potential unless the premises are actively combined in the correct order. He illustrates this with the engaging example of a person who knows that a particular animal is a mule and that all mules are barren, yet still supposes the mule might be pregnant. This underscores the difference between having knowledge potentially and actually.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
Find the statement in the passage that says knowing the premises individually does not necessitate knowledge of the conclusion. The author says there is no difference between knowing only the major premise and knowing both premises without composing them.
The key phrase: 'their existence in the soul however they happen does not necessitate knowledge of the conclusion.'
2
Walk through the mule example
Break down the example: the person knows (1) 'this is a mule' and (2) 'every mule is barren'. But if he does not actively combine them in his mind, he may still suppose the mule is pregnant. This shows that the knowledge is only potential until composition.
The example is powerful because it shows that even with both premises, false opinion can persist.
3
Relate to potential vs. actual knowledge
The author distinguishes knowledge bil-quwwa (potential) and bil-fi'l (actual). The premises are known actually, but the conclusion is known only potentially until composition. Recognize that this is a key epistemological point in Islamic logic.
Potential knowledge is present but not yet activated; actual knowledge is active and producing conclusions.
4
Apply to the void/plenum example
The passage later gives an example of knowledge from a syllogism (that there is no void/plenum) and opinion from the estimative faculty. When the syllogism is not actively considered, the conclusion is not actually present; opinion takes over. This illustrates the same point about actual composition.
This connects potential/actual knowledge with the coexistence of knowledge and opinion from different aspects.

في وقت واحد من جهة واحدة، وأن يزول شبهة دخل؛ قيل على سقراط الحكيم هل المطلوب عندك بالقياس معلوم أو مجهول ؟ فإن كان معلومًا فالطلب محال، وإن كان مجهولا فكيف تعرفه إذا وجدته؟ وهل يمكن أن يظفر الآبق إذا أنه من هو ؟ لكن لا يعرف أنه «أين هو ؟»، فتلك العلامة هي الحد الأوسط عند الطرف. وبالجملة / فالمعرفة إما خاصة وإما عامة. وأيضًا فإن المعرفة إما معرفة بالقوة وإما معرفة بالفعل، ولهذا يمكن أن يكون الشخص عارفًا بالكلي وهو جاهل بالجزئي لا الجهل الذي هو عدم العلم بل الجهل المضاد للعلم مثلا إذا علمنا أن هذه النقلة فرطية ثم رأيناها فحسبنا أنها محققة لعلمنا أنها خير أو غير تلك النقلة. وعلى هذا فإنه قد يظن خيرًا ما كالشجاعة مثلا أنه شر؛ وقد علمنا أن الشر ليس بخير أي شيء كان فيكون طيبا أن خيرًا ما ليس بخير. وقد يعرض للانسان من جهة أخرى علم وظن لشيء واحد معا من جهة، وليس معا من جهة مثل أن الذهن قد يصدق من جهة القياس أن ليس خارج العالم خلاء ولا ملاء؛ ثم إنه إذا أعرض عن ذلك القياس ونظر في الأمر نفسه خاب الوهمية فرسمت وجوب أن يكون هناك خلاء أو ملاء فتتبعها النفس فتظن أن هناك خلاء أو ملاء على سبيل عقله. ثم إذا تذكَّرت عن ذلك القياس انفعلت عن طاعة الوهم فيكون هناك من جهة علم وظن معا، ومن جهة ليس: أما الجهة الأولى فلأن هذا العلم قد اكتسب وحصل وليس يحتاج أن يتألف طلبه كالمشكوك فيه بقياس يستفاد والظن طارئ على هذا العلم وهو مكتسب. وفرق بين هذا المكتسب الذي هو معرض عنه وبين المجهول مطلقا. وأما الجهة الثانية فلاستحالة أن يخطرا جميعًا بالبال أن ليس هناك خلاء أو ملاء بالقياس الموجب لذلك ؛ والنفس تظن أن هناك خلاء أو ملاء، وإن كانت القوة الوهمية مصمّمة على ذلك؛ إذ ليس الوهم والظن شيئًا واحدا على ما عرف.
As for what is specific to it, he does not know it, just as a man believes that the celestial bodies do not share nature with fire, then he supposes that the planets are fiery because they are luminous. This opinion of his is actually specific to the planets, but his knowledge of them is universal, existing in him potentially, because he knows in general that every celestial body does not share nature with fire. But that the planets are not fiery is a particular under this universal judgment, so it is not from one aspect knowledge and opinion. And thus one understands subsequently anything of this sort. How could it not be, since it is known that there is no difference between knowing the major premise and not actually placing the minor under the middle, so that the conclusion is not necessarily known actually, and between knowing both the major and minor, without composing them into a productive composition actually: for their existence in the soul however they happen does not necessitate knowledge of the conclusion unless there is a specific composition between them and the soul observes that composition. For example, one who knows that this is a mule and knows that every mule is barren: if he does not combine them in his mind, he may still suppose that this mule is pregnant. The upshot is that actual knowledge of each one individually does not entail anything unless they are present in the mind in the mentioned order and similar to the intended purpose; rather, it entails potentially. So on this basis, one should understand that a single thing cannot be known and unknown at the same time from one aspect, and that the doubt that entered should be removed. It was said to the wise Socrates: Is the object sought through syllogism known or unknown? If it is known, then the search is impossible; and if it is unknown, how will you recognize it when you find it? And can a runaway slave be captured if he is not known by his very self? The wise man did not address the premises and what pertains to them, but rather defined by a geometrical form how the unknown is opposed to the known. As for his student Plato, he did address that, as the Shaykh indicated in the Shifa'. The summary of the discourse in this context is that the sought is known from the aspect from which it is unknown, and unknown from the aspect from which it is known; it is not then completely unknown such that when found it is not known to be it, nor completely known such that it is in no need of search. For example, the runaway slave is known by a specific mark: who he is? But he does not know 'where is he?'. That mark is the middle term in relation to the end. In general, knowledge is either specific or general. Also, knowledge is either potential or actual. For this reason, a person can be knower of the universal while ignorant of the particular—not the ignorance which is absence of knowledge, but the ignorance contrary to knowledge. For example, if we know that this transfer is excessive, then we see it and we suppose it is verified because we know it is good or other than that transfer. On this basis, one may suppose something good, like courage, to be evil; and we have known that evil is not good, whatever it may be. So it is acceptable that a good thing is not good. And from another aspect, a person may have knowledge and opinion of a single thing together from one aspect, but not together from another aspect. For example, the mind may assent from the aspect of syllogism that there is neither void nor plenum outside the world; then if it turns away from that syllogism and looks into the matter itself, the estimative faculty fails and it depicts the necessity of there being void or plenum, so the soul follows it and supposes that there is void or plenum by way of its intellect. Then when it recalls that syllogism, it is affected by obedience to the estimative faculty. So there is from one aspect knowledge and opinion together, and from another aspect not: as for the first aspect, because this knowledge has been acquired and obtained and does not need its pursuit to be composed like that which is doubted through a syllogism that yields benefit—and opinion is incidental to this knowledge and it is acquired. There is a difference between this acquired knowledge that is ignored and the absolutely unknown. As for the second aspect, because it is impossible for both to occur in the mind: that there is no void or plenum through the syllogism necessitating that, while the soul supposes that there is void or plenum, even if the estimative faculty is determined on that; for the estimative faculty and opinion are not one and the same thing, as has been known.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion addressing the famous Socratic paradox of inquiry: how can we seek what we do not know, and how would we recognize it if found? The author begins by illustrating the distinction between universal knowledge and particular opinion. For example, someone may hold the universal belief that celestial bodies do not share the nature of fire, but mistakenly think that planets are fiery due to their brightness. Here, the person's opinion is particular to the planets, while his knowledge is universal and potential. This shows that from one aspect, the same thing can be known and opined differently. The author then emphasizes that knowledge of premises does not automatically yield the conclusion unless they are actually combined in the mind in the correct order. He gives the example of a mule: knowing both that 'this is a mule' and 'every mule is barren' does not prevent one from supposing the mule is pregnant if the premises are not actively composed. So, knowledge remains potential until actual composition. From this, the author asserts that a single thing cannot be known and unknown from the same aspect at the same time, thus resolving the doubt. He presents the Socratic paradox: if the object of inquiry is known, search is unnecessary; if unknown, it cannot be recognized. The author notes that Socrates did not resolve this, but Plato addressed it, as referenced in Avicenna's Shifa. The resolution is that the sought is known from one aspect and unknown from another. For example, a runaway slave is known by his identity (who he is) but unknown by his location (where he is). The identifying mark serves as the middle term in the syllogism. Knowledge is either particular or general, and either potential or actual. Thus, one can know the universal while being ignorant of the particular—not a total absence of knowledge, but a specific ignorance contrary to knowledge. For instance, if we know that a certain thing is excessive, we might mistakenly view it as verified. Similarly, one may suppose something good like courage to be evil, even though we know evil is not good; this is a case of opinion conflicting with knowledge. Finally, the author discusses how a person can have both knowledge and opinion of the same thing from different aspects. The example: from a demonstrative syllogism, the mind affirms that there is no void or plenum outside the world. But when the syllogism is set aside and the matter is considered by the estimative faculty, one may form the opinion that there is void or plenum. When the original syllogism is recalled, the soul is pulled between the two. In this case, from one aspect (the syllogistic proof) there is knowledge, and from another aspect (the estimative judgment) there is opinion. The two are not simultaneous in the same respect because the estimative faculty's opinion is incidental and the soul cannot hold both the proof and the opinion as active at the same moment. The author stresses that the estimative faculty and opinion are distinct, as previously established.

Key Terms
بالقوة (bil-quwwa): Potential knowledge or existence; the premises are known but not yet composed to produce the conclusion. [Note: Standard Aristotelian/Avicennan terminology; stable.]
بالفعل (bil-fi'l): Actual knowledge or existence; the premises are composed and the conclusion is actively known. [Note: Stable across the tradition.]
ظن (zann): Opinion or supposition, often fallible; can coexist with knowledge when from different aspects. [Note: In later usul al-fiqh, zann often denotes probable knowledge; here it is merely opinion without certainty.]
وهم (wahm): Estimative faculty; a cognitive power that judges non-sensible meanings, often leading to false opinions. [Note: In Avicennan psychology, wahm is a distinct internal sense; later logicians may treat it as a source of error.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The conclusion that the mule is barren becomes unknown.
B) The person may still suppose that the mule is pregnant.
C) The person necessarily becomes ignorant of the middle term.
D) The syllogism becomes invalid.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of premises does not guarantee knowledge of the conclusion unless the premises are actually composed in the mind in the correct order.

The Levels of Assent (Tasdiq) in Acquired Knowledge
Author classifies acquired knowledge and links its levels to the five logical arts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three levels of assent (yaqini, shabih al-yaqini, iqna'i zanni) and explain how each relates to certainty.

Core Idea
Assent has three levels: certainty, likeness of certainty, and probability—each with a different epistemic status.

This passage from the demonstration section presents a foundational classification: acquired knowledge is either assent or conception. Assent itself has three levels, from certainty to mere probability. Understanding these levels is crucial for evaluating the strength of arguments in Islamic theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories of acquired knowledge
Find in the Arabic the division of acquired knowledge into tasdiq and tasawwur. Note that assent is obtained through syllogism and conception through definition.
2
Locate the three levels of assent
Scan for ثلاث مراتب or similar. The levels are: yaqini, shabih al-yaqini, and iqna'i zanni.
The passage introduces them with 'فمنه يقيني...' etc.
3
Define each level from the passage
For yaqini: believed with conviction that it cannot be otherwise and cannot be removed. For shabih al-yaqini: believed but can be overturned if alerted. For iqna'i zanni: believed while acknowledging the possibility of its opposite.
Pay attention to the Arabic wording: 'يعتقد مع اعتقاد أنه لا يمكن أن لا يكون' etc.
4
Compare the epistemic strength
Rank the three levels from strongest to weakest. Understand that yaqini is the goal of demonstration, while the others are used in dialectic, rhetoric, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[0] الجملة الرابعة : في البرهان وما يتعلق به من المباحث وإنها مشتملة على الأنواع والفصول أيضًا [١.٥. ] [ النوع الأول : في منفعة البرهان ] ALA [ ١.١.٥. ][ الفصل ] الأول (۱) من النوع الأول فذلك في الدلالة على الغرض من هذا القسم لما كان العلم المكتسب بالفكرة وغير المكتسب بها على قسمين: أحدهما التصديق والآخر التصور، وكان المكتسب بالفكرة من التصديق حاصلا لنا بقياس ما ومن التصور بحد ما . [1] وكما أن التصديق المكتسب منهما على مراتب [1] فمنه يقيني وهو الذي يعتقد مع اعتقاد أنه لا يمكن أن لا يكون على ما هو عليه، ولا يمكن زوال هذا الاعتقاد . [٢] ومنه ماهو شبيه باليقيني وهو الذي يعتقد مع عدم اعتقاد أنه لا يمكن أن لا يكون على ما هو عليه بل هو بحيث لو نبه عليه عسى أن يبطل استحكام ذلك التصديق . [٣] ومنه ما هو إقناعي ظني وهو الذي يعتقد مع اعتقاد أن لنقيضه إمكانا. [ب] وكذلك التصور المكتسب كما أنه على مراتب [۱] فمنه تصور للشيء بالمعاني الذاتية على وجه يخصه أو على وجه يعمه وغيره. [٢] ومنه تصور للشيء بالمعاني العرضية على وجه يخصه أو على وجه يعمه وغيره. [٣] ومنه تصور للشيء بالمعاني المركبة من الذاتية والعرضية. [ت] فكذلك القول المفيد للتصور والتصديق على مراتب، [١] أما للتصديق؛ فمنه ما يوقع اليقين / وهو البرهاني. ومنه ما يوقع شبيه اليقين؛ وهو [٢٧٣ و] إما الجدلي، أو السوفسطائي المغالطي. ومنه ما يوقع ظنا غالبًا وهو الخطابي، ومنه ما يوقع تخييلاً محركًا للنفس إلى انقباض وانبساط وهو الشعري.
The fourth section: On demonstration (burhan) and the investigations related to it, and it includes types and chapters. [1.5.] The first type: On the benefit of demonstration. [1.1.5.] The first chapter of the first type: On indicating the purpose of this section. Since acquired knowledge (by thought) and that not acquired are of two divisions: one is assent (tasdiq) and the other is conception (tasawwur), and what is acquired by thought from assent occurs to us by some syllogism, and from conception by some definition. And just as acquired assent is of levels: [1] among it is certain (yaqini), which is believed with the belief that it cannot be otherwise than it is, and this belief cannot be removed. [2] Among it is what is like certainty (shabih al-yaqini), which is believed without the belief that it cannot be otherwise than it is; rather, it is such that if one is alerted, perhaps the strengthening of that assent may be invalidated. [3] Among it is persuasive/probable (iqna'i zanni), which is believed with the belief that its opposite is possible. Likewise, acquired conception is of levels: [1] among it is conception of a thing by essential attributes, either in a way specific to it or in a way common to it and others. [2] Among it is conception of a thing by accidental attributes, either specific or common. [3] Among it is conception of a thing by attributes composed of essential and accidental. So likewise, the discourse useful for conception and assent is of levels: As for assent, among it is what produces certainty — that is demonstrative (burhani). Among it is what produces likeness of certainty — that is either dialectical (jadali) or sophistical/mugaliti (sufistani). Among it is what produces a predominant opinion (zann ghalib) — that is rhetorical (khatabi). And among it is what produces imagination moving the soul to aversion or attraction — that is poetical (shi'ri).
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage opens the fourth section of the work, which deals with demonstration (burhan). The author first states that the acquired knowledge (al-'ilm al-muktasab bi al-fikra) is divided into two main categories: assent (tasdiq) and conception (tasawwur). Assent is obtained through some syllogism (qiyas ma), and conception through some definition (hadd ma). The author then explains that each of these has levels. For assent: (1) Certain (yaqini) — believed with the conviction that it cannot be otherwise, and this belief cannot be removed. (2) Likeness of certainty (shabih al-yaqini) — believed without the conviction that it cannot be otherwise; it can be overturned by a nudge. (3) Persuasive/probable (iqna'i zanni) — believed while acknowledging the possibility of its opposite. For conception: (1) Conception by essential attributes (ma'ani dhatiyya), either specific (like a full definition) or common (like a generic definition). (2) Conception by accidental attributes ('aradiyya), similarly specific (like a property) or common (like a general accident). (3) Conception by composite attributes (murakkaba) mixing essential and accidental. The author then connects these levels to the five logical arts: discourse (qawl) useful for assent or conception is of levels. For assent: Demonstrative (burhani) produces certainty; dialectical (jadali) and sophistical (sufistani) produce likeness of certainty; rhetorical (khatabi) produces predominant opinion; poetical (shi'ri) produces imagination that moves the soul. This classification establishes the epistemological foundation for the arts of logic: demonstration is the highest, followed by dialectic and sophistry (both dealing with likeness of certainty), then rhetoric (persuasion), and finally poetics (imagination).

Key Terms
تصديق
يقيني
شبيه اليقيني
إقناعي ظني

B) Shabih al-yaqini
C) Iqna'i zanni
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Acquired assent has three levels: certain, like certain, and persuasive—each corresponding to a distinct epistemological stance.

?

?

The Levels of Conception (Tasawwur) and Their Attributes
Author classifies acquired knowledge and links its levels to the five logical arts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of conception based on essential, accidental, and composite attributes.

Core Idea
Conception of a thing can be by essential, accidental, or mixed attributes—each giving a different depth of understanding.

Just as assent has levels, conception also has levels based on the kind of attributes used. This classification helps logicians and jurists understand what kind of definition or description is being provided.

Guided Path
1
Locate the lines discussing conception levels
In the Arabic, find the passage after the treatment of assent: 'وكذلك التصور المكتسب كما أنه على مراتب'.
2
Identify the three categories of conception
They are: conception by essential attributes (dhatiyya), by accidental attributes ('aradiyya), and by composite attributes (murakkaba).
The passage lists them with 'فمنه تصور...'.
3
Distinguish specific vs. common within each category
Note that each category can be either specific (يخصه) or common (يعمه وغيره). This relates to whether the attributes are exclusive or shared.
4
Relate to definition (hadd) and description (rasm)
Essential attributes correspond to hadd (definition), accidental attributes to rasm (description), and composite to a mix.
This connection is implicit in the classification.

Source Text

[0] الجملة الرابعة : في البرهان وما يتعلق به من المباحث وإنها مشتملة على الأنواع والفصول أيضًا [١.٥. ] [ النوع الأول : في منفعة البرهان ] ALA [ ١.١.٥. ][ الفصل ] الأول (۱) من النوع الأول فذلك في الدلالة على الغرض من هذا القسم لما كان العلم المكتسب بالفكرة وغير المكتسب بها على قسمين: أحدهما التصديق والآخر التصور، وكان المكتسب بالفكرة من التصديق حاصلا لنا بقياس ما ومن التصور بحد ما . [1] وكما أن التصديق المكتسب منهما على مراتب [1] فمنه يقيني وهو الذي يعتقد مع اعتقاد أنه لا يمكن أن لا يكون على ما هو عليه، ولا يمكن زوال هذا الاعتقاد . [٢] ومنه ماهو شبيه باليقيني وهو الذي يعتقد مع عدم اعتقاد أنه لا يمكن أن لا يكون على ما هو عليه بل هو بحيث لو نبه عليه عسى أن يبطل استحكام ذلك التصديق . [٣] ومنه ما هو إقناعي ظني وهو الذي يعتقد مع اعتقاد أن لنقيضه إمكانا. [ب] وكذلك التصور المكتسب كما أنه على مراتب [۱] فمنه تصور للشيء بالمعاني الذاتية على وجه يخصه أو على وجه يعمه وغيره. [٢] ومنه تصور للشيء بالمعاني العرضية على وجه يخصه أو على وجه يعمه وغيره. [٣] ومنه تصور للشيء بالمعاني المركبة من الذاتية والعرضية. [ت] فكذلك القول المفيد للتصور والتصديق على مراتب، [١] أما للتصديق؛ فمنه ما يوقع اليقين / وهو البرهاني. ومنه ما يوقع شبيه اليقين؛ وهو [٢٧٣ و] إما الجدلي، أو السوفسطائي المغالطي. ومنه ما يوقع ظنا غالبًا وهو الخطابي، ومنه ما يوقع تخييلاً محركًا للنفس إلى انقباض وانبساط وهو الشعري.
The fourth section: On demonstration (burhan) and the investigations related to it, and it includes types and chapters. [1.5.] The first type: On the benefit of demonstration. [1.1.5.] The first chapter of the first type: On indicating the purpose of this section. Since acquired knowledge (by thought) and that not acquired are of two divisions: one is assent (tasdiq) and the other is conception (tasawwur), and what is acquired by thought from assent occurs to us by some syllogism, and from conception by some definition. And just as acquired assent is of levels: [1] among it is certain (yaqini), which is believed with the belief that it cannot be otherwise than it is, and this belief cannot be removed. [2] Among it is what is like certainty (shabih al-yaqini), which is believed without the belief that it cannot be otherwise than it is; rather, it is such that if one is alerted, perhaps the strengthening of that assent may be invalidated. [3] Among it is persuasive/probable (iqna'i zanni), which is believed with the belief that its opposite is possible. Likewise, acquired conception is of levels: [1] among it is conception of a thing by essential attributes, either in a way specific to it or in a way common to it and others. [2] Among it is conception of a thing by accidental attributes, either specific or common. [3] Among it is conception of a thing by attributes composed of essential and accidental. So likewise, the discourse useful for conception and assent is of levels: As for assent, among it is what produces certainty — that is demonstrative (burhani). Among it is what produces likeness of certainty — that is either dialectical (jadali) or sophistical/mugaliti (sufistani). Among it is what produces a predominant opinion (zann ghalib) — that is rhetorical (khatabi). And among it is what produces imagination moving the soul to aversion or attraction — that is poetical (shi'ri).
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage opens the fourth section of the work, which deals with demonstration (burhan). The author first states that the acquired knowledge (al-'ilm al-muktasab bi al-fikra) is divided into two main categories: assent (tasdiq) and conception (tasawwur). Assent is obtained through some syllogism (qiyas ma), and conception through some definition (hadd ma). The author then explains that each of these has levels. For assent: (1) Certain (yaqini) — believed with the conviction that it cannot be otherwise, and this belief cannot be removed. (2) Likeness of certainty (shabih al-yaqini) — believed without the conviction that it cannot be otherwise; it can be overturned by a nudge. (3) Persuasive/probable (iqna'i zanni) — believed while acknowledging the possibility of its opposite. For conception: (1) Conception by essential attributes (ma'ani dhatiyya), either specific (like a full definition) or common (like a generic definition). (2) Conception by accidental attributes ('aradiyya), similarly specific (like a property) or common (like a general accident). (3) Conception by composite attributes (murakkaba) mixing essential and accidental. The author then connects these levels to the five logical arts: discourse (qawl) useful for assent or conception is of levels. For assent: Demonstrative (burhani) produces certainty; dialectical (jadali) and sophistical (sufistani) produce likeness of certainty; rhetorical (khatabi) produces predominant opinion; poetical (shi'ri) produces imagination that moves the soul. This classification establishes the epistemological foundation for the arts of logic: demonstration is the highest, followed by dialectic and sophistry (both dealing with likeness of certainty), then rhetoric (persuasion), and finally poetics (imagination).

Key Terms
تصور
معان ذاتية
معان عرضية
مركب

A) Conception by essential attributes
B) Conception by accidental attributes
C) Conception by composite attributes
D) Conception by specific attributes

Key Takeaway
Conception can be through essential, accidental, or mixed attributes, each yielding a different kind of conceptual grasp.

?
Take the concept 'riba'. Determine whether each of these descriptions uses essential or accidental attributes: 'increase in a loan' vs 'excess in an exchange of foodstuffs'.
?

The Five Logical Arts and Their Epistemic Outputs
Author classifies acquired knowledge and links its levels to the five logical arts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to map each of the five logical arts (burhan, jadal, sophistry, khataba, shi'r) to the level of assent or conception it produces.

Core Idea
Each logical art produces a distinct mental effect: demonstration yields certainty, dialectic and sophistry produce likeness of certainty, rhetoric gives probability, and poetics creates imagination.

The passage culminates in linking the levels of assent to the five arts. This is the backbone of Islamic logic's classification of arguments by their epistemic value.

Guided Path
1
Find the list of logical arts in the passage
After the levels of conception, the author mentions discourse useful for conception and assent. For assent, he lists four types: burhani, jadali/sufistani, khatabi, shi'ri.
The Arabic: 'فمنه ما يوقع اليقين وهو البرهاني...'
2
Identify what each art produces
Burhani produces yaqin (certainty). Jadali and sufistani produce shabih al-yaqin (likeness of certainty). Khatabi produces zann ghalib (predominant opinion). Shi'ri produces takhyil (imagination).
Note that dialectic and sophistry are at the same level despite one being sound and the other fallacious.
3
Notice the pairing of dialectic and sophistry
They are paired together under the same output. This is because both use accepted premises, but sophistry employs deception.
4
Reflect on the inclusion of poetics
Poetics does not produce belief but imagination. It is included because it affects the soul, though it is not strictly an argumentative art.
This shows the breadth of the logical tradition.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
beginner
