Ranks of Acquired Knowledge: Tasdiq and Tasawwur
Author classifies acquired knowledge into tasdiq and tasawwur with three ranks each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three ranks of tasdiq and the three types of tasawwur based on the author's classification, and identify examples of each from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Acquired knowledge is not monolithic; it has graded ranks of certainty and conceptualization.

In this passage, the author establishes the fundamental epistemological division of acquired knowledge into tasdiq (assent) and tasawwur (conceptualization), and then details the internal ranks within each. Understanding these ranks is crucial for evaluating the strength of arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main divisions
Find the terms tasdiq and tasawwur in the Arabic. The author states that acquired knowledge is of two divisions: assent and conceptualization.
Tasdiq involves judgement; tasawwur is pure conceptualization.
2
Extract the three ranks of tasdiq
Look for the three ranks: yaqini (certain), shabih al-yaqini (like certain), and iqna'i dhanni (persuasive/probable). Note the defining criteria for each.
The difference lies in the belief about the opposite: impossible, not explicitly impossible, or possible.
3
Understand the criteria for tasdiq ranks
Compare the descriptions: yaqini has a firm belief that the opposite cannot be; shabih al-yaqini lacks that explicit belief; dhanni allows the opposite as possible.
This grading is essential for evaluating the certainty of theological and legal proofs.
4
Extract the three types of tasawwur
Identify the three types: by essential meanings (dhatiyya), accidental meanings ('aradiyya), and composite (murakkaba). Each can be specific or common.
Conceptualization is based on the attributes used to define a thing.
5
Relate the means to the ends
Note that tasdiq is acquired via syllogism (qiyas) and tasawwur via definition (hadd). This links epistemology to the methods taught in logic.
This sets the stage for understanding burhan (demonstration).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لما كان العلم المكتسب بالفكرة وغير المكتسب بها على قسمين: أحدهما التصديق والآخر التصور، وكان المكتسب بالفكرة من التصديق حاصلا لنا بقياس ما ومن التصور بحد ما . وكما أن التصديق المكتسب منهما على مراتب: فمنه يقيني وهو الذي يعتقد مع اعتقاد أنه لا يمكن أن لا يكون على ما هو عليه، ولا يمكن زوال هذا الاعتقاد . ومنه ماهو شبيه باليقيني وهو الذي يعتقد مع عدم اعتقاد أنه لا يمكن أن لا يكون على ما هو عليه بل هو بحيث لو نبه عليه عسى أن يبطل استحكام ذلك التصديق . ومنه ما هو إقناعي ظني وهو الذي يعتقد مع اعتقاد أن لنقيضه إمكانا. وكذلك التصور المكتسب كما أنه على مراتب: فمنه تصور للشيء بالمعاني الذاتية على وجه يخصه أو على وجه يعمه وغيره. ومنه تصور للشيء بالمعاني العرضية على وجه يخصه أو على وجه يعمه وغيره. ومنه تصور للشيء بالمعاني المركبة من الذاتية والعرضية.
Since knowledge acquired by thought and not acquired by thought is of two divisions: one of them is assent and the other is conceptualization, and the acquired by thought from assent comes to us by some syllogism, and from conceptualization by some definition. And just as acquired assent from them has ranks: among it is certain, which is that believed along with the belief that it cannot not be according to what it is, and the removal of this belief is not possible. And among it is that which is like the certain, which is believed along with the absence of belief that it cannot not be according to what it is, but rather it is such that if one is alerted to it, perhaps the firmness of that assent might be invalidated. And among it is that which is persuasive and probable, which is believed along with the belief that its opposite has possibility. And likewise acquired conceptualization, just as it has ranks: among it is conceptualization of a thing by essential meanings in a manner specific to it or in a manner common to it and others. And among it is conceptualization of a thing by accidental meanings in a manner specific to it or in a manner common to it and others. And among it is conceptualization of a thing by meanings composed of essential and accidental.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage classifies acquired knowledge (al-'ilm al-muktasab) into two primary divisions: assent (tasdiq) and conceptualization (tasawwur). The author then details the ranks within each. For tasdiq, there are three ranks: (1) certain (yaqini): a firm belief that the opposite is impossible and the belief cannot be removed; (2) like the certain (shabih al-yaqini): a belief that lacks explicit awareness of the impossibility of the opposite, but is vulnerable to being overturned upon reflection; (3) persuasive and probable (iqna'i dhanni): a belief where the opposite is considered possible. For tasawwur, there are also three ranks based on the meanings (ma'ani) used: (1) essential meanings (dhatiyya) either specific or common; (2) accidental meanings ('aradiyya) similarly; (3) composite meanings (murakkaba) of essential and accidental. This structure shows that knowledge through thought (fira) yields assent via syllogism (qiyas) and conceptualization via definition (hadd). The author is setting up the epistemological framework for the later discussion of demonstration (burhan).

Key Terms
تصديق
تصور
يقيني
إقناعي ظني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What distinguishes 'certain' assent (yaqini) from 'like the certain' (shabih al-yaqini) in this passage?
A) Yaqini is based on a syllogism while shabih al-yaqini is based on definition.
B) In yaqini, the impossibility of the opposite is believed, whereas in shabih al-yaqini it is not explicitly believed.
C) Yaqini is always about essential meanings, shabih al-yaqini about accidental.
D) Yaqini is obtained without thought, shabih al-yaqini requires thought.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge attained through thought is structured: assent has three grades of certainty, and conceptualization has three levels based on essentiality of attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following statements according to the rank of tasdiq they would require: 'The Quran is uncreated' (yaqini), 'The majority of scholars say fasting on Ashura is recommended' (dhanni). Explain your reasoning using the criteria from the passage.
?
Identify which type of tasawwur (essential, accidental, or composite) is used in the definition of 'wine' in fiqh as 'every intoxicating beverage' (composite: essential property of intoxication plus accidental class of beverage).

The Six Types of Teaching and Learning
Classification of teaching and learning into six types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify the six types of teaching and learning as presented by the author, and explain how each type builds on prior knowledge.

Core Idea
All teaching and learning rests on prior knowledge, expressed in six types from industrial to mental.

In this passage, the author presents a comprehensive classification of teaching and learning. Understanding these categories helps clarify the different ways knowledge is transmitted, whether through practice, repetition, discipline, imitation, alerting, or intellectual discourse. The last type, mental teaching and learning, is the focus of logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six types
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning to the enumeration of the six types. List each type by its Arabic name: صناعي, تلقيني, تأديبي, تقليدي, تنبيهي, ذهني.
2
Examine the mechanism of each type
For each type, note the mechanism described: industrial by practice, dictation by repetition, disciplinary by commonplaces, imitative by trust in teacher, alerting by recall, mental by spoken/intelligible discourse.
3
Trace the prior knowledge principle
The author states that all teaching and learning is based on prior knowledge. For each type, identify what prior knowledge is presupposed. For example, industrial learning presupposes knowledge of the craft's actions.
4
Contrast mental with other types
The mental type is distinct because it uses discourse to produce belief or conception. Contrast this with industrial (physical practice) or dictation (memorization). Ask: what makes mental learning the province of logic?

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في التعليم والتعلم والتعريف والتعرُّف ] كل تعليم وتعلم فإنه بعلم قد سبق. وإنهما [١] إما صناعي كالصياغة مثلا؛ وإنه إنما يحصل بالمواظبة على استعمال أفعال تلك الصناعة، [ب] وإما تلقيني مثل تلقين الأشعار وغيرها؛ وإنه إنما يحصل بالمواظبة على التلفظ بتلك الأصوات والألفاظ المرتبة لتصير ملكة [ت] وإما تأديبي؛ وإنه إنما يحصل بالمشهورة على متعلّمه، [ث] وإما تقليدي وهو أن يؤلف الإنسان اعتقاد رأي ما؛ وإنما يحصل من جهة اليقين بالمعلم، [ج] وإما تنبيهي كحال من يعلم أن المغناطيس يجذب الحديد لكنه غافل عنه في دقته فيتعجب منه فيقال له هذا هو المغناطيس الذي عرفت حاله فحينئذ يتنبه ويزول عنه التعجب . [ ] وإما ذهني وهو الذي يكتسب بقول مسموع أو معقول من شأنه أن يوقع اعتقادا أو رأيا أو تصورا لم يكن ؛ وهذا التعليم والتعلم الذهني قد يكون بين إنسان وإنسان، وقد يكون بين إنسان واحد مع نفسه لكن من جهتين فإنه من حيث يحدس الحد الأوسط في القياس معلم، ومن حيث إنه يستفيد النتيجة من القياس متعلم: فالتعليم والتعلم بالذات واحد، وبالاعتبار اثنان. ويجب أن يكون ذلك القول معلوما أولا لا كيف اتفق بل من جهة أن يكون علما ما بالمطلوب إن لم يكن بالفعل. فبالقوة إما التصديق فتتقدمه معلومات ثلاثة : [١] تصور المطلوب وإن لم يصدق به بعد، [٢] وتصور القول الذي يتقدم عليه في المرتبة [٣] وتصديق ذلك القول قياسا كان ذلك القول أو استقراء أو تمثلًا أو غير ذلك. وأما التصور فيجب أن يتقدمه تصور أجزاء الحد أو الرسم لا غير. وفي الصناعات العملية أيضًا يجب أن تعلم أولا مادة العمل والآلة. وكذلك في [1] الحسي فإنه قد يتعلم عن علم قد سبق كمن أدرك شيئًا بالحس ثم نسبه فهو يتطلبه ويتعرفه؛ وهذا التطلب بعد ذلك العلم السابق. وأما إطلاق لفظ التعلم والتعليم على الحسي ففيه من الكلام بل يطلق عليه لفظ التعريف والتعرف فإن إدراك الجزئيات لا يكون علمًا بل معرفة : فحاصل الوجود في الذهني حصول العلة مع المعلول. وأما الحسي فليس كذلك؛ إذ الإحساس الأول لا يكون سببًا موصلا إلى الإحساس الثاني، ولا جزءا من السبب. [ب] ثم الذهني هو الفكري بعينه عند البعض وعند الشيخ إن الذهني أصلح من الفكري؛ إذ الذهني أعم منه ومن الحدسي والفهمي. [ت] فإن الفكر يكون بنوع من الطلب فيكون هناك مطلوب، ثم تتحرك النفس إلى طلب الأوسط على الجهة المذكورة في اكتساب القياس [ث] وأما الحدسي فهو أن يكون المطلوب إذا سنح للذهن تمثل الحد الأوسط من غير طلب. [ج] وأما الفهمي فهو أن لا يكون الحد الأوسط حاصلا بطلب بل بالسماع من معلم.
Section: [On Teaching and Learning, Definition and Recognition] Every teaching and learning is based on prior knowledge. They are either [1] industrial, like blacksmithing for example; it is acquired only by persistence in using the actions of that craft; [b] or dictation-like, such as the dictation of poems and others; it is acquired only by persistence in uttering those sounds and ordered words so that they become a habit; [t] or disciplinary; it is acquired only by [persistence] on the commonplaces concerning its subject matter; [th] or imitative, which is that a person composes the belief of a certain opinion; it is acquired only by way of certainty in the teacher; [j] or alerting, like the state of one who knows that a magnet attracts iron but is heedless of its subtlety and so marvels, then it is said to him: this is the magnet whose state you know, then he becomes alert and the amazement leaves him. [ ] or mental, which is acquired through a spoken or intelligible saying that is apt to produce a belief, opinion, or conception that was not; and this mental teaching and learning may be between one person and another, and may be between one person with himself but from two aspects: for insofar as he intuits the middle term in the syllogism, he is a teacher, and insofar as he benefits from the conclusion of the syllogism, he is a learner. So teaching and learning are one in essence, but two in consideration. And it is necessary that that saying be known first, not just anyhow, but from the aspect that there is some knowledge of the sought thing, if not actually, then potentially. For assent: three items of information precede it: [1] conception of the sought thing even if not yet assented to, [2] conception of the saying that precedes it in rank, [3] assent to that saying, whether that saying be a syllogism, induction, analogy, or other. As for conception, the conception of the parts of the definition or description must precede it, nothing else. And in the practical crafts too, the material of the work and the tool must first be known. Likewise in the sensory, one may learn from prior knowledge, like one who perceives something by sense and then relates it, so he seeks it and identifies it; and this seeking is after that prior knowledge. As for applying the term learning and teaching to the sensory, there is discussion about it; rather, the term recognition and identification is applied to it, for the perception of particulars is not knowledge but recognition. So the outcome of existence in the mental is the obtaining of the cause along with the effect. As for the sensory, it is not so; for the first sensation is not a cause leading to the second sensation, nor a part of the cause. [b] Then the mental is the same as the reflective according to some, but according to the master (Ibn Sina) the mental is more appropriate than the reflective, for the mental is more general than it and than the intuitive and the understanding. [t] For reflection involves a kind of search, so there is a sought, then the soul moves to seek the middle term in the manner mentioned in acquiring the syllogism. [th] As for the intuitive, it is that when the sought occurs to the mind, the middle term is represented without search. [j] As for the understanding, it is that the middle term is not obtained by search but by hearing from a teacher.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage is a classification of teaching and learning, likely from a commentary on Ibn Sina's logic. The author begins by stating the foundational principle: all teaching and learning is based on prior knowledge. Then he enumerates six types: industrial (acquired by practice), dictation-like (by repetition of words to form a habit), disciplinary (through commonplaces), imitative (by trust in the teacher's certainty), alerting (by recalling forgotten knowledge), and mental (through spoken or intelligible discourse that produces belief, opinion, or conception). The mental type is the most relevant to logic, and the author emphasizes that it can occur between two persons or within one person, where the self plays both teacher (by intuiting the middle term) and learner (by deriving the conclusion). The author then discusses prerequisites: for assent, three prior items are needed: conception of the sought, conception of the proposition, and assent to that proposition (whether it is a syllogism, induction, analogy, etc.). For conception, only the conception of the parts of the definition or description is needed. The author argues that sensory recognition is not truly learning (which is of universals) but rather identification of particulars. Finally, the author distinguishes the mental from three other cognitive processes: reflective (with search), intuitive (sudden representation), and understanding (received from a teacher). The master (Ibn Sina) prefers the term 'mental' as it covers all three.

Key Terms
تعليم
تعلم
ذهني
حد أوسط
تصديق
تصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of teaching and learning is acquired through persistence in using the actions of a craft?
A) Industrial
B) Dictation-like
C) Disciplinary
D) Mental

Key Takeaway
Teaching and learning are always based on prior knowledge and can be classified into six types, with mental learning being central to logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which type of teaching and learning is at work when a student memorizes the Qur'an with tajweed. Justify your answer using the author's descriptions.
?
Take a ruling from fiqh (e.g., 'Wine is forbidden') and trace how a student learns it through mental teaching: what prior conceptions (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) are needed?

Tasawwur and Tasdiq
intermediate

Prerequisites for Assent and Conception
Explanation of prerequisites for assent and conception in mental learning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the three prerequisites for assent (tasdiq) and the prerequisite for conception (tasawwur), and apply them to a simple example.

Core Idea
Assent requires three prior items; conception requires only the parts of the definition.

The mental type of teaching and learning, especially in logic, aims to produce either conception (tasawwur) or assent (tasdiq). The author explains precisely what must be known beforehand for each. This is crucial for building valid arguments and definitions.

Guided Path
1
Locate the prerequisites section
Find the sentence: 'ويجب أن يكون ذلك القول معلوما أولا...' and the subsequent enumeration of three items for assent and one for conception.
2
Extract the three prerequisites for assent
The author lists: (1) conception of the sought thing, (2) conception of the proposition that precedes, (3) assent to that proposition. Write them down exactly.
3
Extract the prerequisite for conception
For conception, only 'تصور أجزاء الحد أو الرسم' — conception of the parts of the definition or description — is needed.
4
Apply to a concrete Islamic example
Take the proposition 'The world is created (muhdath).' Identify the three prerequisites: (a) conception of 'world' and 'created', (b) conception of the proposition itself, (c) assent to the evidence that establishes it.

Commentary
This passage is a classification of teaching and learning, likely from a commentary on Ibn Sina's logic. The author begins by stating the foundational principle: all teaching and learning is based on prior knowledge. Then he enumerates six types: industrial (acquired by practice), dictation-like (by repetition of words to form a habit), disciplinary (through commonplaces), imitative (by trust in the teacher's certainty), alerting (by recalling forgotten knowledge), and mental (through spoken or intelligible discourse that produces belief, opinion, or conception). The mental type is the most relevant to logic, and the author emphasizes that it can occur between two persons or within one person, where the self plays both teacher (by intuiting the middle term) and learner (by deriving the conclusion). The author then discusses prerequisites: for assent, three prior items are needed: conception of the sought, conception of the proposition, and assent to that proposition (whether it is a syllogism, induction, analogy, etc.). Finally, the author distinguishes the mental from three other cognitive processes: reflective (with search), intuitive (sudden representation), and understanding (received from a teacher). Tasawwur and Tasdiq

Mental, Reflective, Intuitive, and Understanding
Distinction between mental, reflective, intuitive, and understanding processes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between four cognitive processes — mental (dhihnī), reflective (fikrī), intuitive (hadsī), and understanding (fahmī) — and explain why Ibn Sina preferred 'mental' as the overarching category.

Core Idea
Mental teaching and learning is more general than reflective; it includes intuitive and understanding, which differ by how the middle term is obtained.

The author concludes by comparing the term 'mental' with other cognitive processes. Some equate mental with reflective, but the master (Ibn Sina) argues that mental is broader, encompassing both reflective (with search), intuitive (without search), and understanding (received from teacher). This distinction refines our understanding of how knowledge is acquired in logic.

Guided Path
1
Find the cognitive process section
Locate the section beginning 'ثم الذهني هو الفكري بعينه عند البعض...' and read to the end.
2
Identify the three sub-types
The author mentions reflective (فكري), intuitive (حدسي), and understanding (فهمي). For each, note how the middle term (حد أوسط) is obtained: by search (reflective), by sudden representation (intuitive), or by hearing from a teacher (understanding).
3
Understand why mental is broader
Ibn Sina says mental is 'أعم' (more general) than reflective because it includes both reflective and the others. Mental covers any acquisition through discourse, whether with or without search.
4
Apply to a learning scenario
Consider a student learning the proof for the existence of God. Which process is involved? If he works it out himself, it's reflective; if it suddenly comes to him, intuitive; if taught, understanding. All are instances of mental teaching/learning.

فصل : [ في التعليم والتعلم والتعريف والتعرُّف ] كل تعليم وتعلم فإنه بعلم قد سبق. وإنهما [١] إما صناعي كالصياغة مثلا؛ وإنه إنما يحصل بالمواظبة على استعمال أفعال تلك الصناعة، [ب] وإما تلقيني مثل تلقين الأشعار وغيرها؛ وإنه إنما يحصل بالمواظبة على التلفظ بتلك الأصوات والألفاظ المرتبة لتصير ملكة [ت] وإما تأديبي؛ وإنه إنما يحصل بالمشهورة على متعلّمه، [ث] وإما تقليدي وهو أن يؤلف الإنسان اعتقاد رأي ما؛ وإنما يحصل من جهة اليقين بالمعلم، [ج] وإما تنبيهي كحال من يعلم أن المغناطيس يجذب الحديد لكنه غافل عنه في دقته فيتعجب منه فيقال له هذا هو المغناطيس الذي عرفت حاله فحينئذ يتنبه ويزول عنه التعجب . [ ] وإما ذهني وهو الذي يكتسب بقول مسموع أو معقول من شأنه أن يوقع اعتقادا أو رأيا أو تصورا لم يكن ؛ وهذا التعليم والتعلم الذهني قد يكون بين إنسان وإنسان، وقد يكون بين إنسان واحد مع نفسه لكن من جهتين فإنه من حيث يحدس الحد الأوسط في القياس معلم، ومن حيث إنه يستفيد النتيجة من القياس متعلم: فالتعليم والتعلم بالذات واحد، وبالاعتبار اثنان. ويجب أن يكون ذلك القول معلوما أولا لا كيف اتفق بل من جهة أن يكون علما ما بالمطلوب إن لم يكن بالفعل. فبالقوة إما التصديق فتتقدمه معلومات ثلاثة : [١] تصور المطلوب وإن لم يصدق به بعد، [٢] وتصور القول الذي يتقدم عليه في المرتبة [٣] وتصديق ذلك القول قياسا كان ذلك القول أو استقراء أو تمثلًا أو غير ذلك. وأما التصور فيجب أن يتقدمه تصور أجزاء الحد أو الرسم لا غير. وفي الصناعات العملية أيضًا يجب أن تعلم أولا مادة العمل والآلة. وكذلك في [1] الحسي فإنه قد يتعلم عن علم قد سبق كمن أدرك شيئًا بالحس ثم نسبه فهو يتطلبه ويتعرفه؛ وهذا التطلب بعد ذلك العلم السابق. وأما إطلاق لفظ التعلم والتعليم على الحسي ففيه من الكلام بل يطلق عليه لفظ التعريف والتعرف فإن إدراك الجزئيات لا يكون علمًا بل معرفة : فحاصل الوجود في الذهني حصول العلة مع المعلول. وأما الحسي فليس كذلك؛ إذ الإحساس الأول لا يكون سببًا موصلا إلى الإحساس الثاني، ولا جزءا من السبب. [ب] ثم الذهني هو الفكري بعينه عند البعض وعند الشيخ إن الذهني أصلح من الفكري؛ إذ الذهني أعم منه ومن الحدسي والفهمي. [ت] فإن الفكر يكون بنوع من الطلب فيكون هناك مطلوب، ثم تتحرك النفس إلى طلب الأوسط على الجهة المذكورة في اكتساب القياس [ث] وأما الحدسي فهو أن يكون المطلوب إذا سنح للذهن تمثل الحد الأوسط من غير طلب. [ج] وأما الفهمي فهو أن لا يكون الحد الأوسط حاصلا بطلب بل بالسماع من معلم.
Section: [On Teaching and Learning, Definition and Recognition] Every teaching and learning is based on prior knowledge. They are either [1] industrial, like blacksmithing for example; it is acquired only by persistence in using the actions of that craft; [b] or dictation-like, such as the dictation of poems and others; it is acquired only by persistence in uttering those sounds and ordered words so that they become a habit; [t] or disciplinary; it is acquired only by [persistence] on the commonplaces concerning its subject matter; [th] or imitative, which is that a person composes the belief of a certain opinion; it is acquired only by way of certainty in the teacher; [j] or alerting, like the state of one who knows that a magnet attracts iron but is heedless of its subtlety and so marvels, then it is said to him: this is the magnet whose state you know, then he becomes alert and the amazement leaves him. [ ] or mental, which is acquired through a spoken or intelligible saying that is apt to produce a belief, opinion, or conception that was not; and this mental teaching and learning may be between one person and another, and may be between one person with himself but from two aspects: for insofar as he intuits the middle term in the syllogism, he is a teacher, and insofar as he benefits from the conclusion of the syllogism, he is a learner. So teaching and learning are one in essence, but two in consideration. And it is necessary that that saying be known first, not just anyhow, but from the aspect that there is some knowledge of the sought thing, if not actually, then potentially. For assent: three items of information precede it: [1] conception of the sought thing even if not yet assented to, [2] conception of the saying that precedes it in rank, [3] assent to that saying, whether that saying be a syllogism, induction, analogy, or other. As for conception, the conception of the parts of the definition or description must precede it, nothing else. And in the practical crafts too, the material of the work and the tool must first be known. Likewise in the sensory, one may learn from prior knowledge, like one who perceives something by sense and then relates it, so he seeks it and identifies it; and this seeking is after that prior knowledge. As for applying the term learning and teaching to the sensory, there is discussion about it; rather, the term recognition and identification is applied to it, for the perception of particulars is not knowledge but recognition. So the outcome of existence in the mental is the obtaining of the cause along with the effect. As for the sensory, it is not so; for the first sensation is not a cause leading to the second sensation, nor a part of the cause. [b] Then the mental is the same as the reflective according to some, but according to the master (Ibn Sina) the mental is more appropriate than the reflective, for the mental is more general than it and than the intuitive and the understanding. [t] For reflection involves a kind of search, so there is a sought, then the soul moves to seek the middle term in the manner mentioned in acquiring the syllogism. [th] As for the intuitive, it is that when the sought occurs to the mind, the middle term is represented without search. [j] As for the understanding, it is that the middle term is not obtained by search but by hearing from a teacher.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage is a classification of teaching and learning, likely from a commentary on Ibn Sina's logic. The author begins by stating the foundational principle: all teaching and learning is based on prior knowledge. Then he enumerates six types: industrial (acquired by practice), dictation-like (by repetition of words to form a habit), disciplinary (through commonplaces), imitative (by trust in the teacher's certainty), alerting (by recalling forgotten knowledge), and mental (through spoken or intelligible discourse that produces belief, opinion, or conception). The mental type is the most relevant to logic, and the author emphasizes that it can occur between two persons or within one person, where the self plays both teacher (by intuiting the middle term) and learner (by deriving the conclusion). The author then discusses prerequisites: for assent, three prior items are needed: conception of the sought, conception of the proposition, and assent to that proposition (whether it is a syllogism, induction, analogy, etc.). For conception, only the conception of the parts of the definition or description is needed. The author argues that sensory recognition is not truly learning (which is of universals) but rather identification of particulars. Finally, the author distinguishes the mental from three other cognitive processes: reflective (with search), intuitive (sudden representation), and understanding (received from a teacher). The master (Ibn Sina) prefers the term 'mental' as it covers all three.

Key Terms
ذهني
فكري
حدسي
فهمي
حد أوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina (as reported in the passage), why is the term 'mental' more appropriate than 'reflective'?
A) Because mental includes reflective, intuitive, and understanding
B) Because mental is limited to logical reasoning
C) Because reflective is a subset of intuitive
D) Because mental excludes the sensory

Key Takeaway
Mental teaching/learning encompasses reflective (search), intuitive (sudden), and understanding (taught) processes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Classify the following learning scenarios as reflective, intuitive, or understanding: (a) A student deduces the 'illa for riba after studying several examples. (b) A scholar suddenly sees the connection between two hadith without prior search. (c) A beginner listens to a teacher explain qiyas.

The Three Prerequisites for Assent and the One for Conception
Author distinguishes prior knowledge for assent vs. conception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the prerequisites for taṣdīq and taṣawwur and explain the difference.

Core Idea
Assent requires three prior knowledges; conception only requires one.

In the Islamic theory of knowledge, acquiring a new piece of knowledge depends on prior knowledge. This passage from the logic text distinguishes between the prerequisites for taṣdīq (assent) and taṣawwur (conception). Understanding this difference is crucial for constructing sound arguments and definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of cognitive acquisition
Find taṣdīq (assent) and taṣawwur (conception) in the Arabic. What does each aim to achieve?
Assent is a judgment; conception is a mental image.
2
Extract the three prerequisites for assent
The passage lists three things that must precede assent: (1) conception of the proposition (the sought), (2) conception of the statement that supports it, (3) assent to that supporting statement.
The supporting statement can be a syllogism, induction, analogy, etc.
3
Notice the flexibility of the prior statement
The author explicitly says the prior statement could be a syllogism (qiyās), induction (istiqrā'), analogy (tamthīl), or other. This shows that assent can be built on various types of evidence.
4
Extract the prerequisite for conception
For conception, only one prior piece is needed: conception of the parts of the ḥadd (definition) or rasm (description). No assent is required here.
This means to conceive of a concept, you only need to understand its components.
5
Compare the two sets
Assent requires more: both conception (of two things) and assent. Conception only requires conception of parts. This highlights the higher epistemic demand of assent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب أن يكون ذلك القول معلوما أولا لا كيف اتفق بل من جهة أن يكون علما ما بالمطلوب إن لم يكن بالفعل. فبالقوة إما التصديق فتتقدمه معلومات ثلاثة : [١] تصور المطلوب وإن لم يصدق به بعد، [٢] وتصور القول الذي يتقدم عليه في المرتبة [٣] وتصديق ذلك القول قياسا كان ذلك القول أو استقراء أو تمثلًا أو غير ذلك. وأما التصور فيجب أن يتقدمه تصور أجزاء الحد أو الرسم لا غير.
It is necessary that that statement be known first, not arbitrarily, but from the aspect that it is some knowledge of the sought [object], if not actually, then potentially. As for assent, three pieces of information precede it: [1] the conception of the sought [object] even if not yet assented to, [2] the conception of the statement that precedes it in rank, [3] and the assent to that statement, whether that statement is a syllogism, induction, analogy, or other. As for conception, it must be preceded by the conception of the parts of the definition (ḥadd) or description (rasm), nothing else.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage defines the prerequisite knowledge for two types of cognitive acquisition: taṣdīq (assent) and taṣawwur (conception). The author states that for learning, the prior statement must be known not arbitrarily but as at least potential knowledge of the sought object. For assent, three prior items are needed: (1) mental conception of the propositum, (2) conception of the prior statement, and (3) assent to that statement (which could be a syllogism, induction, analogy, etc.). For conception alone, only the conception of the parts of the ḥadd (definition) or rasm (description) is required. This highlights the different epistemological foundations for different types of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Assent requires three prior mental acts, while conception requires only one: grasping the parts of the definition.

Distinguishing Dhihnī, Fikrī, Ḥadsī, and Fahmī Knowledge
Author distinguishes types of knowledge based on how the middle term is obtained.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mental, reflective, intuitive, and understanding-based knowledge, and explain why Ibn Sina considers 'mental' (dhihnī) more fundamental.

Core Idea
Dhihnī (mental) is the umbrella; fikrī, hadsī, and fahmī are its modes.

In this passage, Ibn Sina clarifies a critical distinction: not all intellectual knowledge is acquired by reflection (fikr). Some knowledge comes intuitively, some from a teacher, and all of it is mental. This classification helps us understand how different types of learning relate to the logical concept of the middle term.

Guided Path
1
Locate the four key terms
In the Arabic text, find the words ذهني (dhihnī), فكري (fikrī), حدسي (hadsī), and فهمي (fahmī). Note their positions and the context around them.
The author uses enumerated letters [ب], [ت], [ث], [ج] to introduce them.
2
Associate each term with its definition
Match each term to its description: fikrī involves seeking the middle term; hadsī involves spontaneous appearance of the middle term; fahmī involves hearing the middle term from a teacher.
The middle term is key: it is sought, appears, or is received.
3
Compare the scope of dhihnī and fikrī
Note that some equate dhihnī and fikrī, but Ibn Sina says dhihnī is more appropriate (أصلح) and more general (أعم), encompassing fikrī, hadsī, and fahmī.
This is a crucial correction: dhihnī is not just fikrī.
4
Apply the classification to a learning scenario
Think of a situation where you learn a new fiqh ruling. How would you classify it? If you reflect on proofs, it's fikrī; if the ruling comes to mind suddenly without seeking, it's hadsī; if you hear it from a teacher, it's fahmī. All are dhihnī.
This shows the practical relevance of the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الصناعات العملية أيضًا يجب أن تعلم أولا مادة العمل والآلة. وكذلك في الحسي فإنه قد يتعلم عن علم قد سبق كمن أدرك شيئًا بالحس ثم نسبه فهو يتطلبه ويتعرفه؛ وهذا التطلب بعد ذلك العلم السابق. وأما إطلاق لفظ التعلم والتعليم على الحسي ففيه من الكلام بل يطلق عليه لفظ التعريف والتعرف فإن إدراك الجزئيات لا يكون علمًا بل معرفة : فحاصل الوجود في الذهني حصول العلة مع المعلول. وأما الحسي فليس كذلك؛ إذ الإحساس الأول لا يكون سببًا موصلا إلى الإحساس الثاني، ولا جزءا من السبب. [ب] ثم الذهني هو الفكري بعينه عند البعض وعند الشيخ إن الذهني أصلح من الفكري؛ إذ الذهني أعم منه ومن الحدسي والفهمي. [ت] فإن الفكر يكون بنوع من الطلب فيكون هناك مطلوب، ثم تتحرك النفس إلى طلب الأوسط على الجهة المذكورة في اكتساب القياس [ث] وأما الحدسي فهو أن يكون المطلوب إذا سُنح للذهن تمثل الحد الأوسط من غير طلب. [ج] وأما الفهمي فهو أن لا يكون الحد الأوسط حاصلا بطلب بل بالسماع من معلم. وبالجملة يجب أن يفرد التعليم الذي نحن في صدده تعليمًا واحدًا وقياسًا واحدا فإن الخلط لا يمتنع أن يتركب من أصناف شتى. والفكرة كالحركة للنفس من شيء إلى شيء؛ فإذا لم توجد في التعليم والتعلم هذه الحركة على وجهها لم تكن هناك فكرة. وإذا كان كل تعليم وتعلم للأمور العقلية إما على سبيل الفكر أو الحس أو تعلم الفهم؛ وليس ذلك في التصديق فقط بل وفي التصور : وكل ذلك ذهني؛ فقولهم تعليم وتعلم ذهني أصوب.
Also in the practical arts, one must first know the material of the work and the tool. Similarly in sensory [perception], for one may learn from prior knowledge, like one who perceives something by sense and then relates it, so he seeks it and recognizes it; this seeking is after that prior knowledge. As for applying the term 'learning' (ta'allum) and 'teaching' (ta'līm) to the sensory, there is discussion about it, rather the terms 'definition' (ta'rīf) and 'recognition' (ta'āruf) are applied to it, for the perception of particulars is not knowledge ('ilm) but cognition (ma'rifa). So the outcome in the mental is the attainment of the cause with the effect. But the sensory is not like that; the first sensation does not cause a second sensation, nor is it part of the cause. [B] Then the mental (dhihnī) is precisely the reflective (fikrī) according to some, while according to the Shaykh [Ibn Sina] the mental is more appropriate than the reflective, for the mental is more general than it and than the intuitive (hadsī) and the understanding (fahmī). [T] For thought (fikr) is through a type of seeking, so there is a sought object, then the soul moves to seek the middle term in the manner mentioned in acquiring the syllogism. [Th] As for the intuitive (hadsī), it is that the sought object, when it occurs to the mind, the representation of the middle term appears without seeking. [J] As for the understanding (fahmī), it is that the middle term is obtained not by seeking but by hearing from a teacher. In summary, the teaching/learning we are concerned with must be a single teaching and a single syllogism, for mixing [types] may produce a composite from various kinds. And thought is like a movement of the soul from something to something; if this movement is not present in teaching and learning in its proper manner, there is no thought. And since every teaching and learning of intellectual matters is either by way of thought, sense, or learning understanding; and that is not only in assent but also in conception: and all that is mental; so their saying 'teaching and learning' is more correct as 'mental'.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage expands on the types of knowledge acquisition. First, the author discusses sensory learning, noting that sensory perception of particulars is not knowledge (ilm) but cognition (ma'rifa). Then, a distinction is made between the mental (dhihnī) and the reflective (fikrī). Some equate them, but the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) holds that dhihnī is broader, encompassing fikrī, hadsī (intuitive), and fahmī (from understanding). The author then defines these three: fikr involves seeking the middle term; hads is immediate grasp of the middle term without seeking; fahm is reception from a teacher. The passage concludes that true intellectual teaching/learning must involve a single movement of thought, whether in assent or conception, and all such are mental, hence 'mental teaching/learning' is the correct term.

Key Terms
ذهني
فكري
حدسي
فهمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, which type of knowledge is most inclusive?
A) Dhihnī (mental)
B) Fikrī (reflective)
C) Ḥadsī (intuitive)
D) Fahmī (understanding)

Key Takeaway
All intellectual knowledge is dhihnī (mental), but it can be acquired reflectively, intuitively, or by understanding; recognizing this avoids conflating different modes of learning.

Teaching as a Unified Mental Movement
Author discusses teaching as a unified act and thought as mental movement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the author insists that teaching must be treated as a single syllogism and identify the role of mental movement in learning.

Core Idea
Teaching is one mental movement, not a collection of scattered facts.

The author opens by asserting that the act of teaching we are studying must be considered a single unified teaching and a single syllogism, even though it may involve various components. He then compares thought to a movement of the soul from one thing to another, warning that without this movement, there is no true thought. This lesson focuses on the unity of the teaching process and the centrality of mental motion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Central Claim
Find the sentence where the author asserts that teaching should be made a single teaching and a single syllogism. Notice the phrase 'وبالجملة يجب أن يفرد التعليم... تعليمًا واحدًا وقياسًا واحدا' – this is the thesis of the passage.
2
Understand the Metaphor of Thought as Movement
The author says 'الفكرة كالحركة للنفس من شيء إلى شيء' – thought is like a movement of the soul from one thing to another. Reflect on how this metaphor implies a directed, purposeful process rather than random associations.
3
Distinguish the Three Modes of Intellectual Learning
The author lists three ways intellectual matters are taught and learned: by thought (فكر), by sense (حس), or by learning understanding (تعلم الفهم). Note that these apply to both assent (تصديق) and conception (تصور), and all are mental.
The phrase 'وكل ذلك ذهني' emphasizes that all these processes are mental.
4
Recognize the Correct Terminology
The author concludes that the term 'mental teaching and learning' (تعليم وتعلم ذهني) is more accurate than other descriptions. Why does he say this? Because all intellectual learning involves mental movement, whether through thought, sense, or understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة يجب أن يفرد التعليم الذي نحن في صدده تعليمًا واحدًا وقياسًا واحدا فإن الخلط لا يمتنع أن يتركب من أصناف شتى. والفكرة كالحركة للنفس من شيء إلى شيء؛ فإذا لم توجد في التعليم والتعلم هذه الحركة على وجهها لم تكن هناك فكرة. وإذا كان كل تعليم وتعلم للأمور العقلية إما على سبيل الفكر أو الحس أو تعلم الفهم؛ وليس ذلك في التصديق فقط بل وفي التصور : وكل ذلك ذهني؛ فقولهم تعليم وتعلم ذهني أصوب. والشيء إذا وقع التصديق به لكان تصديقا بالقوة لشيء آخر فهو إما ملزومه وإما معانده أو كلي فوقه أو جزئي تحته أو معه. والملزوم إذا علم بالفعل كان ذلك العلم علمًا بالقوة إلى لازمه. وعلى هذا إذا علم المعاند بالفعل كان ذلك العلم علما بمعاندة إما بالوضع وإما برفعه والكلي إذا علم وجود حكم عليه كان ذلك علما بالقوة بالجزئي الذي تحته بطريق القياس والجزئي إذا عُلم وجود حكم عليه كان ذلك كليًّا بالقوة بالكلي الذي فوقه على ما عرف في القياس والاستقراء والتمثيل.
And in summary, it is necessary that the teaching we are engaged with be made a single teaching and a single syllogism, for mixing does not prevent being composed of various types. And thought is like movement of the soul from one thing to another; so if this movement does not exist in teaching and learning in its proper manner, there is no thought. And if every teaching and learning of intellectual matters is either by way of thought, or sense, or learning understanding; and that is not only in assent but also in conception: and all of that is mental; so their saying 'mental teaching and learning' is more correct. And when something is assented to, it is a potential assent to another thing: it is either its necessary concomitant, or its opposite, or a universal above it, or a particular under it, or with it. And if the necessary concomitant is actually known, that knowledge is potential knowledge of its necessary consequent. And likewise, if the opposite is actually known, that knowledge is knowledge of opposition either by position or by denial. And if a universal is known to have a judgement upon it, that is potential knowledge of the particular under it by way of syllogism. And if a particular is known to have a judgement upon it, that is potential knowledge of the universal above it, as is known in syllogism, induction, and analogy.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the nature of teaching, learning, and thought. The author begins by asserting that the subject of teaching must be considered as a single unified act and a single syllogism, even though it may be composed of various types. He then likens thought to a movement of the soul from one thing to another; without this movement, there is no thought. Next, he categorizes all intellectual teaching and learning as occurring either through thought (fikr), sense (hiss), or learning understanding (ta'allum al-fahm). This applies not only to assent (tasdiq) but also to conception (tasawwur), and all are mental (dhihni). Following this, he explains the relationships between an object of assent and another thing: it can be a necessary concomitant (luzum), an opposite (mu'anada), a universal above it, a particular under it, or with it. He then elaborates: if the necessary concomitant is actually known, that actual knowledge gives potential knowledge of its necessary consequent (lazim). Similarly, knowing the opposite gives potential knowledge of the opposite. Knowing a universal with a judgement gives potential knowledge of the particular under it via syllogism (qiyas). Knowing a particular with a judgement gives potential knowledge of the universal above it via syllogism, induction (istiqra'), or analogy (tamthil). All examples are from the logical tradition: for instance, knowing that 'every human is animal' (universal) gives potential knowledge of 'Zayd is animal' (particular) through a syllogism.

Relations Between Known and Unknown in Acquiring Knowledge
Author discusses five relations that allow actual knowledge to yield potential knowledge of other things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five relationships (necessary concomitant, opposite, universal, particular, and concomitant) by which actual knowledge yields potential knowledge of other things, and apply them to examples from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Actual knowledge of one thing gives potential knowledge of its related things through five key relationships.

After establishing that learning is a mental movement, the author explains how actual knowledge of one thing can lead to potential knowledge of another. He identifies five types of relationships that link an object of assent to another thing: its necessary concomitant, its opposite, a universal above it, a particular under it, or a concomitant with it. This lesson explores these relations and how they enable inference through syllogism, induction, and analogy.

Guided Path
1
List the Five Relationships
From the passage, extract the five ways an object of assent relates to another thing: ملزوم (necessary concomitant), معاند (opposite), كلي فوقه (universal above), جزئي تحته (particular under), معه (concomitant). Note that these cover logical and epistemological connections.
2
Understand Potential Knowledge from Actual Knowledge
The author repeatedly uses the phrase 'علمًا بالقوة' (potential knowledge). For each relationship, actual knowledge of one term yields potential knowledge of the related term. For example, knowing the malzum gives potential knowledge of its lazim.
3
Analyze the Case of Malzum and Lazim
Examine the sentence: 'والملزوم إذا علم بالفعل كان ذلك العلم علمًا بالقوة إلى لازمه.' If you actually know the necessary concomitant (malzum), you have potential knowledge of its necessary consequent (lazim). This is a logical inference.
Example: Knowing that something is a cause (malzum) gives potential knowledge of its effect (lazim).
4
Extend to Universal and Particular
Read: 'والكلي إذا علم وجود حكم عليه كان ذلك علما بالقوة بالجزئي الذي تحته بطريق القياس.' Knowing a universal with a judgement gives potential knowledge of the particular under it via syllogism. This is the classic syllogistic inference from general to specific.
Example: Knowing 'every human is mortal' (universal) gives potential knowledge that 'Zayd is mortal' (particular) by syllogism.
5
Apply to Induction and Analogy
The author also mentions induction (استقراء) and analogy (تمثيل) as methods for moving from particulars to universals or from one case to another. Identify the direction: induction goes from particular instances to a universal, while analogy moves from one particular to another based on a shared property.
Example: From observing many Muslims that prayer brings humility, one may infer by induction that all prayers have that effect.

The Priority of the 'What' Question in Investigation
Author explains the priority of the 'what' question

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to order the three investigations (what, whether, why) and explain why the 'what' question is prior to the others using Islamic philosophical examples.

Core Idea
You cannot ask whether something exists or why it is, until you first know what it is.

The author argues that the investigation of 'what' (mā) is prior to both 'whether' (hal) and 'why' (lima). This is not merely a procedural preference but a logical necessity. Before inquiring about existence or causation, one must grasp the meaning of the term. Understanding this priority is essential for structuring any scientific inquiry in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three investigations
Recognize that the three māṭlab are 'what' (mā), 'whether' (hal), and 'why' (lima), corresponding to definition, existence, and causation respectively.
2
Understand the priority of 'what' regarding the name
The author states that before asking 'whether' something exists, you must know that the name is assigned to something. If you don't know what the name indicates, you cannot ask about its existence.
3
Understand the priority of 'what' regarding essence
The investigation of 'what' according to the thing itself (essence) also precedes the simple 'whether' question. Example: to ask about the whatness of motion, you must already have some understanding of motion's existence.
4
See how the 'why' investigation is always preceded
After conceiving something and knowing it exists, you can seek its cause. The cause can be either in statement (syllogistic proof) or in reality. The author distinguishes between 'why' according to statement and according to the thing itself.
The passage mentions that sometimes the middle term in a syllogism is also the real cause, so the two inquiries coincide.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمطلب «ما» الذي بحسب الاسم متقدم على كل مطلب، أما على مطلب هل بقسمته فلان الذي لا يعلم أن الاسم لا من شيء وضع لا يتأتى منه السؤال عن أنه هل موجود أو هل هو موجود بحال ما أو ليس وأما على مطلب لم بقسمته فلان الذي لا يفهم معنى اللفظ لأمكنه علة نسبته إلى غيره أو علة وجوده في الخارج أو علة اتصافه بصفة كل ذلك فرع العلم بمفهوم اللفظ . وأما مطلب «ما» الذي بحسب تحقق الأمر في نفسه فمتقدم عن مطلب هل البسيط فإن الذي يطلب ما ذات الحركة وما ذات الزمان؛ فإنما يطلب مائية آخر موجود عنده. وأما أن طلبه هل حركة أو هل زمان أو هل خلاء فيجب أن يفهم أولا ما تدل عليه هذه الأسامي: فإنه يمكن أن يعلم ما يدل عليه الاسم ولا يعلم هل ذلك المدلول عليه موجودا أو غير موجود. فإن كان الحد إنما هو بالحقيقة للموجود ولكن لا يوقف في أول الأمر أن هذا القول حد بحسب الاسم أو بحسب الذات إلا بعد أن يعرف أن الذوات موجودة. وكذلك توضع في التعاليم حدود أشياء تبرهن على وجودها من بعد كالمثلث والمربع وغير ذلك فكان حدا بحسب شرح الاسم، ثم بين وجودها بعد فصار الحد بحسب الذات بل صار حدا بالحقيقة. ويجب أن تعلم أن الفرق بين الذي يفهم من الاسم بالجملة وبين الذي يفهم من الحد بالتفصيل غير قليل فكل إنسان إذا خوطب باسم فهم فهما ما ووقف على ما يدل عليه الاسم إذا كان عالما باللغة. وأما الحد فلا يقف عليه إلا المرتاض بصناعة المنطق فيكون أحد الأمرين معرفة والثاني علما. ومبادئ العلوم تختلف في تقديمها على العلوم وتصدير التعاليم بها؛ ففي البعض إنما يوضع أن الأمر موجود أو غير موجود فقط، وفي البعض على خلاف هذا كما مرّ من قبل في المثلث والمربع مثلا. ثم الأمور التي تذكر في المبادئ قد تكون مفردة، وقد تكون مركبة، والمركبة إنما تليق أن يُستدعى فيها التصديق لا لأن تعطى لها الحدود فإن التركيب الخبري إنما هو للتصديق. وأما الحدود فإنها للمعاني المفردة وما في حكمها والأصول الموضوعة مركبة فإذن لا يتحقق فيها معنى إعطاء الحد والماهية؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون هذا القسم من المبادئ توضع بالهلية. وأما المعاني المفردة فمنها ما هي أعراض، ومنها ما هو غير أعراض بل هي داخلة في جملة موضوع الصناعة والنظر في موضوع الصناعة لا يكون لأن موضوعها فقط بل لأن تلك الأعراض حاصلة له أم لا. وإنها لا يلزم أن تكون بينة فيجب تعريفًا، فيجب إذن أن توضع حدودها في المبادئ دون وجودها بخلاف الأمور الداخلة في الموضوع فإنه يلزم أن يعرف وجودها فإذن في البعض توجد حدودها في المبادئ دون وجودها، وفي البعض توضع حدودها ووجودها، فيتبين من جميع ذلك أن من الأمور المصدرة في الصناعة ما يوضع بهلية فقط، ومنه ما يوضع بماهية، ومنه ما يوضع بهلية وماهيته. وأما مطلب «لمَ» فإنه على كل حال متقدم عن المطلبين معا : فإن طلب عليه الشيء بعد تصور ذلك الشيء فإما طلب اللّمَ الذي بحسب القول ربَّما كان متقدما على طلب اللمَ الذي بحسب الأمر في نفسه؛ فربَّما صح عندنا بقياس إن ج ب ولا تُدرى العلة في نفس الوجود لكون «ج ب»؛ وربَّما لم يكن متقدما عليه . وذلك حيث كان الشيء بينا بنفسه أو بالحس لكن تكون عليه خفية كجذب المغناطيس فإنه إذا أصيبت بالحس خطر بالبال طلب عليه في نفس الأمر لا طلب التصديق به وقد يتفق أن يكون الحد الأوسط في القياس علة للأمر نفسه فيجتمع المطلبان معا في بيان واحد.
The investigation of 'what' according to the name precedes every investigation. As for the investigation of 'whether' in its division, because he who does not know that the name is posited for something cannot proceed to ask whether it exists or does not exist in any state. And as for the investigation of 'why' in its division, because he who does not understand the meaning of the word cannot know the cause of its attribution to something else, or the cause of its existence in the external, or the cause of its being qualified by a quality—all of that is dependent on knowing the concept of the word. And the investigation of 'what' according to the realization of the thing itself precedes the simple investigation of 'whether': for he who seeks the whatness of motion and the whatness of time seeks whatness of something else that exists for him. And as for his asking whether motion exists, or whether time exists, or whether a vacuum exists, he must first understand what these names indicate: for it is possible to know what the name indicates without knowing whether that indicated thing exists or does not exist. For if the definition is in truth for the existent, one does not stop at the outset to say whether this statement is a definition according to the name or according to the essence until after knowing that the essences exist. Similarly, in the sciences, definitions of things are placed whose existence is later demonstrated, like the triangle and square and others, so it is a definition according to the explanation of the name, then their existence is later shown, so the definition becomes according to the essence—indeed, it becomes a definition in truth. And you must know that the difference between what is understood from the name in summary and what is understood from the definition in detail is not small: every person, when addressed with a name, understands something and grasps what the name indicates if he is knowledgeable of the language. But the definition is not grasped except by one trained in the art of logic, so one of the two is recognition (ma'rifa) and the other is knowledge ('ilm). And the principles of the sciences differ in their priority to the sciences and their placement at the outset of the disciplines: in some, it is only placed that the thing exists or does not exist; in others, otherwise, as mentioned before concerning the triangle and square for example. Then the things mentioned in the principles may be simple or composite. Composite things are fit to demand assent (tasdiq) in them, not to be given definitions; for predicative composition is for assent. And definitions are for simple meanings and what is in their status. The posited principles are composite, so the meaning of giving definition and quiddity is not realized in them; and then it is necessary that this kind of principle is placed with the 'whether-ness' (haliyya). As for simple meanings, some are accidents, some are not accidents but rather are included in the subject matter of the art. And the consideration in the subject matter of the art is not only because of its subject, but also because of whether those accidents belong to it or not. And they are not necessarily self-evident, so they must be defined. Therefore, their definitions must be placed in the principles without their existence, unlike the things included in the subject, whose existence must be known. So in some, their definitions are placed in the principles without their existence; in some, both their definitions and existence are placed. Thus it becomes clear from all that that among the things placed at the outset of the art, some are placed with 'whether-ness' only, some with 'whatness', and some with 'whether-ness' and 'whatness'. And as for the investigation of 'why', it is in any case preceded by the other two investigations together: for the inquiry into something after conceiving it is either the inquiry into the 'why' according to statement, which may precede the inquiry into the 'why' according to the thing itself. For it may be established for us by syllogism that 'J is B', but the cause in the existence itself is not known because of 'J is B'. And sometimes it is not preceded by it, when the thing is evident by itself or by sense, but the cause is hidden, like the attraction of a magnet: when it is perceived by sense, the inquiry into its cause occurs to the mind, not the inquiry into affirming it. And it may happen that the middle term in the syllogism is the cause of the thing itself, so the two inquiries come together in a single explanation.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the three fundamental types of investigation (مطالب) and their order of priority: the 'what' question (ما), the 'whether' question (هل), and the 'why' question (لم). The author, likely Ibn Sina or a commentator, begins by asserting that the investigation of 'what' according to the name (بحسب الاسم) is prior to every other investigation. This is because before one can ask whether something exists (هل موجود) or why it has a certain attribute, one must first know what the word means. If someone does not know that a name is assigned to something, they cannot proceed to ask about its existence. Similarly, to seek the cause (why), one must understand the meaning of the word. Thus, the conceptual understanding from the name is foundational.

Next, the author distinguishes between the 'what' according to the name and the 'what' according to the realization of the thing itself (بحسب تحقق الأمر في نفسه). The latter precedes the simple 'whether' question because, for example, to ask about the whatness of motion or time presupposes that these things are already known to exist in some manner. One must first understand what the terms 'motion' and 'time' indicate before asking if they exist or if a vacuum exists.

The author then discusses definitions (حدود). A definition can be either by name (شرح الاسم) or by essence (بحسب الذات). Initially, one might not know if a given definition is by name or by essence until the existence of the defined essence is established. In teaching sciences, definitions are often given for things whose existence is later demonstrated, like triangle or square. Then the definition becomes a real definition after existence is proven.

There is an important epistemological distinction: understanding from a name is general and accessible to anyone competent in the language, whereas understanding from a definition is detailed and requires training in logic. The former is recognition (معرفة), the latter is knowledge (علم).

The author then moves to the principles of sciences (مبادئ العلوم). These principles differ: some only state existence, others more. The things in principles may be simple or composite. Composite statements (predicative combinations) demand assent (تصديق), not definitions, because definitions are for simple meanings. Since the principles themselves are composite, they cannot be given definitions; they are placed with 'whether-ness' (بالهلية), meaning they assert existence.

Simple meanings in principles may be accidents or part of the subject matter. Their definitions are placed in the principles without their existence, unless they are part of the subject, in which case existence must be known. So some principles are given with 'whether-ness' only, some with 'whatness', some with both.

Finally, the 'why' investigation (طلب لم) is always preceded by the other two together. After conceiving something, one may ask for the 'why' according to statement (e.g., a syllogistic proof) which may precede the search for the real cause. Sometimes the thing is evident but its cause is hidden, like magnetic attraction—the existence is known by sense, but the cause is sought. Sometimes the middle term of a syllogism is both the logical proof and the real cause, so the two inquiries (proof and cause) coincide in a single demonstration.

Key Terms
مطلب ما
مطلب هل
مطلب لم
ماهية
هلية

Key Takeaway
The 'what' question (definition) is epistemologically prior to both the 'whether' (existence) and 'why' (causation) questions.

Definitions by Name vs. Essence and Principles of Sciences
Author discusses definitions by name vs. essence and principles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between definition by name (sharḥ al-ism) and definition by essence (ḥadd bi-ḥasab al-dhāt), and identify how principles in sciences are placed with either haliyya, mahiyya, or both.

Core Idea
A definition can be initially by name, becoming a real definition only after the existence of the defined essence is established.

The author distinguishes between definitions that merely explain the meaning of a name and those that capture the essence of a thing. In teaching sciences, definitions of things like triangle are initially given by name, then later proven to exist. This distinction is crucial for understanding how scientific principles are set forth—some with only existence (haliyya), some with only definition (mahiyya), and some with both.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two senses of definition
A definition (ḥadd) can be either by name (sharḥ al-ism) or by essence (bi-ḥasab al-dhāt). A real definition is of an existent entity.
2
Recognize the epistemological difference
Knowledge from the name is general and accessible to anyone competent in language, while knowledge from the definition is detailed and requires training in logic. The former is maʿrifa (recognition), the latter is ʿilm (knowledge).
The author says: 'one of the two is recognition, the other is knowledge.'
3
Examine definitions in the sciences
In geometry, definitions of triangle and square are given first (by name), then their existence is demonstrated (e.g., through construction). After demonstration, the definition becomes by essence.
The author uses triangle and square as examples.
4
Analyze the principles of sciences
Principles (mabādiʾ) can be simple or composite. Composite principles demand assent, not definitions. Simple meanings (accidents or subject parts) are defined in principles without existence if not self-evident; if part of subject, existence is given.
Some principles are placed with haliyya only, some with mahiyya, some with both.

عليه خفية كجذب المغناطيس فإنه إذا أصيبت بالحس خطر بالبال طلب عليه في نفس الأمر لا طلب التصديق به وقد يتفق أن يكون الحد الأوسط في القياس علة للأمر نفسه فيجتمع المطلبان معا في بيان واحد.
The investigation of 'what' according to the name precedes every investigation. As for the investigation of 'whether' in its division, because he who does not know that the name is posited for something cannot proceed to ask whether it exists or does not exist in any state. And as for the investigation of 'why' in its division, because he who does not understand the meaning of the word cannot know the cause of its attribution to something else, or the cause of its existence in the external, or the cause of its being qualified by a quality—all of that is dependent on knowing the concept of the word. And the investigation of 'what' according to the realization of the thing itself precedes the simple investigation of 'whether': for he who seeks the whatness of motion and the whatness of time seeks whatness of something else that exists for him. And as for his asking whether motion exists, or whether time exists, or whether a vacuum exists, he must first understand what these names indicate: for it is possible to know what the name indicates without knowing whether that indicated thing exists or does not exist. For if the definition is in truth for the existent, one does not stop at the outset to say whether this statement is a definition according to the name or according to the essence until after knowing that the essences exist. Similarly, in the sciences, definitions of things are placed whose existence is later demonstrated, like the triangle and square and others, so it is a definition according to the explanation of the name, then their existence is later shown, so the definition becomes according to the essence—indeed, it becomes a definition in truth. And you must know that the difference between what is understood from the name in summary and what is understood from the definition in detail is not small: every person, when addressed with a name, understands something and grasps what the name indicates if he is knowledgeable of the language. But the definition is not grasped except by one trained in the art of logic, so one of the two is recognition (ma'rifa) and the other is knowledge ('ilm). And the principles of the sciences differ in their priority to the sciences and their placement at the outset of the disciplines: in some, it is only placed that the thing exists or does not exist; in others, otherwise, as mentioned before concerning the triangle and square for example. Then the things mentioned in the principles may be simple or composite. Composite things are fit to demand assent (tasdiq) in them, not to be given definitions; for predicative composition is for assent. And definitions are for simple meanings and what is in their status. The posited principles are composite, so the meaning of giving definition and quiddity is not realized in them; and then it is necessary that this kind of principle is placed with the 'whether-ness' (haliyya). As for simple meanings, some are accidents, some are not accidents but rather are included in the subject matter of the art. And the consideration in the subject matter of the art is not only because of its subject, but also because of whether those accidents belong to it or not. And they are not necessarily self-evident, so they must be defined. Therefore, their definitions must be placed in the principles without their existence, unlike the things included in the subject, whose existence must be known. So in some, their definitions are placed in the principles without their existence; in some, both their definitions and existence are placed. Thus it becomes clear from all that that among the things placed at the outset of the art, some are placed with 'whether-ness' only, some with 'whatness', and some with 'whether-ness' and 'whatness'. And as for the investigation of 'why', it is in any case preceded by the other two investigations together: for the inquiry into something after conceiving it is either the inquiry into the 'why' according to statement, which may precede the inquiry into the 'why' according to the thing itself. For it may be established for us by syllogism that 'J is B', but the cause in the existence itself is not known because of 'J is B'. And sometimes it is not preceded by it, when the thing is evident by itself or by sense, but the cause is hidden, like the attraction of a magnet: when it is perceived by sense, the inquiry into its cause occurs to the mind, not the inquiry into affirming it. And it may happen that the middle term in the syllogism is the cause of the thing itself, so the two inquiries come together in a single explanation.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the three fundamental types of investigation (مطالب) and their order of priority: the 'what' question (ما), the 'whether' question (هل), and the 'why' question (لم). The author, likely Ibn Sina or a commentator, begins by asserting that the investigation of 'what' according to the name (بحسب الاسم) is prior to every other investigation. This is because before one can ask whether something exists (هل موجود) or why it has a certain attribute, one must first know what the word means. If someone does not know that a name is assigned to something, they cannot proceed to ask about its existence. Similarly, to seek the cause (why), one must understand the meaning of the word. Thus, the conceptual understanding from the name is foundational.

Next, the author distinguishes between the 'what' according to the name and the 'what' according to the realization of the thing itself (بحسب تحقق الأمر في نفسه). The latter precedes the simple 'whether' question because, for example, to ask about the whatness of motion or time presupposes that these things are already known to exist in some manner. One must first understand what the terms 'motion' and 'time' indicate before asking if they exist or if a vacuum exists.

The author then discusses definitions (حدود). A definition can be either by name (شرح الاسم) or by essence (بحسب الذات). Initially, one might not know if a given definition is by name or by essence until the existence of the defined essence is established. In teaching sciences, definitions are often given for things whose existence is later demonstrated, like triangle or square. Then the definition becomes a real definition after existence is proven.

There is an important epistemological distinction: understanding from a name is general and accessible to anyone competent in the language, whereas understanding from a definition is detailed and requires training in logic. The former is recognition (معرفة), the latter is knowledge (علم).

The author then moves to the principles of sciences (مبادئ العلوم). These principles differ: some only state existence, others more. The things in principles may be simple or composite. Composite statements (predicative combinations) demand assent (تصديق), not definitions, because definitions are for simple meanings. Since the principles themselves are composite, they cannot be given definitions; they are placed with 'whether-ness' (بالهلية), meaning they assert existence.

Simple meanings in principles may be accidents or part of the subject matter. Their definitions are placed in the principles without their existence, unless they are part of the subject, in which case existence must be known. So some principles are given with 'whether-ness' only, some with 'whatness', some with both.

Finally, the 'why' investigation (طلب لم) is always preceded by the other two together. After conceiving something, one may ask for the 'why' according to statement (e.g., a syllogistic proof) which may precede the search for the real cause. Sometimes the thing is evident but its cause is hidden, like magnetic attraction—the existence is known by sense, but the cause is sought. Sometimes the middle term of a syllogism is both the logical proof and the real cause, so the two inquiries (proof and cause) coincide in a single demonstration.

شرح الاسم
حد بحسب الذات

Conception of the Impossible by Analogy
Author explains analogical conception of impossibles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Islamic logicians conceive of impossible things through analogy with existents.

Core Idea
Impossibles are conceived by analogy, not directly.

We often ask about things that cannot exist. But how can we even think of them? The author answers that even the impossible is conceived through a prior conception of existents, either by simple analogy or by combining non-impossible parts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
The author asks: if something is impossible and has no form in existence, how can it be conceived in the mind?
2
Distinguish simple vs composite impossibles
Simple impossibles (e.g., void, opposite of God) have no parts; composite impossibles (e.g., a flying man) have parts that are not impossible individually.
3
Analyze the analogical method for simple impossibles
A simple impossible is conceived by analogy with an existing thing that shares a relation: void as a receptacle for bodies, opposite of God as hot is to cold.
The impossible itself has no essence; we grasp it through a possible thing.
4
Analyze the method for composite impossibles
First conceive the non-impossible parts (e.g., human, flying), then imagine their combination analogous to existing composites.
Composition itself is conceived because it exists in real things.
5
Conclude the epistemological principle
All conception of non-existents is based on prior knowledge of existents. This is how the term 'non-existent' signifies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في كيفية إصابة المجهولات من المعلومات وكل واحد من هذه المطالب إنما يتوصل إليه بأمور معلومة لكن يجب أن تعلم أن المطلوب إذا كان معدوما محال الوجود كيف يتصور (۱) حتى يطلب بعد ذلك هل هو أو ليس؛ والمحال لا صورة له في الوجود فكيف تؤخذ عنه صورة في الذهن يكون ذلك المتصور معناه. فنقول في الجواب إن هذا المحال إما أن يكون مفردًا لا تركيب فيه ولا تفصيل، ولا يمكن أن يتصور إلا بنوع من المقايسة بالموجود كالخلاء وضد الله تعالى: فإن الخلاء إنما يتصور للأجسام كالقابل، والضد بأنه الله تعالى كالحار للبارد فيكون المحال متصورا بصورة آخر ممكن ينسب إليه المحال. فأما في ذاته فلا يمكن أن يكون متصورا أو معقولا؛ وكيف ولا ذات له البتة. وأما الذي فيه تركيب ما وتفصيل مثل إنسان يطير فإنما يتصور أولا تفاصيل التي هي غير محالة ثم يتصور لتلك التفاصيل اقتران ما على قياس اقتران الأشياء الموجودة المركبة؛ فتكون هناك أجزاء وتأليف. والتأليف من جملة ما هو تأليف متصور لوجوده فإذن المعدوم إنما يتصور بتصور متقدم للموجودات؛ فعلى هذا النحو تعطى دلالة اسم المعدوم. والعلم بصدق قضية كلية مستلزم للعلم بانتساب محمولها إلى كل واحد من جزئيات موضوعها لكن بالقوة لا بالفعل لأنا إذا عرفنا أن كل إنسان حيوان لا نعرف مثلا أن الذي هو في البحر حيوان لأنا لا نعرفه بالفعل. نعم لو اقترن بالعلم السابق علمنا بأن زيدًا موجود وعلمنا بأنه إنسان علمنا أنه حيوان؛ وهذا معنى قولنا إن انتساب المحمول إلى كل واحد من جزئيات الموضوع معلوم بالقوة. ومن هذا علم أن العلم بصدق القضية الكلية لا يتوقف على العلم بكل
Chapter: On how to attain unknown things from known things. Each of these queries is reached through known matters, but you must know that if the sought thing is non-existent and impossible in existence, how is it conceived so that afterwards one asks whether it is or not? The impossible has no form in existence, so how can a form of it be taken in the mind such that the conceived thing is its meaning? We say in answer: This impossible is either simple, having no composition or detail, and it cannot be conceived except by some kind of analogy with an existing thing, like the void and the opposite of God Almighty. For the void is conceived in relation to bodies as a receptacle, and the opposite of God Almighty as hot is to cold. Thus the impossible is conceived through the form of another possible thing to which the impossible is related. As for its essence, it cannot be conceived or understood; indeed, it has no essence at all. As for that which has some composition and detail, like 'a man who flies', it is first conceived through details that are not impossible, then a certain conjunction of those details is conceived analogously to the conjunction of existing composite things. Thus there are parts and composition. Composition is among what is conceived as composition due to its existence. So the non-existent is only conceived through a prior conception of existents. In this way, the signification of the term 'non-existent' is given. Knowledge of the truth of a universal proposition entails knowledge of the attribution of its predicate to each one of the particulars of its subject, but potentially, not actually. For if we know that every human is an animal, we do not know, for example, that the one who is in the sea is an animal, because we do not know him actually. Yes, if the prior knowledge is joined by knowledge that Zayd exists and that he is a human, we would know that he is an animal. This is the meaning of our statement that the attribution of the predicate to each one of the particulars of the subject is known potentially. From this it is known that knowledge of the truth of a universal proposition does not depend on knowledge of every...
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological explanation of how one can conceive of non-existent or impossible things and how universal knowledge relates to particulars. The author begins with a crucial question: if something is impossible (like a void or a partner to God), how can we even conceive it to ask whether it exists? The answer is that simple impossibles are conceived by analogy with existing things. For example, 'void' is conceived as a receptacle for bodies, and 'opposite of God' is conceived by analogy with opposites like hot and cold. The impossible itself has no essence in the mind; we only grasp it through a possible thing to which it is related. For composite impossibles (e.g., a flying human), we first conceive the individual parts (human, flying) which are not impossible, then we imagine a combination analogous to how existing composites are conjoined. Thus, all conception of non-existents is based on prior knowledge of existents. Then the author turns to universal propositions. Knowing a universal like 'Every human is an animal' entails potential knowledge that each particular human is an animal. We do not know actually every individual, but if we later know a specific individual exists and is human, then the universal knowledge becomes actual. This explains how universal knowledge does not require enumerative knowledge of every particular. The lesson underscores the foundational role of analogical conception and the distinction between potential and actual knowledge in Islamic logic.

معدوم
محال
تصور
بالقوة
