Potential Knowledge from Universal Propositions
Author explains potential knowledge from universal propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between potential and actual knowledge of particulars derived from universal propositions.

Core Idea
Universal knowledge gives potential, not actual, knowledge of each particular.

When you know that every human is an animal, do you know that each specific human is an animal? The answer is yes, but only potentially until you actually encounter that human. This distinction is crucial in Islamic logic and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the universal claim
Take a universal proposition like 'Every human is an animal'. It is a statement about all individuals of a kind.
2
Define potential vs actual knowledge
Potential knowledge (bil-quwwa) means the knowledge is implicitly available but not yet actualized. Actual knowledge (bil-fi'l) requires direct encounter or additional premises.
3
Analyze the example given
The author says: knowing 'every human is an animal' does not mean we actually know that a specific human (e.g., one in the sea) is an animal, because we do not know that human actually.
4
Identify the condition for actualization
If we later learn that Zayd exists and is human, then the universal knowledge combines with that particular knowledge to produce actual knowledge that Zayd is an animal.
5
Conclude the epistemological principle
Knowledge of a universal proposition does not require enumerative knowledge of every particular; it only entails potential knowledge of each particular.

فصل: في كيفية إصابة المجهولات من المعلومات وكل واحد من هذه المطالب إنما يتوصل إليه بأمور معلومة لكن يجب أن تعلم أن المطلوب إذا كان معدوما محال الوجود كيف يتصور (۱) حتى يطلب بعد ذلك هل هو أو ليس؛ والمحال لا صورة له في الوجود فكيف تؤخذ عنه صورة في الذهن يكون ذلك المتصور معناه. فنقول في الجواب إن هذا المحال إما أن يكون مفردًا لا تركيب فيه ولا تفصيل، ولا يمكن أن يتصور إلا بنوع من المقايسة بالموجود كالخلاء وضد الله تعالى: فإن الخلاء إنما يتصور للأجسام كالقابل، والضد بأنه الله تعالى كالحار للبارد فيكون المحال متصورا بصورة آخر ممكن ينسب إليه المحال. فأما في ذاته فلا يمكن أن يكون متصورا أو معقولا؛ وكيف ولا ذات له البتة. وأما الذي فيه تركيب ما وتفصيل مثل إنسان يطير فإنما يتصور أولا تفاصيل التي هي غير محالة ثم يتصور لتلك التفاصيل اقتران ما على قياس اقتران الأشياء الموجودة المركبة؛ فتكون هناك أجزاء وتأليف. والتأليف من جملة ما هو تأليف متصور لوجوده فإذن المعدوم إنما يتصور بتصور متقدم للموجودات؛ فعلى هذا النحو تعطى دلالة اسم المعدوم. والعلم بصدق قضية كلية مستلزم للعلم بانتساب محمولها إلى كل واحد من جزئيات موضوعها لكن بالقوة لا بالفعل لأنا إذا عرفنا أن كل إنسان حيوان لا نعرف مثلا أن الذي هو في البحر حيوان لأنا لا نعرفه بالفعل. نعم لو اقترن بالعلم السابق علمنا بأن زيدًا موجود وعلمنا بأنه إنسان علمنا أنه حيوان؛ وهذا معنى قولنا إن انتساب المحمول إلى كل واحد من جزئيات الموضوع معلوم بالقوة. ومن هذا علم أن العلم بصدق القضية الكلية لا يتوقف على العلم بكل
Chapter: On how to attain unknown things from known things. Each of these queries is reached through known matters, but you must know that if the sought thing is non-existent and impossible in existence, how is it conceived so that afterwards one asks whether it is or not? The impossible has no form in existence, so how can a form of it be taken in the mind such that the conceived thing is its meaning? We say in answer: This impossible is either simple, having no composition or detail, and it cannot be conceived except by some kind of analogy with an existing thing, like the void and the opposite of God Almighty. For the void is conceived in relation to bodies as a receptacle, and the opposite of God Almighty as hot is to cold. Thus the impossible is conceived through the form of another possible thing to which the impossible is related. As for its essence, it cannot be conceived or understood; indeed, it has no essence at all. As for that which has some composition and detail, like 'a man who flies', it is first conceived through details that are not impossible, then a certain conjunction of those details is conceived analogously to the conjunction of existing composite things. Thus there are parts and composition. Composition is among what is conceived as composition due to its existence. So the non-existent is only conceived through a prior conception of existents. In this way, the signification of the term 'non-existent' is given. Knowledge of the truth of a universal proposition entails knowledge of the attribution of its predicate to each one of the particulars of its subject, but potentially, not actually. For if we know that every human is an animal, we do not know, for example, that the one who is in the sea is an animal, because we do not know him actually. Yes, if the prior knowledge is joined by knowledge that Zayd exists and that he is a human, we would know that he is an animal. This is the meaning of our statement that the attribution of the predicate to each one of the particulars of the subject is known potentially. From this it is known that knowledge of the truth of a universal proposition does not depend on knowledge of every...
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological explanation of how one can conceive of non-existent or impossible things and how universal knowledge relates to particulars. The author begins with a crucial question: if something is impossible (like a void or a partner to God), how can we even conceive it to ask whether it exists? The answer is that simple impossibles are conceived by analogy with existing things. For example, 'void' is conceived as a receptacle for bodies, and 'opposite of God' is conceived by analogy with opposites like hot and cold. The impossible itself has no essence in the mind; we only grasp it through a possible thing to which it is related. For composite impossibles (e.g., a flying human), we first conceive the individual parts (human, flying) which are not impossible, then we imagine a combination analogous to how existing composites are conjoined. Thus, all conception of non-existents is based on prior knowledge of existents. Then the author turns to universal propositions. Knowing a universal like 'Every human is an animal' entails potential knowledge that each particular human is an animal. We do not know actually every individual, but if we later know a specific individual exists and is human, then the universal knowledge becomes actual. This explains how universal knowledge does not require enumerative knowledge of every particular. The lesson underscores the foundational role of analogical conception and the distinction between potential and actual knowledge in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
بالقوة
بالفعل
قضية كلية
جزئيات

Key Takeaway
Universal knowledge implies potential, not actual, knowledge of each particular; actual knowledge requires additional information about the particular.

How the Mind Conceives the Impossible
Author explains how non-existent and impossible objects are conceived through analogy with existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two types of impossible objects and the role of analogy and composition in their conception.

Core Idea
Impossibles are conceived not directly, but through analogies with existents.

How can we think about something that has no existence? This is a key epistemological puzzle. In this passage, the author resolves it by distinguishing simple impossibles (conceived by analogy) from composite impossibles (conceived by combining possible parts). Understanding this process is essential for any inquiry that involves non-existent objects, such as in kalam when discussing the attributes of God or the void.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
The author starts by noting that every inquiry is based on known things, but if the object is non-existent and impossible, how can it be conceived at all?
The challenge is that the impossible has no form in existence from which a mental image could be taken.
2
Distinguish the two types of impossibles
The author divides the impossible into two categories: simple (no composition or detail) and composite (has parts and composition).
Examples of simple impossibles: the void and the opposite of God. Example of composite: a flying human.
3
Analyze the solution for simple impossibles
Simple impossibles cannot be conceived directly but only by analogy with existent things. For instance, the void is conceived as a receptacle for bodies, and the opposite of God is conceived by analogy with opposites like hot and cold.
The impossible is conceived through the form of another possible thing to which it is attributed.
4
Analyze the solution for composite impossibles
For composite impossibles, we first conceive the parts (which are not impossible individually), then combine them analogously to how existing composite things are combined.
The composition itself is conceived because composition exists in the world.
5
Synthesize the general principle
The conclusion is that the non-existent is only conceived through a prior conception of existents, whether by direct analogy or by compositional analogy.
This explains how the meaning of the term for a non-existent thing is given.

وكل واحد من هذه المطالب إنما يتوصل إليه بأمور معلومة لكن يجب أن تعلم أن المطلوب إذا كان معدوما محال الوجود كيف يتصور (۱) حتى يطلب بعد ذلك هل هو أو ليس؛ والمحال لا صورة له في الوجود فكيف تؤخذ عنه صورة في الذهن يكون ذلك المتصور معناه. فنقول في الجواب إن هذا المحال إما أن يكون مفردًا لا تركيب فيه ولا تفصيل، ولا يمكن أن يتصور إلا بنوع من المقايسة بالموجود كالخلاء وضد الله تعالى: فإن الخلاء إنما يتصور للأجسام كالقابل، والضد بأنه الله تعالى كالحار للبارد فيكون المحال متصورا بصورة آخر ممكن ينسب إليه المحال. فأما في ذاته فلا يمكن أن يكون متصورا أو معقولا؛ وكيف ولا ذات له البتة. وأما الذي فيه تركيب ما وتفصيل مثل إنسان يطير فإنما يتصور أولا تفاصيل التي هي غير محالة ثم يتصور لتلك التفاصيل اقتران ما على قياس اقتران الأشياء الموجودة المركبة؛ فتكون هناك أجزاء وتأليف. والتأليف من جملة ما هو تأليف متصور لوجوده فإذن المعدوم إنما يتصور بتصور متقدم للموجودات؛ فعلى هذا النحو تعطى دلالة اسم المعدوم.
And each one of these inquiries is arrived at through known things, but you must know that when the sought thing is non-existent and impossible in existence, how is it conceived so that afterwards it is sought whether it is or is not? And the impossible has no form in existence, so how can a form be taken from it in the mind such that that conception is its meaning? So we say in response: This impossible is either a simple term with no composition or detail, and it cannot be conceived except by a kind of comparison with the existent, like the void and the opposite of God, may He be exalted. For the void is conceived in relation to bodies as a receptacle, and the opposite of God as the hot is to the cold. Thus the impossible is conceived through the form of another possible thing to which the impossible is attributed. As for its essence, it cannot be conceived or intellected; how could it, since it has no essence at all. As for that which has some composition and detail, such as 'a flying human,' it is first conceived in its details, which are not impossible, then those details are conceived with a certain combination analogous to the combination of existing composite things. Thus there are parts and composition. And composition is among what is composition conceived due to its existence. So the non-existent is only conceived through a prior conception of existents. In this way, the indication of the name of the non-existent is given.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion of how the mind can conceive non-existent or impossible objects, which is necessary for inquiry. The author begins by stating that every inquiry (مطلب) is reached through known things. But a problem arises: if the object of inquiry is non-existent and impossible, how can it be conceived at all? Since it has no form in existence, no mental image can be directly taken from it. The author then divides the impossible into two types: simple and composite. A simple impossible (e.g., a void or an opposite of God) cannot be conceived directly but only by analogy with existent things. For example, the void is conceived as a receptacle for bodies, and the opposite of God is conceived by analogy with opposites like hot and cold. So the impossible is conceived through the form of a possible thing. A composite impossible (e.g., a flying human) is conceived by first imagining the parts (human and flying), which are possible individually, and then combining them in a way analogous to existing composites. Thus the conception of the non-existent always depends on prior conception of existents. The author concludes that this is how the meaning of the term for a non-existent thing is given.

The non-existent is conceived through a prior conception of existents, either by analogy or by composition.

Resolving the Meno Paradox: Conception and Assent
Author presents Meno's paradox and resolves it using the distinction between conception and assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the distinction between conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) resolves the Meno paradox and justify why primary principles are necessary for learning.

Core Idea
You can seek what you know in conception but not yet in assent.

The Meno paradox poses a challenge to all learning: how can you seek knowledge you don't have? The author of this text resolves the paradox by distinguishing two aspects of knowledge: conception (having a mental image) and assent (judging that something is true). Like recognizing a runaway slave by appearance but not knowing his location, learning is possible when we know something in one respect and seek it in another. This insight also grounds the need for foundational principles in both conception and assent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the paradox
Read the opening: Meno argues that learning is impossible because you cannot seek what you already know (since it's already had) nor what you don't know (since you wouldn't recognise it). Identify the two horns of the dilemma.
2
Identify the two kinds of knowledge
Notice the author distinguishes between conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). Conception is a mental grasp without judgment (e.g., the image of a person). Assent is a judgment (e.g., that person is in the house).
3
Apply the runaway slave example
The author uses the example of a runaway slave: you know him by conception (appearance) but not by assent (location). When you find him, the potential knowledge of assent becomes actual. This shows how the seeker knows in one respect and is ignorant in another.
The example shows the paradox only works if you assume complete knowledge or complete ignorance.
4
Recognise the need for primary principles
The author concludes that all learning requires prior knowledge, which would lead to infinite regress unless there are self-evident primary principles (mabadi' ula) for both conception and assent. These principles are the starting points for all knowledge.
This is a foundationalist response to the regress argument.

وكذلك الذي جرى بين مانن [Menon]) وسقراط أنه خاطب سقراط [۲۷۷ظ] في إبطال التعليم فقال: إن الطالب علماً تاماً إما أن يكون طالبًا لما يعلمه؛ وذلك باطل لاستحالة تحصيل ما يكون حاصلا ، وإما أن يكون طالبا لما يجهله؛ وإنه باطل أيضًا لأنه لا يعلمه إذا أصابه كمن يطلب عبدًا آبقا لا يعرفه. فإنه علم حل هذه الشبهة أيضًا بما مرَّ ؛ وقد مرَّ الكلام فيه كذلك. وذلك أن المطلوب إن كان معلومًا علماً تاماً فلا يمكن طلبه، وإن كان مجهولاً جهلا تاما فلا يمكن طلبه أيضا بل من اللوازم أن يكون مجهولا بوجه ومعلوما بوجهين، معلوم تصورًا بالفعل، ومعلوم تصديقا بالقوة والمثال الذي أورده مانن لا يكون كما ينبغي، فإنا نعلم المطلوب بالتصور أولا كما نعلم الآبق بالتصور أولا؛ وقد علمنا من قبل علما بالتصديق. فإذا سلكنا السبيل إليه وشاهدناه حصل في الحال ذلك العلم الذي كان من قبل. والآبق قد يكون معلومًا من جهة التصور ومجهولاً من جهة المكان فطلبه إذن من جهة ما هو مجهول لا من جهة ما هو معلوم. ثم إذا تقرر أنه كيف يكون التعليم والتعلم الذهني؛ وإن ذلك إنما يحصل بعلم سابق فيجب أن يكون عندنا مبادٍ أولى للتصديق ومبادٍ أولى للتصور. ولو أنه كان كل تعليم وتعلم بعلم ثم كان كل علم بتعليم وتعلم لذهب الأمر إلى
And similarly, the exchange that occurred between Meno and Socrates—he addressed Socrates in invalidating learning, saying: 'The seeker of complete knowledge is either seeking what he already knows—this is false because it is impossible to obtain what is already present—or he is seeking what he does not know—and this is also false because he would not recognize it if he found it, like someone seeking a runaway slave he does not know.' So he knew the solution to this difficulty also by what has passed, and the discussion has passed similarly. That is that the sought thing—if it is known with complete knowledge, it cannot be sought, and if it is unknown with complete ignorance, it cannot be sought either. Rather, it must be unknown in one respect and known in two respects: known in conception (tasawwur) actually, and known in assent (tasdiq) potentially. And the example that Meno brought is not as it should be, for we know the sought thing by conception first, just as we know the runaway by conception first, and we already had knowledge by assent. So when we take the path to it and see it, that knowledge which was beforehand occurs immediately. And the runaway may be known from the aspect of conception and unknown from the aspect of location, so his seeking is thus from the aspect of what is unknown, not from the aspect of what is known. Then when it is established how mental teaching and learning are, and that it only occurs through prior knowledge, then there must be for us primary principles for assent and primary principles for conception. And if every teaching and learning were through knowledge, and then every knowledge were through teaching and learning, the matter would go to
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage addresses the Meno paradox, which argues that learning is impossible: one cannot seek what one already knows or what one does not know. The author resolves it by stating that the object is known in conception (tasawwur) actually but unknown in assent (tasdiq) potentially. Using the example of a runaway slave, the seeker knows him by conception (his appearance) but does not know his location (assent). When found, the potential knowledge becomes actual. The author then argues that all learning requires prior knowledge, so there must be primary principles (mabadi' ula) for both knowledge by conception and knowledge by assent, otherwise there would be an infinite regress. This establishes the foundationalist epistemology underlying logical inquiry.

تصديق
مبادٍ أولى

Key Takeaway
Learning is possible because the seeker has conception of the object but lacks assent, which can be acquired through investigation, and this process requires foundational primary principles.

Resolving Meno's Paradox: Potential and Actual Knowledge
Author resolves Meno's paradox and asserts necessity of first principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the resolution of Meno's paradox using the distinction between potential (bil-quwwa) and actual (bil-fi'l) knowledge, and identify the necessity of first principles (mabadi' uwal) for both conceptualization (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq).

Core Idea
Learning is possible because prior knowledge is potential, not absent; first principles terminate the regress.

Meno's paradox threatens the very possibility of learning: how can we seek what we do not know? And if we already know it, seeking is pointless. The author resolves this by distinguishing between actual and potential knowledge, and between conceptualization and assent. He then shows that to avoid an infinite regress of learning, we must possess first principles that are known without a middle term. This passage is crucial for understanding the epistemological foundations of Islamic logic and how knowledge acquisition is justified.

Guided Path
1
Understand Meno's Paradox
The paradox states that learning is impossible because if you know something, you don't need to seek it; if you are completely ignorant, you won't recognize it when you find it. Identify this dilemma as presented in the passage.
The author references the dialogue between Meno and Socrates.
2
Identify the Resolution: Three Aspects
The author says the sought object is known in one aspect and unknown in two aspects: it is known in conceptualization actually (tasawwur bil-fi'l) and known in assent potentially (tasdiq bil-quwwa). This breaks the false dichotomy of complete knowledge vs complete ignorance.
Example of the runaway slave: known by description (conceptualization), unknown location (assent potential).
3
Apply the Resolution to the 'Two is Even' Example
The author uses the case of knowing that every two is even but not knowing that a particular two (in your sleeve) is even. The resolution: knowledge of that particular's twoness is potential, not actual. Once actualized, the evenness follows.
This shows that potential knowledge can be actualized without new knowledge from scratch.
4
Recognize the Necessity of First Principles
To avoid infinite regress (every learning requires prior knowledge), we must accept that there are first principles: things assented to without a middle term (axioms) and simple concepts conceptually apprehended without definition. These are the foundations of all knowledge.
The author announces the next topic: principles of certain assent, i.e., the starting points of demonstration (burhan).

واحد من جزئيات موضوعها. وعلم من هذا حل الشبهة المشهورة؛ وهو أنكم تعلمون أن كل اثنين زوج ولا تعلمون أن الذي في كمي زوج مع أنه اثنان لأن العلم باثنينيته ما في كمه بالقوة لا بالفعل حتى لو علمنا أن الذي في كمه اثنان علمنا أنه زوج. وقد يقال في حل هذه الشبهة إنا لا ندعي أن كل اثنين زوج بل ندعي أن كل اثنين مما عرفناه فهو زوج؛ وهذا فاسد فإن الاثنين زوج عرف أو لم يعرف وهذا من جملة ما قد مرَّ من قبل. وكذلك الذي جرى بين مانن وسقراط أنه خاطب سقراط في إبطال التعليم فقال: إن الطالب علماً تاماً إما أن يكون طالبًا لما يعلمه؛ وذلك باطل لاستحالة تحصيل ما يكون حاصلا ، وإما أن يكون طالبا لما يجهله؛ وإنه باطل أيضًا لأنه لا يعلمه إذا أصابه كمن يطلب عبدًا آبقا لا يعرفه. فإنه علم حل هذه الشبهة أيضًا بما مرَّ ؛ وقد مرَّ الكلام فيه كذلك. وذلك أن المطلوب إن كان معلومًا علماً تاماً فلا يمكن طلبه، وإن كان مجهولاً جهلا تاما فلا يمكن طلبه أيضا بل من اللوازم أن يكون مجهولا بوجه ومعلوما بوجهين، معلوم تصورًا بالفعل، ومعلوم تصديقا بالقوة والمثال الذي أورده مانن لا يكون كما ينبغي، فإنا نعلم المطلوب بالتصور أولا كما نعلم الآبق بالتصور أولا؛ وقد علمنا من قبل علما بالتصديق. فإذا سلكنا السبيل إليه وشاهدناه حصل في الحال ذلك العلم الذي كان من قبل. والآبق قد يكون معلومًا من جهة التصور ومجهولاً من جهة المكان فطلبه إذن من جهة ما هو مجهول لا من جهة ما هو معلوم. ثم إذا تقرر أنه كيف يكون التعليم والتعلم الذهني؛ وإن ذلك إنما يحصل بعلم سابق فيجب أن يكون عندنا مبادٍ أولى للتصديق ومبادٍ أولى للتصور. ولو أنه كان كل تعليم وتعلم بعلم ثم كان كل علم بتعليم وتعلم لذهب الأمر إلى غير النهاية : فلم يكن تعلم وتعليم بل لا محالة أن تكون عندنا أمور مصدق بها بلا وسط، وأمور متصورة بلا وسط، وأن تكون هي المبادئ الأولى للتصديق والتصور. ولنبدأ بمبادئ التصديق ولنستعمل أولا مبادئ التصديق اليقيني؛وبالله التوفيق.
One of the particulars of its subject. And from this is known the resolution of the famous perplexity: that you know that every two is even, but you do not know that that which is in your sleeve is even, even though it is two, because the knowledge of its twoness is in potentiality, not in actuality, such that if we knew that what is in his sleeve is two, we would know that it is even. And it may be said in resolving this perplexity: we do not claim that every two is even, but we claim that every two that we have known is even; and this is false, for the two is even whether known or not, and this is among the things that have passed before. Likewise that which occurred between Meno and Socrates, that he addressed Socrates in refuting teaching, saying: The seeker of complete knowledge is either a seeker of what he knows – and that is false because of the impossibility of acquiring what is already present – or a seeker of what he is ignorant of – and that is also false because he does not know it when he encounters it, like someone seeking a runaway slave he does not know. For he knew the resolution of this perplexity also by what has passed; and the discussion has passed likewise. And that is that if the sought is known completely, its seeking is impossible; and if it is completely unknown, its seeking is also impossible. Rather, it is necessary that it be unknown in one aspect and known in two aspects: known in conceptualization (tasawwur) actually, and known in assent (tasdiq) potentially. And the example that Meno gave is not as it should be, for we know the sought first by conceptualization, just as we know the runaway first by conceptualization; and we had prior knowledge by assent. So when we proceed to it and see it, there occurs immediately that knowledge which was prior. And the runaway may be known in terms of conceptualization but unknown in terms of place, so his seeking is then in terms of what is unknown, not in terms of what is known. Then when it is established how intellectual teaching and learning occur, and that this only happens through prior knowledge, it is necessary that we have first principles for assent and first principles for conceptualization. And if every teaching and learning were through knowledge, and then every knowledge through teaching and learning, it would lead to infinity: so there would be no learning or teaching. Rather, we necessarily have things that are assented to without a middle term, and things that are conceptualized without a middle term, and these are the first principles of assent and conceptualization. And let us begin with the principles of assent and first employ the principles of certain assent; and with God is success.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage resolves the famous Meno paradox concerning the possibility of learning. The author first addresses a simpler example: knowing that every two is even but not knowing that a particular two (in one's sleeve) is even. The resolution is that knowledge of that particular's twoness is potential, not actual. The objection that 'every two we have known is even' is false because the property holds regardless. Then the author recounts Meno's argument against teaching: one cannot seek what one already knows or what one is completely ignorant of. The solution is that the sought object is known in one aspect (conceptualization actually) and unknown in another (assent potentially). The analogy of a runaway slave illustrates this: you know the slave by description (conceptualization) but not his location. So seeking is for the unknown aspect. From this, the author concludes that learning requires prior knowledge, and to avoid an infinite regress, we must have first principles of assent (self-evident propositions) and first principles of conceptualization (simple concepts). The passage ends by indicating that the next topic will be the principles of certain assent, setting the stage for the discussion of demonstration (burhan).

The Two Types of Acquired Knowledge and Certainty in Burhan
Author distinguishes conceptual and assentive knowledge, defines certain knowledge, and links burhan to certain premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish conceptual (tasawwuri) from assentive (tasdiqi) knowledge, and identify the modal condition that elevates a true proposition to certain knowledge (yaqini) suitable for burhan.

Core Idea
Certainty requires not just knowing that something is so, but knowing it cannot be otherwise.

In this passage, the author lays the epistemological groundwork for demonstration. He distinguishes two types of acquired knowledge — conceptual and assentive — and then refines the notion of certainty. You will learn why not every true assent qualifies as yaqini and how this modal condition is essential for burhan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two kinds of acquired knowledge
The author says acquired knowledge can be conceptual (tasawwuri) or assentive (tasdiqi). Note the method: conceptual via definitions, postulates, positions; assentive via syllogisms.
Conceptual knowledge provides the terms; assentive knowledge provides the truth value and modality.
2
Distinguish absolute results from certain results
The author contrasts 'absolute results' (you know that something is so, but not that it cannot be otherwise) from stronger assent that includes modal necessity.
Absolute results are not enough for burhan; they lack the 'cannot be otherwise' component.
3
Define true certain knowledge (yaqini)
The author defines yaqini as knowledge that something is so AND that it cannot be otherwise, with an unshakeable belief.
If the 'cannot be otherwise' component is missing, the certainty is only temporary.
4
Connect burhan to certain premises
The passage concludes that burhan is a syllogism composed of yaqini premises.
This anchors demonstration in the highest epistemic standard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أو لا بأن العلم المكتسب قد يكون علمًا تصوريًا، وقد يكون علمًا تصديقيًا؛ فيقال علم مكتسب للتصور الواقع بالحدود والمصادرات والأوضاع التي يفتتح بها المعلوم. ويقال لكل تصديق حق وقع من قياس منتج أن كل كذا كذا أو ليس كذا لما كان أخص من هذا وهو كل تصديق حق وقع من قياس يوقع التصديق بأن كذا كذا ، ويوقع أيضًا بأنه لا يمكن أن لا يكون كذا. وفرق [۲۷۸] و بين التصديقين؛ إذ النتائج المطلقة يعلم أنها كذا ولا يعلم معها التصديق بها لا يمكن أن لا يكون كذا إلا إذا أخذ المطلق عامًا للضروري ما دام الذات موجودة وللضروري ما دام الموضوع موجودًا على ما وضع ثم العلم الذي هو بالحقيقة يقيني هو ما يعتقد منه أن كذا كذا ويعتقد أنه لا يمكن أن لا يكون كذا اعتقادًا لا يمكن أن يزول والعلم اليقيني هو الثاني بالنسبة إلى المطلق، وهذا اليقيني أيضًا بالنسبة إلى العلم اليقيني. فإن العلم اليقيني إذا لم يقترن به هذا الثاني فهو يقيني يمكن أن يكون غير دائم بل وقتا ما فالبرهان قياس مؤلف يقيني.
Know first that acquired knowledge can be conceptual (tasawwuri) or assentive (tasdiqi). Acquired knowledge is said for conceptualization achieved through definitions, postulates, and positions by which the thing known is opened. And for every true assent that occurs from a productive syllogism that every such-and-such is such-and-such or not such-and-such, since it is more specific than this, it is every true assent that occurs from a syllogism that produces assent that such-and-such is such-and-such, and also produces that it cannot be otherwise. There is a difference between the two assents; because absolute results are known that they are such-and-such, but one does not know with them the assent that they cannot be otherwise unless the absolute is taken as general for the necessary as long as essence exists and for the necessary as long as subject exists according to what is established. Then knowledge that is truly certain is that which holds that such-and-such is such-and-such and holds that it cannot be otherwise with a belief that cannot be eliminated. Certain knowledge is the second relative to the absolute. This certain knowledge is also relative to certain knowledge. For if certain knowledge is not accompanied by this second, it is certain that can be non-permanent but temporary. So demonstration is a syllogism composed of certain premises.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage expands on the epistemological foundations of demonstration. The author first distinguishes between tasawwuri (conceptual) and tasdiqi (assentive) knowledge, noting that each can be acquired through definitions (conceptual) or syllogisms (assentive). For assentive knowledge, he points out that a true assent from a productive syllogism gives both the fact and the impossibility of its opposite—this is the highest form of certainty. He contrasts 'absolute' results (merely knowing that something is so) with the stronger certainty that includes modal necessity. True certain knowledge (yaqini) is defined as a belief that something is so and cannot be otherwise, and this belief is unshakeable. If the belief lacks the 'cannot be otherwise' component, it is only temporary certainty. The passage then concludes that burhan is a syllogism composed of certain (yaqini) premises. This anchors demonstration in the most rigorous epistemic standard.

علم تصديقي
يقيني

Key Takeaway
Burhan requires premises that are not only true but also believed to be necessarily so — this is yaqini certainty.

Self-Evident Propositions and Certain Knowledge
Author defines conditions for self-evident propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a proposition is self-evident (bayan bi-nafsihi) based on the presence or absence of a causal connection between its terms.

Core Idea
Self-evident knowledge requires no proof—its truth is necessitated by the subject's essence.

How do we know a proposition is true when its predicate has no causal link to its subject? The passage addresses this by distinguishing self-evident propositions from those requiring proof. Here we focus on self-evident propositions: if the relation is clear from the subject's essence, no demonstration is needed and certainty is attained.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate
Find the term being described (subject) and the attribute claimed about it (predicate). Example: 'the world is temporally originated' – 'the world' is subject, 'temporally originated' is predicate.
2
Check for a causal connection (sabab)
Does the predicate exist in the subject because of a separate cause? If so, a middle term may be needed. If no cause exists, the relation is either self-evident or cannot be proven.
The passage defines sabab as an ontological cause linking predicate to subject.
3
Determine if the relation is self-evident
If the predicate's relation is immediately known from the subject's essence—without needing a middle term—then it is self-evident. The text says 'the relation is due to the essence of the subject' (لذات الموضوع).
Self-evidence here means the mind directly grasps the connection without inference.
4
Confirm certainty
If self-evident, the knowledge is certain (yaqini) and no proof is required. The passage states: 'since the connection is necessitated, the knowledge obtained is certain.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في كيفية تعرف ما ليس لمحموله سبب في موضوعه وفي الاستقراء والتجربة وموجبهما ثم لقائل أن يقول إنه إذا لم يكن بين المحمول والموضوع سبب في نفس الوجود فكيف نبين النسبة بينهما ببيان؟ فنقول إذا كان ذلك بينا بنفسه فلا حاجة إلى البيان بل يحصل فيه التعين من جهة أن نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع لذات الموضوع فذات الموضوع تجب مواصلتها للمحمول؛ وقد وجبت المواصلة فيكون العلم الحاصل يقينيًا. وإن لم يكن بينا بنفسه فلا يمكن البتة أن يقع به يقين غير زائل لأنه إذا جعل الوسط ما ليس بسبب لم يمكن أن يطلب به العلم اليقيني. وإذا جعل ما هو سبب كان الوسط سببا؛ وكلامنا فيما ليس له سبب أصلا فسببه أن تكون أمثال هذه بينة بنفسها أو بالاستقراء؛ غير أنه إذا بين بالاستقراء فلا يخلو من أن يكون وجود نسبة المحمول إلى جزئيات الموضوع بينا بنفسه بلا سبب إذن إنما يبين بالاستقراء بهذا النوع أو يكون وجود نسبة المحمول إلى جزئيات الموضوع في نفسه بسبب، فإن كان الأول فإما أن يكون البيان بالحس فقط ؛ وذلك لا يوجب الدوام ولا رفع إمكان الزوال. وإما أن يكون بالعقل؛ وهذا غير جائز ؛ إذ المحمول لا يمكن أن يكون ذاتيا مقوما؛ إذ المقوم غير مطلوب في الحقيقة لما بينه من بعد بل وجوده لما هو ذاتي له بين . وإن كان عرضيّاً كان ذلك من الأعراض اللازمة لكلي يقال على الجزئيات فيكون هذا العرض لازما لشيء من المعاني الذاتية للجزئيات؛ إذ العرض الذي هذا صفته هذا شأنه. وإذا كان كذلك كان حمله على جزئي لأجل معنى موجود له ولغيره من الذاتيات فيكون ذلك سببًا عاما لوجود هذا العرض في الجزئيات؛ وكلامنا فيما لا سبب له. وإذا عُلم من غير جهة ذلك السبب لم يكن ذلك العلم يقينيا، ولا يمكن أن يكون عرضًا للمعنى العام حتى يصح أن يكون مطلوبًا لكنه ذاتي لكل واحد من الجزئيات عن آخرها. فإن الذاتي لجميع الجزئيات لا يصح أن يكون عرضيا للمعنى الكلي المساوي لها؛ لأنه ليس شيء من موضوعات ذلك الكلي يعرض له ذلك الحمل سلبه أو إيجابه. وإذا لم يكن عارضًا لشيء منها فكيف يكون عارضًا لكلها وعارض طبيعة الكلي عارض للكل على ما عرف. وأما إن كان حال المحمول عند جزئيات الموضوع غير بين بنفسه بل يمكن أن يبين ببيان فذلك البيان إما أن لا يوجب كل واحد منهما اليقين الحقيقي الذي نقصده؛ فكيف يوضع ما ليس يقينيا اليقين الحقيقي الذي بعده وإما أن يوجب فيجب أن يتفق في السبب كما مرَّ فيكون وجود السبب للمعنى الكلي أولا . وإذا لم يكن السبب نافعا في المعنى الكلي فلا يكون نافعاً في الجزئي وإن كان نافعا في الكلي، فالنافع هو القياس لا الاستقراء. وأما أن يكون لا سبب هناك البتة فيكون إما بينا بنفسه، وإما بالاستقراء؛ والكلام فيهما قد مرَّ ؛ فظهر أن ما لا سبب لنسبة محموله إلى موضوعه فإما أن يكون بينا بنفسه، وإما أن لا يمكن بيانه.
Chapter: On how to know what has no cause for its predicate in its subject, and on induction and experience and what they necessitate. Then an objector may say: if there is no cause between the predicate and the subject in existence itself, how can we demonstrate the relation between them by a proof? We say: if that is self-evident, there is no need for proof; rather, determination occurs because the relation of the predicate to the subject is due to the essence of the subject, so the essence of the subject necessitates its connection to the predicate; and since the connection is necessitated, the knowledge obtained is certain. If it is not self-evident, it is absolutely impossible for unshakable certainty to occur through it, because if one takes as middle term what is not a cause, it cannot be used to seek certain knowledge. And if one takes what is a cause, the middle term is a cause; but our discourse is about what has no cause at all. So its cause is that such things are either self-evident or [known] by induction. However, if it is proven by induction, it cannot be free from either: the existence of the relation of the predicate to the particulars of the subject is self-evident without a cause—thus it is only proven by induction in this way—or the existence of the relation of the predicate to the particulars of the subject is in itself due to a cause. If it is the first, then the proof is either only through sense, but that does not necessitate permanence nor remove the possibility of change; or through intellect, but this is not permissible, because the predicate cannot be essential and constitutive, since the constitutive is not sought in reality due to its being evident, rather its existence is evident for what is essential to it. If it is accidental, then it is among the necessary accidents of a universal predicated of particulars, so this accident is necessary for something among the essential meanings of the particulars, for the accident that has this description has this property. If so, its predication of a particular is due to a meaning existing in it and in other essential meanings, so that is a general cause for the existence of this accident in the particulars; but our discourse is about what has no cause. If it is known other than through that cause, that knowledge is not certain. And it cannot be an accident of the general meaning so as to be sought, but it is essential for each of the particulars one after another. For what is essential to all particulars cannot be accidental to the universal meaning coextensive with them, because none of the subjects of that universal have that predication, whether negative or positive, occurring to them. And if it is not an accident to any of them, how can it be an accident to all? And the accident to the nature of the universal is an accident to all, as known. As for if the state of the predicate with respect to the particulars of the subject is not self-evident but can be proven by a proof, then that proof either does not necessitate each of them with the true certainty we intend—so how can something non-certain be used for the true certainty that follows?—or it does necessitate, and then it must agree in cause as previously, so the existence of the cause for the universal meaning comes first. If the cause is not useful for the universal meaning, it is not useful for the particular; but if it is useful for the universal, then what is useful is the syllogism, not induction. But if there is no cause at all, then it is either self-evident or [known] by induction; the discussion of these has passed. So it appears that what has no cause for the relation of its predicate to its subject is either self-evident or cannot be proven.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological investigation into how we can have certain knowledge about propositions where the predicate has no causal connection (sabab) to the subject. The author opens by addressing a potential objection: if there is no cause linking predicate and subject in existence, how can we demonstrate the relation? The answer distinguishes two cases. First, if the relation is self-evident (bayan bi-nafsihi), then no proof is needed; the relation is necessitated by the subject's essence, yielding certainty. Second, if it is not self-evident, then certainty is impossible, because any middle term would either be a non-cause (useless) or a cause (contradicting the assumption of no cause). The author then turns to induction (istiqra'). Induction can only work if the relation of the predicate to the particulars is either self-evident (then induction merely collects what is already known) or caused. If self-evident, it is either by sense (which does not guarantee permanence or eliminate possibility of change) or by intellect (not possible for a predicate that is not essential). If the predicate is accidental (aradi), it must be a necessary accident of a universal, and then its predication of a particular is due to a meaning that is a cause, contrary to the assumption. Therefore, for propositions with no cause, the only possibilities are self-evidence or impossibility of proof. All examples used in the commentary must come from the Islamic sciences. For instance, the proposition 'every human is rational' is self-evident—its predicate is essential. Induction, like 'all intoxicants are forbidden' based on observing specific intoxicants, is limited because sense observation does not guarantee permanence.

Key Terms
بين بنفسه
سبب

D) The predicate is accidental (aradi) to the subject.

Key Takeaway
A proposition with no causal connection between its terms must be self-evident to yield certain knowledge.

10 min
Limits of Induction (Istiqra') for Certainty
Author critiques induction for achieving certainty in no-cause predications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate when induction (istiqra') can and cannot produce certainty, based on the absence of a causal connection between terms.

Core Idea
Induction cannot give certainty for no-cause propositions because sense observation is impermanent and accidental predicates require a cause.

Induction (istiqra') is often used to infer universal rules from particular observations. But can it yield certain knowledge when there is no causal link between predicate and subject? The passage dissects induction and shows its limitations: it either relies on self-evidence (making induction redundant) or introduces a cause (contradicting the assumption of no cause). This lesson helps you understand when induction is epistemically weak.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two scenarios for induction
The passage says that if a proposition is proven by induction, either the relation to particulars is self-evident without cause, or it has a cause. Identify which scenario is under discussion.
Our focus is on the first scenario—when there is no cause.
2
Analyze sense-based induction
If the relation is self-evident and known only through sense (hiss), the passage states it does not guarantee permanence (al-dawam) nor remove the possibility of change (imkan al-zawal). This means sense observation cannot yield lasting certainty.
Example: observing that a certain intoxicant is forbidden does not prove all intoxicants are permanently forbidden without additional reasoning.
3
Analyze intellect-based induction
If the relation is self-evident through intellect (aql), the predicate must be essential (dhati). But if it is accidental (aradi), it cannot be self-evident through intellect; it would require a cause, which contradicts the no-cause assumption.
Thus, for accidental predicates, induction fails to give certainty without a cause.
4
Conclude on induction's limits for no-cause propositions
The passage concludes that for propositions with no causal connection, induction cannot produce certainty. Either the relation is self-evident (then induction is unnecessary) or it has a cause (then induction is not the proper method—syllogism is).
Therefore, if you encounter a proposition with no cause and it is not self-evident, it cannot be proven at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في كيفية تعرف ما ليس لمحموله سبب في موضوعه وفي الاستقراء والتجربة وموجبهما ثم لقائل أن يقول إنه إذا لم يكن بين المحمول والموضوع سبب في نفس الوجود فكيف نبين النسبة بينهما ببيان؟ فنقول إذا كان ذلك بينا بنفسه فلا حاجة إلى البيان بل يحصل فيه التعين من جهة أن نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع لذات الموضوع فذات الموضوع تجب مواصلتها للمحمول؛ وقد وجبت المواصلة فيكون العلم الحاصل يقينيًا. وإن لم يكن بينا بنفسه فلا يمكن البتة أن يقع به يقين غير زائل لأنه إذا جعل الوسط ما ليس بسبب لم يمكن أن يطلب به العلم اليقيني. وإذا جعل ما هو سبب كان الوسط سببا؛ وكلامنا فيما ليس له سبب أصلا فسببه أن تكون أمثال هذه بينة بنفسها أو بالاستقراء؛ غير أنه إذا بين بالاستقراء فلا يخلو من أن يكون وجود نسبة المحمول إلى جزئيات الموضوع بينا بنفسه بلا سبب إذن إنما يبين بالاستقراء بهذا النوع أو يكون وجود نسبة المحمول إلى جزئيات الموضوع في نفسه بسبب، فإن كان الأول فإما أن يكون البيان بالحس فقط ؛ وذلك لا يوجب الدوام ولا رفع إمكان الزوال. وإما أن يكون بالعقل؛ وهذا غير جائز ؛ إذ المحمول لا يمكن أن يكون ذاتيا مقوما؛ إذ المقوم غير مطلوب في الحقيقة لما بينه من بعد بل وجوده لما هو ذاتي له بين . وإن كان عرضيّاً كان ذلك من الأعراض اللازمة لكلي يقال على الجزئيات فيكون هذا العرض لازما لشيء من المعاني الذاتية للجزئيات؛ إذ العرض الذي هذا صفته هذا شأنه. وإذا كان كذلك كان حمله على جزئي لأجل معنى موجود له ولغيره من الذاتيات فيكون ذلك سببًا عاما لوجود هذا العرض في الجزئيات؛ وكلامنا فيما لا سبب له. وإذا عُلم من غير جهة ذلك السبب لم يكن ذلك العلم يقينيا، ولا يمكن أن يكون عرضًا للمعنى العام حتى يصح أن يكون مطلوبًا لكنه ذاتي لكل واحد من الجزئيات عن آخرها. فإن الذاتي لجميع الجزئيات لا يصح أن يكون عرضيا للمعنى الكلي المساوي لها؛ لأنه ليس شيء من موضوعات ذلك الكلي يعرض له ذلك الحمل سلبه أو إيجابه. وإذا لم يكن عارضًا لشيء منها فكيف يكون عارضًا لكلها وعارض طبيعة الكلي عارض للكل على ما عرف. وأما إن كان حال المحمول عند جزئيات الموضوع غير بين بنفسه بل يمكن أن يبين ببيان فذلك البيان إما أن لا يوجب كل واحد منهما اليقين الحقيقي الذي نقصده؛ فكيف يوضع ما ليس يقينيا اليقين الحقيقي الذي بعده وإما أن يوجب فيجب أن يتفق في السبب كما مرَّ فيكون وجود السبب للمعنى الكلي أولا . وإذا لم يكن السبب نافعا في المعنى الكلي فلا يكون نافعاً في الجزئي وإن كان نافعا في الكلي، فالنافع هو القياس لا الاستقراء. وأما أن يكون لا سبب هناك البتة فيكون إما بينا بنفسه، وإما بالاستقراء؛ والكلام فيهما قد مرَّ ؛ فظهر أن ما لا سبب لنسبة محموله إلى موضوعه فإما أن يكون بينا بنفسه، وإما أن لا يمكن بيانه.
Chapter: On how to know what has no cause for its predicate in its subject, and on induction and experience and what they necessitate. Then an objector may say: if there is no cause between the predicate and the subject in existence itself, how can we demonstrate the relation between them by a proof? We say: if that is self-evident, there is no need for proof; rather, determination occurs because the relation of the predicate to the subject is due to the essence of the subject, so the essence of the subject necessitates its connection to the predicate; and since the connection is necessitated, the knowledge obtained is certain. If it is not self-evident, it is absolutely impossible for unshakable certainty to occur through it, because if one takes as middle term what is not a cause, it cannot be used to seek certain knowledge. And if one takes what is a cause, the middle term is a cause; but our discourse is about what has no cause at all. So its cause is that such things are either self-evident or [known] by induction. However, if it is proven by induction, it cannot be free from either: the existence of the relation of the predicate to the particulars of the subject is self-evident without a cause—thus it is only proven by induction in this way—or the existence of the relation of the predicate to the particulars of the subject is in itself due to a cause. If it is the first, then the proof is either only through sense, but that does not necessitate permanence nor remove the possibility of change; or through intellect, but this is not permissible, because the predicate cannot be essential and constitutive, since the constitutive is not sought in reality due to its being evident, rather its existence is evident for what is essential to it. If it is accidental, then it is among the necessary accidents of a universal predicated of particulars, so this accident is necessary for something among the essential meanings of the particulars, for the accident that has this description has this property. If so, its predication of a particular is due to a meaning existing in it and in other essential meanings, so that is a general cause for the existence of this accident in the particulars; but our discourse is about what has no cause. If it is known other than through that cause, that knowledge is not certain. And it cannot be an accident of the general meaning so as to be sought, but it is essential for each of the particulars one after another. For what is essential to all particulars cannot be accidental to the universal meaning coextensive with them, because none of the subjects of that universal have that predication, whether negative or positive, occurring to them. And if it is not an accident to any of them, how can it be an accident to all? And the accident to the nature of the universal is an accident to all, as known. As for if the state of the predicate with respect to the particulars of the subject is not self-evident but can be proven by a proof, then that proof either does not necessitate each of them with the true certainty we intend—so how can something non-certain be used for the true certainty that follows?—or it does necessitate, and then it must agree in cause as previously, so the existence of the cause for the universal meaning comes first. If the cause is not useful for the universal meaning, it is not useful for the particular; but if it is useful for the universal, then what is useful is the syllogism, not induction. But if there is no cause at all, then it is either self-evident or [known] by induction; the discussion of these has passed. So it appears that what has no cause for the relation of its predicate to its subject is either self-evident or cannot be proven.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological investigation into how we can have certain knowledge about propositions where the predicate has no causal connection (sabab) to the subject. The author opens by addressing a potential objection: if there is no cause linking predicate and subject in existence, how can we demonstrate the relation? The answer distinguishes two cases. First, if the relation is self-evident (bayan bi-nafsihi), then no proof is needed; the relation is necessitated by the subject's essence, yielding certainty. Second, if it is not self-evident, then certainty is impossible, because any middle term would either be a non-cause (useless) or a cause (contradicting the assumption of no cause). The author then turns to induction (istiqra'). Induction can only work if the relation of the predicate to the particulars is either self-evident (then induction merely collects what is already known) or caused. If self-evident, it is either by sense (which does not guarantee permanence or eliminate possibility of change) or by intellect (not possible for a predicate that is not essential). If the predicate is accidental (aradi), it must be a necessary accident of a universal, and then its predication of a particular is due to a meaning that is a cause, contrary to the assumption. Therefore, for propositions with no cause, the only possibilities are self-evidence or impossibility of proof. All examples used in the commentary must come from the Islamic sciences. For instance, the proposition 'every human is rational' is self-evident—its predicate is essential. Induction, like 'all intoxicants are forbidden' based on observing specific intoxicants, is limited because sense observation does not guarantee permanence.

Key Terms
استقراء
عرضي
ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does sense-based induction (al-bayan bi al-hiss) not produce lasting certainty?
A) Because the senses are fallible and can be deceived.
B) Because it does not guarantee permanence (al-dawam) nor remove the possibility of change (imkan al-zawal).
C) Because it only applies to essential predicates.
D) Because it requires a logical syllogism to be valid.

Key Takeaway
Induction without a causal connection can only produce probable knowledge, not certainty, because sense observation is changeable and non-essential predicates require a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Collect observations of a property (e.g., that prayer (salat) prevents lewdness) and assess whether induction from a few examples can yield certainty according to the passage's criteria.
?
Explain why, according to the passage, induction cannot prove that all coffee (a new substance) is halal or haram without a known cause (sabab) linking it to a ruling.

Sensation as the Foundation of Knowledge
Author explains that all knowledge begins with sensation and that intellection processes sensory data into universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the epistemological principle that sensation is the origin of all knowledge and explain its scope using examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Sensation provides the raw material for all knowledge, even the most abstract.

The author begins with a powerful claim: if you lose a sense, you lose the knowledge that depends on it. This establishes sensation as the indispensable starting point for human knowledge. Understanding this principle is crucial for grasping how Islamic scholars viewed the relationship between sensory experience and intellectual concepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core epistemological claim
Find the statement 'Whoever loses a sense loses knowledge' and understand its meaning: it applies only to knowledge that depends specifically on that sense.
2
Recognize the scope of the claim
Note that the author specifies 'knowledge whose existence depends on that sense' – not all knowledge, only that which requires sensory input.
3
Examine the examples
Look at the mathematical and natural examples: mathematical premises about the earth being in the center, natural premises about earth being heavy and fire light. These are known through sensory observation.
4
Connect to Islamic sciences
Think of a parallel example from Kalam: the knowledge of the temporality of the world (huduth) depends on sensory observation of change. Without sensation, the minor premise 'The world changes' could not be known.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في بيان كيفية انتفاع النفس بالحس من المعاني وكيف تكتسب في المعقولات وبيان المفردات من المعاني وكيف تكتسب وفي التركيب الأول وكيف ينتهي إليه تحليل القياسات. قيل : إن من فَقَدَ حسّا فقدَ علمًا أي العلم الذي يفتقر وجوده إلى ذلك الحس؛ وإنه من الضروريات إذا افتقر وجود علم ما إلى ذلك الحس. فنقول: إن المبادئ التي منها نتوصل إلى العلم اليقيني برهان واستقراء أي الاستقراء الذاتي؛ والاستقراء يستند إلى الحس. وكذلك البرهان فمقدماته ومبادئها؛ فإن الكل منها إنما يحصل بالحس، وبأن يكتسب بتوسطه خيالات المفردات لتتصرف فيها القوة العقلية تصرفًا تكتسب به الأمور الكلية مفردة، وتركبها على هيئة القول. وإن رام أحد أن يوضحها لمن يذهل عنها لم يمكنه إلا بالاستقراء لأنها أوائل؛ ولا برهان عليها مثل أن المقدمات الرياضية الموجودة في بيان أن الأرض في الوسط؛ والمقدّمات الطبيعية المأخوذة في بيان أن الأرض ثقيلة والنار خفيفة. وكذلك فإن أوائل العوارض الذاتية لكل واحد من الموضوعات فإنما يعرف بالحس أولا ثم يكتسب من المحسوس معقول آخر مثل المثلث والسطح وغير ذلك في علم الهندسة مفارقة كان أو غير مفارقة. فإن الوصول إليها أولا بالحس، فهذا قول مجمل قيل في التعليم الأول (۱) ، فلا بد من التعرُّض له على سبيل التفصيل. فنقول: يجب أن تعلم أن شيئًا من المعقول لا يمكن أن يكون محسوسا، ولا من المحسوس أن يكون معقولاً. وإن كان الحس مبدأ ما يحصل كثير من المعقولات، فالإنسان المحسوس مثلا يناله الحس بقدر ما من العِظَمِ وهيئة ما من الكيفية ووضع ما في أجزاء أعضائه ووضع له في مكانه. وكذلك مثال هذه (۱) أنا لوطيقا الثانية لأرسطو طاليس، ص ٤٩٥ .
Chapter: On explaining how the soul benefits from sensation regarding meanings, and how it acquires intelligibles, and explaining the simple meanings and how they are acquired, and the first composition and how the analysis of syllogisms ends in it. It is said: Whoever loses a sense loses knowledge, i.e., knowledge whose existence depends on that sense; and it is among necessities if the existence of some knowledge depends on that sense. We say: The principles from which we reach certain knowledge are demonstration and induction, i.e., essential induction; and induction relies on sensation. Likewise demonstration, its premises and their principles; for all of them are obtained only by sensation, and by acquiring through its mediation the images of simple things so that the intellectual power can act upon them in a way that acquires universal things as simple, and combines them in the form of speech. And if someone tries to clarify them to one who is heedless of them, he cannot except by induction because they are first principles; and there is no demonstration for them, such as the mathematical premises used to show that the earth is in the center, and the natural premises taken to show that the earth is heavy and fire is light. Likewise the first principles of essential attributes for each subject are known first by sensation, then another intelligible is acquired from the sensible, such as triangle, surface, etc., in geometry, whether separate or not. For reaching them is first by sensation. This is a summary statement given in the First Teaching, so it must be addressed in detail. We say: You must know that nothing of the intelligible can be sensible, nor of the sensible can be intelligible. And although sensation is the beginning from which many intelligibles are obtained, for example, the sensible man is attained by sensation in terms of some magnitude, some qualitative shape, some position of his limbs, and some location in his place. And likewise the example of this (1) The Second Analytics of Aristotle, p. 495.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the role of sensation in acquiring knowledge, specifically focusing on how the soul obtains intelligible concepts. The author begins by stating a principle: if a person lacks a sense, he lacks the knowledge that depends on that sense. He then explains that the principles leading to certain knowledge are demonstration (burhan) and essential induction (istiqra' dhati), both of which rely on sensation. Even demonstration's premises ultimately originate from sensation, because the intellect first receives sensory images of simple things, then abstracts universals from them and combines them into propositions. The author notes that these first principles cannot be explained except through induction, as they are primary and undemonstrable. He gives examples from mathematics and natural philosophy. Then he clarifies that while intelligibles originate from sensation, they are not themselves sensible; the intellect processes sensory information to arrive at universal concepts. For instance, the sensible human is perceived with particular attributes, but the intelligible 'human' is abstract. The passage underscores the dependence of human knowledge on sensory experience, consistent with Aristotelian epistemology.

Key Terms
المعقول
المحسوس
الاستقراء
المبادئ

Checkpoint
What does the author mean when he says 'Whoever loses a sense loses knowledge'?
A) All knowledge is lost
B) Only knowledge dependent on that sense is lost
C) Only empirical knowledge is lost
D) Only theoretical knowledge is lost

Key Takeaway
Sensation is the gateway to knowledge; without it, the premises of demonstration and induction cannot be obtained.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Provide a real example from Usul al-Fiqh where a ruling depends on sensory evidence, and explain why the ruling would be impossible without that sense.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq

Two Paths to Certainty: Demonstration and Induction
Author explains that all knowledge begins with sensation and that intellection processes sensory data into universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how burhan (demonstration) and istiqra' (essential induction) both depend on sensation, and identify their roles in acquiring certain knowledge.

Core Idea
Demonstration and induction both rely on sensation for their starting points.

The author identifies two methods for reaching certain knowledge: demonstration and essential induction. Both are grounded in sensation. Understanding this dual reliance is key to appreciating how Islamic scholars validated their arguments.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish demonstration from induction
Demonstration (burhan) is a syllogism yielding certain conclusions from known premises. Induction (istiqra') gathers universal principles by enumerating sensible particulars. The author calls it 'essential induction' (istiqra' dhati).
2
Identify the role of sensation in induction
Induction depends on sensation because it requires observing multiple particular instances. For example, inductively establishing that 'all intoxicants are forbidden' requires sensing the effect of different intoxicants.
3
Identify the role of sensation in demonstration
Demonstration's premises ultimately trace back to sensation. The author says, 'all of them are obtained only by sensation'. Even mathematical premises like 'the earth is at the center' are based on sensory observations.
4
Apply to Islamic science
In Usul al-Fiqh, the principle 'every intoxicant is forbidden' is known by induction from many instances of intoxication. This universal then serves as a major premise in demonstration to derive rulings for new cases.

Chapter: On explaining how the soul benefits from sensation regarding meanings, and how it acquires intelligibles, and explaining the simple meanings and how they are acquired, and the first composition and how the analysis of syllogisms ends in it. It is said: Whoever loses a sense loses knowledge, i.e., knowledge whose existence depends on that sense; and it is among necessities if the existence of some knowledge depends on that sense. We say: The principles from which we reach certain knowledge are demonstration and induction, i.e., essential induction; and induction relies on sensation. Likewise demonstration, its premises and their principles; for all of them are obtained only by sensation, and by acquiring through its mediation the images of simple things so that the intellectual power can act upon them in a way that acquires universal things as simple, and combines them in the form of speech. And if someone tries to clarify them to one who is heedless of them, he cannot except by induction because they are first principles; and there is no demonstration for them, such as the mathematical premises used to show that the earth is in the center, and the natural premises taken to show that the earth is heavy and fire is light. Likewise the first principles of essential attributes for each subject are known first by sensation, then another intelligible is acquired from the sensible, such as triangle, surface, etc., in geometry, whether separate or not. For reaching them is first by sensation. This is a summary statement given in the First Teaching, so it must be addressed in detail. And although sensation is the beginning from which many intelligibles are obtained, for example, the sensible man is attained by sensation in terms of some magnitude, some qualitative shape, some position of his limbs, and some location in his place. And likewise the example of this (1) The Second Analytics of Aristotle, p. 495.

The passage underscores the dependence of human knowledge on sensory experience, consistent with Aristotelian epistemology.

From Sensible to Intelligible: The Abstraction Process
Author explains that all knowledge begins with sensation and that intellection processes sensory data into universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the process by which the intellect abstracts universal intelligibles from sensible particulars, and distinguish between the sensible and the intelligible.

Core Idea
Intelligibles are not sensible; the intellect abstracts universals from sensory images.

The author emphasizes that although sensation is the source, the intelligible concept is not itself sensible. The intellect takes the sensory image, strips away particularities, and produces a universal. This abstraction process is central to understanding how we form concepts like 'human', 'triangle', etc.

Guided Path
1
Contrast sensible vs intelligible
Note the author's statement: 'nothing of the intelligible can be sensible, nor of the sensible can be intelligible'. Sensible man has magnitude, shape, position; the intelligible 'man' is universal and abstract.
2
Understand the abstraction mechanism
The intellectual power (al-quwwa al-‘aqliyya) acts upon the sensory images (khayalat al-mufradat) to 'acquire universal things as simple'. This is the process of abstraction.
3
Apply to geometry
The concept of triangle is abstracted from many particular triangular shapes perceived by the senses. The author gives triangle and surface as examples from geometry.
4
Apply to Islamic theology
The concept of 'contingent' (mumkin) is abstracted from observing things that come into existence and pass away. Without sensible observation of change, the universal 'contingent' cannot be formed.

فصل : في بيان كيفية انتفاع النفس بالحس من المعاني وكيف تكتسب في المعقولات وبيان المفردات من المعاني وكيف تكتسب وفي التركيب الأول وكيف ينتهي إليه تحليل القياسات. قيل : إن من فَقَدَ حسّا فقدَ علمًا أي العلم الذي يفتقر وجوده إلى ذلك الحس؛ وإنه من الضروريات إذا افتقر وجود علم ما إلى ذلك الحس. فنقول: إن المبادئ التي منها نتوصل إلى العلم اليقيني برهان واستقراء أي الاستقراء الذاتي؛ والاستقراء يستند إلى الحس. وكذلك البرهان فمقدماته ومبادئها؛ فإن الكل منها إنما يحصل بالحس، وبأن يكتسب بتوسطه خيالات المفردات لتتصرف فيها القوة العقلية تصرفًا تكتسب به الأمور الكلية مفردة، وتركبها على هيئة القول. وإن رام أحد أن يوضحها لمن يذهل عنها لم يمكنه إلا بالاستقراء لأنها أوائل؛ ولا برهان عليها مثل أن المقدمات الرياضية الموجودة في بيان أن الأرض في الوسط؛ والمقدّمات الطبيعية المأخوذة في بيان أن الأرض ثقيلة والنار خفيفة. وكذلك فإن أوائل العوارض الذاتية لكل واحد من الموضوعات فإنما يعرف بالحس أولا ثم يكتسب من المحسوس معقول آخر مثل المثلث والسطح وغير ذلك في علم الهندسة مفارقة كان أو غير مفارقة. فإن الوصول إليها أولا بالحس، فهذا قول مجمل قيل في التعليم الأول (۱) ، فلا بد من التعرُّض له على سبيل التفصيل. فنقول: يجب أن تعلم أن شيئًا من المعقول لا يمكن أن يكون محسوسا، ولا من المحسوس أن يكون معقولاً. وإن كان الحس مبدأ ما يحصل كثير من المعقولات، فالإنسان المحسوس مثلا يناله الحس بقدر ما من العِظَمِ وهيئة ما من الكيفية ووضع ما في أجزاء أعضائه ووضع له في مكانه. وكذلك مثال هذه (۱) أنا لوطيقا الثانية لأرسطو طاليس، ص ٤٩٥ .
Chapter: On explaining how the soul benefits from sensation regarding meanings, and how it acquires intelligibles, and explaining the simple meanings and how they are acquired, and the first composition and how the analysis of syllogisms ends in it. It is said: Whoever loses a sense loses knowledge, i.e., knowledge whose existence depends on that sense; and it is among necessities if the existence of some knowledge depends on that sense. We say: The principles from which we reach certain knowledge are demonstration and induction, i.e., essential induction; and induction relies on sensation. Likewise demonstration, its premises and their principles; for all of them are obtained only by sensation, and by acquiring through its mediation the images of simple things so that the intellectual power can act upon them in a way that acquires universal things as simple, and combines them in the form of speech. And if someone tries to clarify them to one who is heedless of them, he cannot except by induction because they are first principles; and there is no demonstration for them, such as the mathematical premises used to show that the earth is in the center, and the natural premises taken to show that the earth is heavy and fire is light. Likewise the first principles of essential attributes for each subject are known first by sensation, then another intelligible is acquired from the sensible, such as triangle, surface, etc., in geometry, whether separate or not. For reaching them is first by sensation. This is a summary statement given in the First Teaching, so it must be addressed in detail. We say: You must know that nothing of the intelligible can be sensible, nor of the sensible can be intelligible. And although sensation is the beginning from which many intelligibles are obtained, for example, the sensible man is attained by sensation in terms of some magnitude, some qualitative shape, some position of his limbs, and some location in his place. And likewise the example of this (1) The Second Analytics of Aristotle, p. 495.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the role of sensation in acquiring knowledge, specifically focusing on how the soul obtains intelligible concepts. The author begins by stating a principle: if a person lacks a sense, he lacks the knowledge that depends on that sense. He then explains that the principles leading to certain knowledge are demonstration (burhan) and essential induction (istiqra' dhati), both of which rely on sensation. Even demonstration's premises ultimately originate from sensation, because the intellect first receives sensory images of simple things, then abstracts universals from them and combines them into propositions. The author notes that these first principles cannot be explained except through induction, as they are primary and undemonstrable. He gives examples from mathematics and natural philosophy. Then he clarifies that while intelligibles originate from sensation, they are not themselves sensible; the intellect processes sensory information to arrive at universal concepts. For instance, the sensible human is perceived with particular attributes, but the intelligible 'human' is abstract. The passage underscores the dependence of human knowledge on sensory experience, consistent with Aristotelian epistemology.

Sense as the Foundation of Knowledge
Author explains the necessity of sense for knowledge and the roles of induction and demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how sense perception and induction provide the basis for certain knowledge, using examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Loss of a sense means loss of knowledge dependent on that sense.

This lesson explores the opening principle of the passage: that sense perception is a necessary condition for certain knowledge. We will examine how induction and even demonstration ultimately rest on sensory data, and how first principles are known through induction, not demonstration. This is crucial for understanding the epistemological foundations of Islamic logic and sciences like kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle about sense loss
Find the statement: 'Whoever loses a sense loses knowledge.' Understand that this means knowledge of things perceived by that sense is inaccessible without it.
2
Relate the principle to induction and demonstration
The author says the principles of certain knowledge are demonstration and essential induction. Induction depends on sense. Demonstrate this by noting that even demonstration's premises ultimately come from sense.
3
Recognize the role of induction for first principles
First principles cannot be demonstrated; they are known by induction from sensory examples. The author gives mathematical and natural science examples (e.g., earth at center, heavy/light).
In fiqh, the prohibition of khamr is known by revelation, but the concept of 'intoxicant' is known by induction from specific cases.
