فنقول: يجب أن تعلم أن شيئًا من المعقول لا يمكن أن يكون محسوسا، ولا من المحسوس أن يكون معقولاً. وإن كان الحس مبدأ ما يحصل كثير من المعقولات، فالإنسان المحسوس مثلا يناله الحس بقدر ما من العِظَمِ وهيئة ما من الكيفية ووضع ما في أجزاء أعضائه ووضع له في مكانه. وكذلك مثال هذه الأحوال في عضو عضو منه فلا يخلو من أن يكون هذا الذي يناله الحس هو الإنسان المعقول، أو الإنسان المعقول غير هذا وإن كان يلازمه. ثم من اليقين أن الإنسان المعقول مشترك فيه على السواء فزيد عند العقل إنسان مثل عمرو وعُمرَ، وكذلك مثل بكر وذلك بالتواطؤ (۱) المطلق. والمحسوس لا يمكن أن يكون كذلك أي محسوس كان فإذن الإنسان المعقول غير ما يكون في الخيال من الإنسان المحسوس ألا ترى؟ أن الإنسان المعقول يجب أن يكون مجردًا عن شريطة تلحقه من خارج لكنه طبيعة معقولة متهيأة لأن يعرض لها من العوارض التي مرَّ ذكرها. وتلك العوارض إنما تلحق الإنسان من جهة مادته ٣٠٤ / التي تختص به؛ والإنسان الذي تصوره في العقل هو مجرد بتجريد العقل عن المادة ولواحقها.
ثم نقول: إن الموجودات قسمان: [1] معقولة الذوات في الوجود، [ب] ومحسوسة الذوات في الوجود. أما الأول فهي الذي لا مادة لها ولا لواحق مادة؛ وإنما هي معقولة بذاتها. وأما الثاني فذواتها في الوجود غير معقولة بل محسوسة لكن العقل يجعلها بحيث تصير معقولة لأنه تجرد حقيقتها عن لواحق المادة؛ ونقول إنه إنما يكتسب تصور المعقولات بتوسط الحس على وجه واحد وهو أن الحس يأخذ صور المحسوسات ويسلمها إلى القوة الخيالية فتصير تلك الصور موضوعات لفعل العقل النظري؛ فتكون هناك صور كثيرة متخالفة بعوارض مثل ما يجد زيدًا مختصا بكذا وعمروا مختصا بكذا: فيقبل على هذه العوارض فينزعها كأنه يعشر هذه العوارض عنها ويطرحها من جانب حتى يتوصل إلى المعاني التي تشترك فيه ولا تختلف؛ فيحصلها ويتصورها.
Chapter: On explaining how the soul benefits from the sense in [acquiring] meanings and how it acquires intelligibles, and on explaining the simple [expressions] of meanings and how they are acquired, and on the first composition and how the analysis of syllogisms ends in it.
It is said: Whoever loses a sense loses knowledge, i.e., knowledge whose existence depends on that sense; and it is among necessities if the existence of some knowledge depends on that sense. We say: The principles by which we arrive at certain knowledge are demonstration and induction, i.e., essential induction; and induction depends on sense. Likewise demonstration: its premises and their principles; for all of them are only obtained by sense, and by acquiring through it the images of simple [expressions] so that the intellective power may act upon them in a way that acquires universal matters as simple, and compose them in the form of a statement. If anyone tries to clarify them to one who is heedless of them, he cannot do so except by induction because they are first principles; there is no demonstration for them, such as the mathematical premises found in explaining that the earth is at the center, and the natural premises taken in explaining that the earth is heavy and fire is light. Likewise, the first of the essential accidents for each subject are known first by sense, then another intelligible is acquired from the sensible, such as triangle, surface, and other things in geometry, whether separate or non-separate. For reaching them is first by sense. This is a summary statement said in the First Teaching, so it must be addressed in detail.
We say: You must know that nothing intelligible can be sensible, nor anything sensible can be intelligible. Even though the sense is a principle for the occurrence of many intelligibles, the sensible human, for example, is perceived by sense in a certain amount of magnitude, a certain qualitative form, a certain position of the parts of his limbs, and a position for him in his place. Likewise, the example of these states in each limb: it cannot be that the one perceived by sense is the intelligible human, or that the intelligible human is other than this even though it accompanies it. Then it is certain that the intelligible human is shared equally, for Zayd in the intellect is human like 'Amr and 'Umar, and similarly Bakr, and that by absolute univocity. The sensible cannot be like that; whatever sensible it is, therefore the intelligible human is other than what is in the imagination of the sensible human. Do you not see that the intelligible human must be abstracted from any condition that attaches to it from outside, but it is an intelligible nature prepared for the accidents that have been mentioned to occur to it? Those accidents only attach to the human from the aspect of his matter which is specific to him; and the human whose form is in the intellect is abstracted by the abstraction of the intellect from matter and its concomitants.
Then we say: The existents are two divisions: [1] those whose essences are intelligible in existence, and [2] those whose essences are sensible in existence. As for the first, they are that which has no matter nor matter's concomitants; they are intelligible by themselves. As for the second, their essences in existence are not intelligible but sensible, yet the intellect makes them such that they become intelligible because it abstracts their reality from matter's concomitants. We say that the conception of intelligibles is only acquired through the sense in one way: the sense takes the forms of sensibles and delivers them to the imaginative faculty, so those forms become subjects for the act of the theoretical intellect. There are many forms differing by accidents, such as you find Zayd specific to such-and-such and 'Amr specific to such-and-such. The intellect attends to these accidents and strips them as if it separates these accidents from them and casts them aside until it arrives at the meanings that are common and not differing; so it obtains them and conceives them.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological exposition on how the human soul acquires intelligible concepts from sensory experience, a key topic in Islamic logic and philosophy. The author begins by citing the principle that loss of a sense leads to loss of knowledge dependent on that sense, establishing sense as a necessary condition for certain knowledge. He then states that the principles of certain knowledge are demonstration (burhan) and essential induction (istiqra' dhati). Induction relies on sense, and even demonstration rests on premises that originate from sense. The author explains that simple images from sense are taken by the intellective power to form universal concepts and compose them into propositions. If one tries to explain first principles to someone oblivious, only induction is possible because they are primary and not demonstrable. Examples from mathematics and natural science illustrate how initial concepts like 'earth at the center' or 'heavy/light' come from sense. The first part concludes that this is a summary of Aristotle's 'First Teaching' (Posterior Analytics) and requires detailed elaboration.

The author then provides the detailed explanation. He asserts a fundamental distinction: nothing intelligible can be sensible, and vice versa. The sensible human is perceived with specific magnitude, form, position, and place, whereas the intelligible human is a universal shared by all individuals like Zayd, 'Amr, and Bakr, univocally. The intelligible is abstracted from all external conditions and matter. Accidents attach only from matter; the intellect abstracts the essence from matter and its concomitants.

Finally, the author divides existents into two categories: those intelligible in themselves (immaterial) and those sensible in themselves (material). The latter become intelligible through intellectual abstraction. The process of acquisition is described: sense takes the forms of sensibles and delivers them to the imagination, which presents them to the theoretical intellect. The intellect then strips away individual accidents (e.g., Zayd's specific attributes) to reach the common invariant meaning, thereby conceiving the universal. This passage builds on Aristotelian epistemology and is foundational for understanding how Islamic logicians approached the problem of universals and concept formation. Examples from fiqh: just as the universal 'intoxicant' is abstracted from particular wines and nabidh, the intellect abstracts the universal 'human' from individual humans.

الاستقراء الذاتي
برهان

Sense perception and induction are the foundation for all certain knowledge, including the premises of demonstration.

The Process of Abstraction: From Sensible to Intelligible
Author details the distinction between sensible and intelligible and the abstraction process.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the process of tajrid (abstraction) by which the intellect forms universal concepts from sensory particulars, using examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
The intelligible is not sensible; it is abstracted from matter and accidents by the intellect.

This lesson delves into the core of the passage: the distinction between the sensible and the intelligible, and the mechanism of abstraction. The author explains that the intelligible human is a universal shared by all individuals, while the sensible human is particular with specific attributes. The intellect strips away accidents to reach the common essence. This process is foundational for understanding how concepts are formed in logic and how rulings are derived in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish sensible from intelligible
Note the author's assertion: nothing intelligible can be sensible, and vice versa. The sensible human is perceived with specific magnitude, form, and position; the intelligible human is a universal shared equally by all individuals (e.g., Zayd, 'Amr, Bakr).
The intelligible is univocal (بالتواطؤ المطلق), meaning it applies in the same sense to all individuals.
2
Understand the role of abstraction (tajrid)
The intellect abstracts the essence from matter and its concomitants. The human in the intellect is 'abstracted by the abstraction of the intellect from matter and its concomitants.'
Tajrid is the act of stripping away material and accidental features to grasp the universal.
3
Follow the three-step process
First, sense takes forms of sensibles and delivers them to imagination. Second, the imagination presents these forms to the theoretical intellect. Third, the intellect strips individual accidents and extracts the common meaning.
Example: from Zayd, 'Amr, and Bakr, the intellect abstracts the universal 'human' by ignoring differences like height, weight, etc.
4
Apply to a fiqh concept
Consider the universal 'intoxicant' (muskir). The sense perceives wine, nabidh, etc., each with specific properties. The intellect abstracts the common property of causing intoxication, ignoring differences in color, taste, etc.
This abstraction is essential for legal analogy (qiyas) in usul al-fiqh.

فصل : في بيان كيفية انتفاع النفس بالحس من المعاني وكيف تكتسب في المعقولات وبيان المفردات من المعاني وكيف تكتسب وفي التركيب الأول وكيف ينتهي إليه تحليل القياسات
قيل : إن من فَقَدَ حسّا فقدَ علمًا أي العلم الذي يفتقر وجوده إلى ذلك الحس؛ وإنه من الضروريات إذا افتقر وجود علم ما إلى ذلك الحس. فنقول: إن المبادئ التي منها نتوصل إلى العلم اليقيني برهان واستقراء أي الاستقراء الذاتي؛ والاستقراء يستند إلى الحس. وكذلك البرهان فمقدماته ومبادئها؛ فإن الكل منها إنما يحصل بالحس، وبأن يكتسب بتوسطه خيالات المفردات لتتصرف فيها القوة العقلية تصرفًا تكتسب به الأمور الكلية مفردة، وتركبها على هيئة القول. وإن رام أحد أن يوضحها لمن يذهل عنها لم يمكنه إلا بالاستقراء لأنها أوائل؛ ولا برهان عليها مثل أن المقدمات الرياضية الموجودة في بيان أن الأرض في الوسط؛ والمقدّمات الطبيعية المأخوذة في بيان أن الأرض ثقيلة والنار خفيفة. وكذلك فإن أوائل العوارض الذاتية لكل واحد من الموضوعات فإنما يعرف بالحس أولا ثم يكتسب من المحسوس معقول آخر مثل المثلث والسطح وغير ذلك في علم الهندسة مفارقة كان أو غير مفارقة. فإن الوصول إليها أولا بالحس، فهذا قول مجمل قيل في التعليم الأول (۱) ، فلا بد من التعرُّض له على سبيل التفصيل.
فنقول: يجب أن تعلم أن شيئًا من المعقول لا يمكن أن يكون محسوسا، ولا من المحسوس أن يكون معقولاً. وإن كان الحس مبدأ ما يحصل كثير من المعقولات، فالإنسان المحسوس مثلا يناله الحس بقدر ما من العِظَمِ وهيئة ما من الكيفية ووضع ما في أجزاء أعضائه ووضع له في مكانه. وكذلك مثال هذه الأحوال في عضو عضو منه فلا يخلو من أن يكون هذا الذي يناله الحس هو الإنسان المعقول، أو الإنسان المعقول غير هذا وإن كان يلازمه. ثم من اليقين أن الإنسان المعقول مشترك فيه على السواء فزيد عند العقل إنسان مثل عمرو وعُمرَ، وكذلك مثل بكر وذلك بالتواطؤ (۱) المطلق. والمحسوس لا يمكن أن يكون كذلك أي محسوس كان فإذن الإنسان المعقول غير ما يكون في الخيال من الإنسان المحسوس ألا ترى؟ أن الإنسان المعقول يجب أن يكون مجردًا عن شريطة تلحقه من خارج لكنه طبيعة معقولة متهيأة لأن يعرض لها من العوارض التي مرَّ ذكرها. وتلك العوارض إنما تلحق الإنسان من جهة مادته ٣٠٤ / التي تختص به؛ والإنسان الذي تصوره في العقل هو مجرد بتجريد العقل عن المادة ولواحقها.
ثم نقول: إن الموجودات قسمان: [1] معقولة الذوات في الوجود، [ب] ومحسوسة الذوات في الوجود. أما الأول فهي الذي لا مادة لها ولا لواحق مادة؛ وإنما هي معقولة بذاتها. وأما الثاني فذواتها في الوجود غير معقولة بل محسوسة لكن العقل يجعلها بحيث تصير معقولة لأنه تجرد حقيقتها عن لواحق المادة؛ ونقول إنه إنما يكتسب تصور المعقولات بتوسط الحس على وجه واحد وهو أن الحس يأخذ صور المحسوسات ويسلمها إلى القوة الخيالية فتصير تلك الصور موضوعات لفعل العقل النظري؛ فتكون هناك صور كثيرة متخالفة بعوارض مثل ما يجد زيدًا مختصا بكذا وعمروا مختصا بكذا: فيقبل على هذه العوارض فينزعها كأنه يعشر هذه العوارض عنها ويطرحها من جانب حتى يتوصل إلى المعاني التي تشترك فيه ولا تختلف؛ فيحصلها ويتصورها.
Chapter: On explaining how the soul benefits from the sense in [acquiring] meanings and how it acquires intelligibles, and on explaining the simple [expressions] of meanings and how they are acquired, and on the first composition and how the analysis of syllogisms ends in it.
It is said: Whoever loses a sense loses knowledge, i.e., knowledge whose existence depends on that sense; and it is among necessities if the existence of some knowledge depends on that sense. We say: The principles by which we arrive at certain knowledge are demonstration and induction, i.e., essential induction; and induction depends on sense. Likewise demonstration: its premises and their principles; for all of them are only obtained by sense, and by acquiring through it the images of simple [expressions] so that the intellective power may act upon them in a way that acquires universal matters as simple, and compose them in the form of a statement. If anyone tries to clarify them to one who is heedless of them, he cannot do so except by induction because they are first principles; there is no demonstration for them, such as the mathematical premises found in explaining that the earth is at the center, and the natural premises taken in explaining that the earth is heavy and fire is light. Likewise, the first of the essential accidents for each subject are known first by sense, then another intelligible is acquired from the sensible, such as triangle, surface, and other things in geometry, whether separate or non-separate. For reaching them is first by sense. This is a summary statement said in the First Teaching, so it must be addressed in detail.
We say: You must know that nothing intelligible can be sensible, nor anything sensible can be intelligible. Even though the sense is a principle for the occurrence of many intelligibles, the sensible human, for example, is perceived by sense in a certain amount of magnitude, a certain qualitative form, a certain position of the parts of his limbs, and a position for him in his place. Likewise, the example of these states in each limb: it cannot be that the one perceived by sense is the intelligible human, or that the intelligible human is other than this even though it accompanies it. Then it is certain that the intelligible human is shared equally, for Zayd in the intellect is human like 'Amr and 'Umar, and similarly Bakr, and that by absolute univocity. The sensible cannot be like that; whatever sensible it is, therefore the intelligible human is other than what is in the imagination of the sensible human. Do you not see that the intelligible human must be abstracted from any condition that attaches to it from outside, but it is an intelligible nature prepared for the accidents that have been mentioned to occur to it? Those accidents only attach to the human from the aspect of his matter which is specific to him; and the human whose form is in the intellect is abstracted by the abstraction of the intellect from matter and its concomitants.
Then we say: The existents are two divisions: [1] those whose essences are intelligible in existence, and [2] those whose essences are sensible in existence. As for the first, they are that which has no matter nor matter's concomitants; they are intelligible by themselves. As for the second, their essences in existence are not intelligible but sensible, yet the intellect makes them such that they become intelligible because it abstracts their reality from matter's concomitants. We say that the conception of intelligibles is only acquired through the sense in one way: the sense takes the forms of sensibles and delivers them to the imaginative faculty, so those forms become subjects for the act of the theoretical intellect. There are many forms differing by accidents, such as you find Zayd specific to such-and-such and 'Amr specific to such-and-such. The intellect attends to these accidents and strips them as if it separates these accidents from them and casts them aside until it arrives at the meanings that are common and not differing; so it obtains them and conceives them.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological exposition on how the human soul acquires intelligible concepts from sensory experience, a key topic in Islamic logic and philosophy. The author begins by citing the principle that loss of a sense leads to loss of knowledge dependent on that sense, establishing sense as a necessary condition for certain knowledge. He then states that the principles of certain knowledge are demonstration (burhan) and essential induction (istiqra' dhati). Induction relies on sense, and even demonstration rests on premises that originate from sense. The author explains that simple images from sense are taken by the intellective power to form universal concepts and compose them into propositions. If one tries to explain first principles to someone oblivious, only induction is possible because they are primary and not demonstrable. Examples from mathematics and natural science illustrate how initial concepts like 'earth at the center' or 'heavy/light' come from sense. The first part concludes that this is a summary of Aristotle's 'First Teaching' (Posterior Analytics) and requires detailed elaboration.

The author then provides the detailed explanation. He asserts a fundamental distinction: nothing intelligible can be sensible, and vice versa. The sensible human is perceived with specific magnitude, form, position, and place, whereas the intelligible human is a universal shared by all individuals like Zayd, 'Amr, and Bakr, univocally. The intelligible is abstracted from all external conditions and matter. Accidents attach only from matter; the intellect abstracts the essence from matter and its concomitants.

Finally, the author divides existents into two categories: those intelligible in themselves (immaterial) and those sensible in themselves (material). The latter become intelligible through intellectual abstraction. The process of acquisition is described: sense takes the forms of sensibles and delivers them to the imagination, which presents them to the theoretical intellect. The intellect then strips away individual accidents (e.g., Zayd's specific attributes) to reach the common invariant meaning, thereby conceiving the universal. This passage builds on Aristotelian epistemology and is foundational for understanding how Islamic logicians approached the problem of universals and concept formation. Examples from fiqh: just as the universal 'intoxicant' is abstracted from particular wines and nabidh, the intellect abstracts the universal 'human' from individual humans.

Key Terms
المعقول
المحسوس
التجريد
القوة الخيالية
العقل النظري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the role of the imaginative faculty (al-quwwa al-khayaliyya) in the acquisition of intelligibles?
A) It directly produces universal concepts.
B) It stores sensory forms and presents them to the theoretical intellect.
C) It strips away accidents from the forms.
D) It is not involved; only sense and intellect are needed.

Key Takeaway
The intellect abstracts universal concepts from sensory particulars by stripping away individual accidents, a process essential for concept formation in logic and fiqh.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the three-step process, trace how the universal 'intoxicant' (muskir) is abstracted from particular intoxicating substances. Identify what the intellect strips away and what common meaning remains.
?

The Intelligible Universal vs. the Sensible Particular
Author asks whether the sensible human is the intelligible human.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the intelligible universal from the sensible particular and explain why they are not identical.

Core Idea
The intelligible universal is shared equally by all individuals; the sensible particular is unique and material.

In Islamic logic, a key starting point is the distinction between universal concepts in the intellect and the particular things we perceive with our senses. The author asks: is the sensible human the same as the intelligible human? Through careful reasoning, he shows they are not identical—the intelligible is shared equally and stripped of matter, while the sensible is bound to matter and individuality. This lesson will help you grasp this foundational distinction that underpins much of Islamic epistemology and ontology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Candidates
The author considers two possibilities: the sensible human (what sense perceives) and the intelligible human (the concept in the mind). Find the terms 'الإنسان المعقول' and 'الإنسان المحسوس' in the Arabic.
2
Note the Property of Univocality
The author says the intelligible human is shared equally (بالتواطؤ المطلق) among all individuals like Zayd, Amr, and Bakr. This means the concept applies in exactly the same way to each individual.
Univocality (التواطؤ) contrasts with equivocation (الاشتراك اللفظي).
3
Contrast with the Sensible
The sensible human cannot be shared in this way because each sensible individual is unique, bound by matter and specific accidents. This proves that the two are not identical.
4
Abstract Nature of the Intelligible
The intelligible human must be stripped (مجرد) of every external condition and matter. It is a pure nature that can receive accidents, but as a concept it is free of material attachments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأحوال في عضو عضو منه فلا يخلو من أن يكون هذا الذي يناله الحس هو الإنسان المعقول، أو الإنسان المعقول غير هذا وإن كان يلازمه. ثم من اليقين أن الإنسان المعقول مشترك فيه على السواء فزيد عند العقل إنسان مثل عمرو وعُمرَ، وكذلك مثل بكر وذلك بالتواطؤ المطلق. والمحسوس لا يمكن أن يكون كذلك أي محسوس كان فإذن الإنسان المعقول غير ما يكون في الخيال من الإنسان المحسوس ألا ترى؟ أن الإنسان المعقول يجب أن يكون مجردًا عن شريطة تلحقه من خارج لكنه طبيعة معقولة متهيأة لأن يعرض لها من العوارض التي مرَّ ذكرها. وتلك العوارض إنما تلحق الإنسان من جهة مادته التي تختص به؛ والإنسان الذي تصوره في العقل هو مجرد بتجريد العقل عن المادة ولواحقها.
ثم نقول: إن الموجودات قسمان: معقولة الذوات في الوجود، ومحسوسة الذوات في الوجود. أما الأول فهي الذي لا مادة لها ولا لواحق مادة؛ وإنما هي معقولة بذاتها. وأما الثاني فذواتها في الوجود غير معقولة بل محسوسة لكن العقل يجعلها بحيث تصير معقولة لأنه تجرد حقيقتها عن لواحق المادة؛ ونقول إنه إنما يكتسب تصور المعقولات بتوسط الحس على وجه واحد وهو أن الحس يأخذ صور المحسوسات ويسلمها إلى القوة الخيالية فتصير تلك الصور موضوعات لفعل العقل النظري؛ فتكون هناك صور كثيرة متخالفة بعوارض مثل ما يجد زيدًا مختصا بكذا وعمروا مختصا بكذا: فيقبل على هذه العوارض فينزعها كأنه يعشر هذه العوارض عنها ويطرحها من جانب حتى يتوصل إلى المعاني التي تشترك فيه ولا تختلف؛ فيحصلها ويتصورها.
The states in each limb of it: it is inevitable that what the sense attains is either the intelligible human, or the intelligible human is other than this even if it accompanies it. Then it is certain that the intelligible human is shared equally, so Zayd in the intellect is a human like Amr and like Bakr, and that by absolute univocality. The sensible cannot be like that, whatever sensible it is. So then the intelligible human is other than what is in the imagination of the sensible human. Do you not see? The intelligible human must be stripped of every condition that attaches to it from outside, but it is an intelligible nature prepared for the accidents that have been mentioned to occur to it. Those accidents only attach to the human from the aspect of its matter that is specific to it. The human whose form is in the intellect is stripped by the intellect's abstraction of matter and its attachments.
Then we say: existents are of two kinds: those whose essences are intelligible in existence, and those whose essences are sensible in existence. As for the first, they are that which have no matter nor attachments of matter; they are intelligible by themselves. As for the second, their essences in existence are not intelligible but sensible, but the intellect makes them such that they become intelligible because it abstracts their reality from the attachments of matter. And we say that one only acquires the conception of intelligibles through the mediation of sense in one way: that sense takes the forms of sensibles and delivers them to the imaginative faculty, so those forms become subjects for the action of the theoretical intellect. Then there are many forms diverse by accidents, such as one finds Zayd specific to something and Amr specific to something. So it [the intellect] takes these accidents, strips them, as if it strips these accidents from them and casts them aside until it arrives at the meanings that are shared and do not differ; then it obtains and conceptualizes them.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the intelligible universal and an epistemological explanation of how the intellect abstracts it. The author begins by questioning whether what sense perceives is identical to the intelligible concept (e.g., 'human') or not. He argues that the intelligible 'human' is shared equally among all individuals (Zayd, Amr, Bakr) by univocality, while sensible particulars are unique. Therefore, the intelligible human is distinct from the imaginative image of a sensible human. The author emphasizes that the intelligible human must be free from any external conditions; it is a pure nature that can receive accidents but as a concept is abstracted by the intellect from matter and its attachments. Then the author divides existents into two categories: those whose essences are intelligible in themselves (immaterial, such as angels or perhaps intelligences) and those whose essences are sensible but become intelligible through abstraction. The process of abstraction is described: sense delivers images to the imagination, where many diverse images exist with different accidents. The theoretical intellect then strips away these accidents to arrive at the common meanings shared across individuals. This is the one way the intellect acquires universal concepts via the senses. The argument uses Islamic examples (Zayd, Amr, Bakr) and references the role of the imaginative faculty and theoretical intellect in the epistemic process.

Key Terms
الإنسان المعقول (al-insān al-ma'qūl): The universal concept 'human' that exists in the intellect, shared by all individual humans, and stripped of matter and accidents.
الإنسان المحسوس (al-insān al-mah̬sūs): The particular sensible human individual (e.g., Zayd, Amr) perceived by the senses, bound by matter and specific accidents.
التجريد (al-tajrīd): The act of the intellect stripping a concept of material attachments and accidents to obtain a universal meaning.
المادة (al-mādda): The material substrate from which sensible things are composed and which carries individual accidents.
القوة الخيالية (al-quwwa al-khayāliyya): The imaginative faculty that stores sensible forms and presents them to the theoretical intellect for abstraction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can the intelligible 'human' not be identical to the sensible human?
A) Because the sensible human is material and individual, while the intelligible is shared equally by all.
B) Because the sensible human is in the imagination, while the intelligible is in the external world.
C) Because the intelligible human requires a specific condition, while the sensible does not.
D) Because the sensible human is a genus, while the intelligible is a species.

Key Takeaway
The intelligible universal is abstracted from matter and shared by all individuals, while the sensible particular is unique and material.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether each of the following is an example of the intelligible universal or the sensible particular: (a) your own individual teacher, (b) the concept 'teacher' in the mind. Explain your reasoning using the passage's terms 'univocality' and 'abstraction'.
?

The Epistemological Process of Abstraction (Tajrid)
Author explains how the intellect acquires universals via sense and imagination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the three-step process by which the intellect abstracts universal concepts from sensible particulars, as outlined in the passage.

Core Idea
Abstraction proceeds from sense to imagination to intellect, stripping away accidents to reveal the shared essence.

How do we come to know universal concepts like 'human', 'white', or 'knowledge'? The author explains that it is through a process involving sense perception, imagination, and intellectual abstraction. The senses deliver forms of sensible objects to the imaginative faculty, which stores them with their individual accidents. Then the theoretical intellect strips away these accidental differences to grasp the common, universal meaning. This lesson will walk you through that process, which is fundamental to Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Starting Point: Sense Perception
The author says that sense (الحس) takes the forms of sensibles (صور المحسوسات) and delivers them to the imaginative faculty. This is the first step in acquiring universals.
The senses perceive individual, material objects like Zayd and Amr.
2
The Role of the Imaginative Faculty
The imaginative faculty (القوة الخيالية) stores these forms, which are diverse by accidents (e.g., Zayd's specific height, Amr's specific color). This provides the raw material for abstraction.
3
The Theoretical Intellect's Action
The theoretical intellect (العقل النظري) then examines these stored images, strips away (ينزع) the individual accidents, and extracts the meanings that are shared and do not differ among the individuals.
It 'strips' or 'abstracts' (تجريد) the essence from accidental properties.
4
The Output: Universal Concepts
After stripping the accidents, the intellect obtains and conceptualizes (يحصلها ويتصورها) the universal meanings, such as the intelligible 'human'.
These universals are the basis for definitions and scientific knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأحوال في عضو عضو منه فلا يخلو من أن يكون هذا الذي يناله الحس هو الإنسان المعقول، أو الإنسان المعقول غير هذا وإن كان يلازمه. ثم من اليقين أن الإنسان المعقول مشترك فيه على السواء فزيد عند العقل إنسان مثل عمرو وعُمرَ، وكذلك مثل بكر وذلك بالتواطؤ المطلق. والمحسوس لا يمكن أن يكون كذلك أي محسوس كان فإذن الإنسان المعقول غير ما يكون في الخيال من الإنسان المحسوس ألا ترى؟ أن الإنسان المعقول يجب أن يكون مجردًا عن شريطة تلحقه من خارج لكنه طبيعة معقولة متهيأة لأن يعرض لها من العوارض التي مرَّ ذكرها. وتلك العوارض إنما تلحق الإنسان من جهة مادته التي تختص به؛ والإنسان الذي تصوره في العقل هو مجرد بتجريد العقل عن المادة ولواحقها.

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the intelligible universal and an epistemological explanation of how the intellect abstracts it. The author begins by questioning whether what sense perceives is identical to the intelligible concept (e.g., 'human') or not. He argues that the intelligible 'human' is shared equally among all individuals (Zayd, Amr, Bakr) by univocality, while sensible particulars are unique. Therefore, the intelligible human is distinct from the imaginative image of a sensible human. The author emphasizes that the intelligible human must be free from any external conditions; it is a pure nature that can receive accidents but as a concept is abstracted by the intellect from matter and its attachments. Then the author divides existents into two categories: those whose essences are intelligible in themselves (immaterial, such as angels or perhaps intelligences) and those whose essences are sensible but become intelligible through abstraction. The process of abstraction is described: sense delivers images to the imagination, where many diverse images exist with different accidents. The theoretical intellect then strips away these accidents to arrive at the common meanings shared across individuals. This is the one way the intellect acquires universal concepts via the senses. The argument uses Islamic examples (Zayd, Amr, Bakr) and references the role of the imaginative faculty and theoretical intellect in the epistemic process.

Key Terms
الإنسان المعقول (al-insān al-ma'qūl): The universal concept 'human' that exists in the intellect, shared by all individual humans, and stripped of matter and accidents.
الإنسان المحسوس (al-insān al-mah̬sūs): The particular sensible human individual (e.g., Zayd, Amr) perceived by the senses, bound by matter and specific accidents.
التجريد (al-tajrīd): The act of the intellect stripping a concept of material attachments and accidents to obtain a universal meaning.
المادة (al-mādda): The material substrate from which sensible things are composed and which carries individual accidents.
القوة الخيالية (al-quwwa al-khayāliyya): The imaginative faculty that stores sensible forms and presents them to the theoretical intellect for abstraction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The theoretical intellect
B) The imaginative faculty
C) The practical intellect
D) The sensible faculty

Key Takeaway
Abstraction is the process by which the intellect, starting from sensory images through the imagination, strips away accidental differences to arrive at universal concepts.

The Four Ways Sense Leads to Assent
Author enumerates and explains the four ways sense perception leads to assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish and classify the four modes by which sense perception contributes to certain knowledge (tasdiq).

Core Idea
Sense does not give certainty directly; the intellect uses sense in four distinct ways to achieve assent.

In this passage, the author explains how the raw data from the senses is transformed by the intellect into certain judgments. He identifies four distinct modes: accidental, partial syllogism, induction, and experience. Understanding these modes is crucial for evaluating how knowledge claims are grounded in observation, especially in fields like kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four modes
List the four modes as given: (1) by accident (bi al-'arad), (2) by partial syllogism (al-qiyas al-juz'i), (3) by induction (al-istiqra'), (4) by experience (al-tajriba). Note that these are the ways the intellect uses sensory input to produce assent.
2
Analyze the first mode: by accident
In this mode, sense provides an initial stimulus, but the resulting judgment is either innate (fitri) or demonstrative (burhani). The role of sense is accidental – it triggers the intellect but is not the essential cause of the knowledge.
Example: Seeing a flash of lightning may trigger the innate knowledge that there is a cause, but the certainty comes from the intellect, not the sense alone.
3
Examine partial syllogism
Here, the intellect already possesses a universal judgment about a genus. When it senses individuals of a species under that genus, it applies the universal to that species. This is not a full syllogism but a specific application.
Example: The intellect knows that 'every animal is mortal'. Sensing a particular horse allows applying that judgment to the species 'horse'.
4
Distinguish induction from experience
Induction (istiqra') alerts the intellect to a universal by enumerating particulars, but does not force assent – it is a reminder. Experience (tajriba) involves repeated observation leading to a judgment of essential connection, as when we infer that a property is essential because it persists.
Example: Induction: observing several swans are white alerts to the possibility that all swans are white. Experience: repeatedly observing that scammony purges leads to the certain judgment that purging is essential to scammony.
5
Apply the classification
Consider a real Islamic science example: the proof of the existence of God through the argument from temporal origination (huduth). This argument uses experience of change in the world. Which mode(s) does it rely on?
It primarily relies on the mode of experience (tajriba) combined with demonstrative reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أن هذا الصنع ما هو وكيف هو فذلك من علم النفس لكن الذي يقوله هنا هو أن الحس يؤدي إلى النفس أمورًا مختلطة غير معقولة؛ والعقل يجعلها معقولة؛ فإذا أفردها كان له أن يركبها تركيبا مفهما لمعنى الشيء كالحد والرسم، أو تركيبا جازما بل يقول إن تصديق المعقولات يكتسب بالحس على وجوه أربعة أحدها بالعرض والثاني بالقياس الجزئي، والثالث بالاستقراء، والرابع بالتجربة. [١] أما الأول فهو أن يكتسب من الحس بالوجه الذي قلناه وتتبع ذلك أحكام بعضها بالفطرة وبعضها بالبرهان. والفطري هو باتصال من العقل وبالنور المفاض على الأنفس الطبيعية من العقل الفعّال لكنه وإن كان كذلك فإن الحس مبدأ ما بالعرض لا بالذات والبرهاني بالحد الأوسط وبالقوة ذلك المبدأ . [٢] وأما الثاني منها فهو أن يكون عند العقل حكمها كلي على الجنس فيحس أشخاص نوع لذلك الجنس، ويحمل ذلك الحكم على النوع. [٣] وأما الثالث فكثير من الأوليات لا يتبين للعقل بالطريق المذكور أولا بل إذا استقرأ جزئياته تتنبه على اعتقاد الكلي فيكون الاستقراء منبها عليه لا موجبا مثل أن مساس الشيئين الغير المتماسين لشيء واحد يوجب قسمته لذلك الشيء، فهذا ربَّما لا يكون ثابتا في النفس فكلما يحس بجزئيته تنبه له العقل ومعتقده. [٤] وأما الرابع فكأنه مشتمل على القياس والاستقراء؛ وليست إفادته بالأوليات الصرفة بل بمكتسبات بالحس مثل حكمنا بجذب المغناطيس وإسهال السقمونيا وغير ذلك كما مر في بيان التجربة من قبل، فإنه إذا تكرر كثيرًا حكم العقل أن هذا ذاتي لهذا الشيء؛ وليس اتفاقيًّا عنه، فإن الاتفاقي لا يدوم، فهذه هي الإنجاز لاستفادة العقل علمًا تصديقيًا بسبب من الحس.
As for what this craft is and how it is, that belongs to psychology, but what he says here is that sense delivers to the soul confused and unintelligible things; and the intellect makes them intelligible; so when it isolates them, it has the ability to combine them in a composition that conveys the meaning of the thing, like definition and description, or in a decisive composition. Rather, he says that the assent to intelligibles is acquired through sense in four ways: first by accident, second by partial syllogism, third by induction, and fourth by experience. [1] As for the first, it is that one acquires from sense in the manner we said, and there follow from that judgments, some by innate disposition and some by demonstration. The innate is by connection from the intellect and by the light poured upon natural souls from the active intellect, but even if it is so, sense is a principle in some way by accident, not essentially; and the demonstrative is by the middle term and potentially that principle. [2] As for the second, it is that there is at the intellect a universal judgment about the genus, then it perceives individuals of a species of that genus, and it applies that judgment to the species. [3] As for the third, many primary propositions are not evident to the intellect by the aforementioned method initially, but when it inducts its particulars, it is alerted to belief in the universal, so induction is a reminder, not a necessitator, like the fact that the touching of two non-touching things to one thing necessitates division of that thing – this may not be established in the soul; whenever it senses a particular of it, the intellect and its belief are alerted to it. [4] As for the fourth, it is as if it comprises both syllogism and induction, and its benefit is not from pure primary propositions but from acquisitions through sense, like our judgment that the magnet attracts and scammony purges, and other such things, as was mentioned earlier in the explanation of experience; for when it is repeated many times, the intellect judges that this is essential to this thing, and not accidental to it, because the accidental does not persist. These are the means for the intellect to acquire certain knowledge through sense.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological classification of how sense perception contributes to the acquisition of assent (tasdiq). The author first clarifies that sense presents the soul with confused data; the intellect then organizes it into concepts and judgments. The central claim is that assent to intelligibles is acquired from sense in four distinct ways: by accident, by partial syllogism, by induction, and by experience. The first mode (by accident) occurs when sense provides a starting point, and then judgments follow either innately (fitri) or demonstratively (burhani). The innate judgment arises from the active intellect, but sense is only an accidental principle; the demonstrative judgment uses a middle term. The second mode (partial syllogism) occurs when the intellect already has a universal judgment about a genus, then senses individuals of a species under that genus and applies the universal to that species. The third mode (induction) serves as a reminder: many primary truths are not immediately evident, but by inspecting particulars the intellect becomes aware of the universal; induction does not necessitate but alerts. The example given is that two non-touching things touching one thing forces division of that thing – repeated sensation awakens the intellect to the universal rule. The fourth mode (experience) combines syllogism and induction; it does not rely on pure primaries but on repeated sensory observations, such as the magnet attracting iron or scammony causing purgation. After many repetitions, the intellect judges that the attribute is essential (dhati) to the thing, not accidental, because what is accidental does not persist. This is how the mind achieves certain knowledge via sense.

Key Terms
تصديق (tasdiq): Assent to a proposition; certain knowledge that a judgment is true, as opposed to mere conception (tasawwur).
بالعرض (bi al-'arad): Accidentally; knowledge obtained from sense indirectly, where sense is not the essential cause but a incidental stimulus.
القياس الجزئي (al-qiyas al-juz'i): Partial syllogism; applying a universal judgment about a genus to a species after sensing its individuals. [Note: This term is less common; in later logic it may be subsumed under ordinary syllogism or analogy.]
الاستقراء (al-istiqra'): Induction; enumerating particulars to alert the mind to a universal, but not necessitating it.
التجربة (al-tajriba): Experience; repeated observation leading to a judgment of essential connection, used in medicine and natural sciences.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does induction (istiqra') contribute to knowledge?
A) It necessitates the universal judgment with certainty.
B) It alerts the intellect to the universal without necessitating it.
C) It provides the primary premises for demonstration.
D) It is the same as experience (tajriba).

Key Takeaway
Sense assists the intellect in four ways, but only the fourth mode (experience) can yield certain essential judgments through repeated observation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Distinguishing Knowledge, Opinion, and Ignorance
Author defines knowledge, certainty, opinion, and ignorance, and explains their relationships.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between knowledge (ilm), certainty (yaqin), opinion (zann), and ignorance (jahl) according to the Mantiq Kabir, and determine whether a given belief qualifies as knowledge or opinion based on necessity and possibility.

Core Idea
Knowledge demands impossibility of error; opinion allows for the possibility of the opposite.

This passage from the Mantiq Kabir lays down the foundational epistemological categories that every student of Islamic logic must master: what distinguishes certain knowledge from mere opinion, and how ignorance relates to both. Understanding these distinctions is essential for evaluating the strength of any claim in the Islamic sciences, from theological proofs to juristic rulings.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of knowledge (ilm)
Find the sentence where the author defines assertive knowledge (al-ilm al-tasdiqi). Note that it involves believing something is such, and certainty (yaqin) adds the conviction that it cannot be otherwise, with no possibility of removal.
The author notes that this is not a real definition but an explanation of the term.
2
Identify the three opposing beliefs to knowledge
Look for the list of beliefs that oppose knowledge: (1) belief that the thing is not such, (2) belief that it cannot be such, (3) belief that it can be not such.
The first is pure ignorance; the other two are types of opinion.
3
Distinguish the two types of opinion (zann)
The passage divides opinion based on the nature of the object. For a thing that is necessarily such, believing it can be otherwise yields a true opinion compounded with ignorance. For a contingent thing, allowing the opposite while believing correctly yields true opinion with simple ignorance.
Opinion always includes the possibility of the opposite, unlike knowledge.
4
Classify ignorance (jahl) as simple or composite
Simple ignorance is absence of any opinion; composite ignorance is holding a false belief that opposes knowledge. The author distinguishes these based on whether the false belief is held with preponderance (false opinion) or decisively (composite ignorance).
This classification helps in diagnosing errors in reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في حال العلم والظن وتشاركهما وتباينهما وفي تفهم الذهن والفهم والحدس والذكاء والصناعة والحكمة
من المعلوم أن هنا علما بشيء، وهنا ظنا به، وأن الاختلاف فيهما من جهة الوثاقة والمعلق، وأنهما داخلان تحت الرأي، وأن بينهما موضع مقايسة ومناسبة، وليس كل علم يحسن بقياس بالظن بل العلم التصديقي، ولا كل علم مع كل ظن بل مع ظن يوافقه في جنس الرأي. وإن ما سواه من الظن فيجب أن يقاس بالجهل والعلم التصديقي هو أن يعتقد في الشيء أنه كذا؛ واليقين منه هو أن يعتقد في الشيء أنه كذا، ويعتقد أنه لا يمكن أن لا يكون كذا اعتقادًا لا يمكن زواله والكلام في العلم أنه يكون غنيا عن التعريف قد مرَّ في أول الكتاب، فهذا لا يكون تعريفا في الحقيقة بل هو تفسير اللفظ المستعمل عندهم. ثم إنه إذا كان بينا بنفسه فلا يمكن زواله البتة، وإذا كان بينا بالبرهان فكذلك؛ وإنه هو المكتسب. والذي يخالفه أصناف من الاعتقادات مثل اعتقاد في ذلك الشيء أنه لا يكون كذا بالفعل، واعتقاد أنه لا يمكن أن يكون كذا، واعتقاد يمكن أن لا يكون كذا فالاعتقاد في ذلك الشيء أنه ليس كذا هو جهل مضاد للعلم لا يشاركه لكن اعتقاد أنه يمكن أن لا يكون كذا إما في الموجود الذي هو كذا وليس من شأنه أن لا يكون إلا كذا، أو في الموجود الذي هو كذا وليس من شأنه أن لا يكون كذا؛ وكل واحد من هذين يسمّى ظنّا. والأول منهما هو ظن صادق مركب بجهل مضاد فإن كان الشيء في نفسه كما اعتقده وهو في نفسه ممكن أن لا يكون كذا. واعتقاد أنه على صفة الثبوت والقطع كان ذلك علما لا ظنا. وإن كان الشيء في نفسه كذا ولا يمكن أن لا يكون كذا كان ظناً مركباً بجهل مضاد للعلم. وإن كان الشيء في نفسه كما اعتقده لكن اعتقاده يمكن أن لا يكون كذا هو تجويز من جهة أن الشيء الذي يفرضه كذا عسى أن لا يكون كذا فهو ظن صادق مركب بجهل بسيط هو عدم العلم.
والجهل منه بسيط ومنه مركب، فالبسيط هو أن لا يكون في النفس رأي في أنه كذا، والمركب هو أن لا يكون في النفس الرأي الحق مع حصول رأي باطل مضاد العلم حصولاً قطعياً. فإن كان مع تجويز أنه لا يكون كذا لكن المثل غالب إلى الرأي الباطل فهو الظنُّ الكاذب. والأول يسمى بسيطًا لما أنه ليس فيه إلا عدم الرأي فقط بخلاف الثاني فإنه عدم رأي مع وجود رأي آخر. وكما أن العلم قد يكتسب بتوسط شيء يوقع العلم فكذلك الظنُّ يكتسب بتوسط شيء يوقع الظن. والعلم في الجملة مخالف للظَّن في هيئة العقد وفي الأمور التي العلم أولى بها ولا يمكن أن يجتمع العلم والظن في شيء واحد لشخص واحد. وكذلك الظنُّ الصادق والكاذب لأنهما إذا تساويا كان شكا في الأمرين، وإذا لم يتساويا كان أحدهما ظناً وهو الراجح دون الآخر؛ إذ ذاك وهم بل يمكن أن يكون لشخص علم بشيء ولا جزء ظن بذلك الشيء.
وأما الكلام في الصناعة وغيرها فذلك لا يكون من هذا العلم بل من الطبيعيات الخلقية غير أن التعرُّض لتعريفها في هذا الموضع على وفق الحكمة
Chapter: On the state of knowledge and opinion, their sharing and divergence, and on understanding of the mind, comprehension, intuition, intelligence, craft, and wisdom.
It is known that here there is knowledge of something, and here opinion of it, and that the difference between them is in terms of reliability and connection, and that they both fall under belief, and that there is a place of comparison and suitability between them. Not every knowledge is good to be measured by opinion, but rather assertive knowledge, and not every knowledge with every opinion, but with an opinion that agrees with it in the genus of belief. And what is other than that of opinion must be measured by ignorance. And assertive knowledge is that one believes of a thing that it is such; and certainty from it is that one believes of a thing that it is such, and believes that it cannot be otherwise, a belief that cannot be removed. And the discussion about knowledge being independent of definition has passed in the beginning of the book; so this is not a definition in reality but an explanation of the term used by them. Then, if it is self-evident, it cannot be removed at all, and if it is evident by demonstration, likewise; and it is acquired. And that which opposes it are kinds of beliefs, such as belief in that thing that it is not such actually, and belief that it cannot be such, and belief that it can be not such. So belief in that thing that it is not such is ignorance opposing knowledge, not sharing with it. But the belief that it can be not such is either regarding an existent which is such and it is not of its nature to be but such, or regarding an existent which is such and it is not of its nature not to be such; and each of these is called opinion. The first of them is a true opinion compounded with opposing ignorance. For if the thing is in itself as he believed it and it is in itself possible that it not be such, and he believes it in the manner of affirmation and decisiveness, that would be knowledge, not opinion. And if the thing is in itself such and cannot be otherwise, then it is an opinion compounded with opposing ignorance. And if the thing is in itself as he believed it but his belief is that it can be not such, that is a permission [of the opposite] from the aspect that the thing he assumes to be such perhaps is otherwise, so it is a true opinion compounded with simple ignorance, which is absence of knowledge.
And ignorance: from it is simple and from it composite. The simple is that there is no opinion in the soul about it being such. The composite is that there is no true opinion in the soul, along with the occurrence of a false opinion opposing knowledge, occurring decisively. And if there is with permission that it be not such, but the preponderance is towards the false opinion, then it is false opinion. The first is called simple because there is nothing in it except the absence of opinion only, unlike the second, for it is absence of opinion with the existence of another opinion. And just as knowledge may be acquired by means of something that causes knowledge, so opinion is acquired by means of something that causes opinion. And knowledge in general differs from opinion in the form of the judgment and in matters where knowledge is more appropriate, and knowledge and opinion cannot coexist in one thing for one person. Likewise, true and false opinion, for if they are equal, it would be doubt in the two matters, and if they are not equal, then one of them is opinion, which is the preponderant, not the other; for then it would be illusion. Rather, it is possible for a person to have knowledge of something and no part of opinion concerning that thing.
As for the discussion about craft and other things, that is not from this science but from natural dispositions, except that the exposure of its definition in this place is in accordance with wisdom.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This epistemological passage from Mantiq Kabir 2 systematically distinguishes between knowledge (ilm) and opinion (zann), their types, and their relation to ignorance (jahl). The author begins by stating that both knowledge and opinion fall under belief (ra'y) but differ in reliability. Not every type of knowledge should be compared with opinion; only assertive knowledge (al-ilm al-tasdiqi) is considered. The author then defines assertive knowledge as believing something is the case, and certainty (yaqin) as such a belief combined with the conviction that it cannot be otherwise, a conviction that cannot be removed. Knowledge can be self-evident (bina nafsihi) or acquired through demonstration (bina al-burhan). The author then lists three opposing beliefs: belief that the thing is not so (actual falsehood), belief that it cannot be so (impossibility), and belief that it can be otherwise (possibility). The first is ignorance (jahl) opposing knowledge. The second type (belief that it can be not such) is divided into two cases: (a) concerning a thing that is necessarily such (i.e., its nature requires it), and (b) concerning a thing that is such but could be otherwise. Both are called opinion, but the first is a true opinion compounded with opposing ignorance? Actually the text says: 'The first of them is a true opinion compounded with opposing ignorance.' Then it refines: if the thing is in itself possible but one believes it decisively, that is knowledge; if the thing is necessary but one believes it with possibility, that is a true opinion compounded with ignorance; if one believes a thing as it is but allows the opposite, that is true opinion with simple ignorance. The author then defines simple ignorance as absence of any belief, and composite ignorance as holding a false belief. False opinion occurs when the preponderant belief is false. The passage concludes that knowledge and opinion differ in the form of the judgment and cannot coexist in the same person regarding the same thing. Like knowledge, opinion can be acquired through a cause. The final remark notes that discussion of craft belongs to natural sciences.

Key Terms
العلم
اليقين
الظن
الجهل
التصديقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what distinguishes knowledge (ilm) from opinion (zann) in terms of the believer's conviction?
A) Knowledge requires demonstration, while opinion does not.
B) Knowledge involves the belief that the thing cannot be otherwise, while opinion allows for the possibility of the opposite.
C) Knowledge is always about necessary truths, while opinion is about contingent truths.
D) Opinion is always based on probability, while knowledge is based on certainty.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge requires the belief that a thing cannot be otherwise, while opinion always permits the possibility of the opposite, even if the belief is correct.

Opinion (Zann): A Belief Without Certainty
Author defines opinion and classifies it into true and false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish true opinion from false opinion based on their relation to reality and the presence of certainty.

Core Idea
Opinion is a belief that could be otherwise, even if it matches reality.

The passage opens by describing two types of belief about something that is not necessarily always that way. Each is called opinion (zann). One is a true opinion accompanied by a kind of ignorance, the other is false opinion. Understanding this distinction is fundamental to evaluating the strength of our beliefs in religious matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios for opinion
The passage first gives two scenarios about an existent that is such-and-such. Find these two scenarios. They describe the conditions under which belief qualifies as opinion.
The phrase 'في الموجود الذي هو كذا' introduces the first scenario.
2
Classify each as true or false opinion
The author labels the first scenario as true opinion (zann sadiq) and the second as false opinion (zann kadhib). Notice the difference: in true opinion, the belief matches reality but could be otherwise; in false opinion, the belief does not match reality.
True opinion is sometimes called 'zann sadiq' and false opinion 'zann kadhib'.
3
Identify the type of ignorance in true opinion
The text says the first (true opinion) is composed with 'opposite ignorance' (jahl mudadd) and the second is composed with 'simple ignorance' (jahl basit). Find these phrases and understand what each means for the belief's epistemic status.
'Opposite ignorance' means the believer holds a false view contrary to truth; 'simple ignorance' is mere absence of knowledge.
4
Contrast opinion with knowledge
The passage notes that if the belief is held with certainty (qat') and the thing cannot be otherwise, it becomes knowledge ('ilm), not opinion. This reinforces that the lack of certainty is the core of opinion.
Knowledge requires both the belief being true and the impossibility of it being false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الموجود الذي هو كذا وليس من شأنه أن لا يكون إلا كذا، أو في الموجود الذي هو كذا وليس من شأنه أن لا يكون كذا؛ وكل واحد من هذين يسمّى ظنّا. والأول منهما هو ظن صادق مركب بجهل مضاد فإن كان الشيء في نفسه كما اعتقده وهو في نفسه ممكن أن لا يكون كذا. واعتقاد أنه على صفة الثبوت والقطع كان ذلك علما لا ظنا. وإن كان الشيء في نفسه كذا ولا يمكن أن لا يكون كذا كان ظناً مركباً بجهل مضاد للعلم. وإن كان الشيء في نفسه كما اعتقده لكن اعتقاده «أ» يمكن أن لا يكون كذا هو تجويز من جهة أن الشيء الذي يفرضه كذا عسى أن لا يكون كذا فهو ظن صادق مركب بجهل بسيط هو عدم العلم. والجهل منه بسيط ومنه مركب، فالبسيط هو أن لا يكون في النفس رأي في أنه كذا، والمركب هو أن لا يكون في النفس الرأي الحق مع حصول رأي باطل مضاد العلم حصولاً قطعياً. فإن كان مع تجويز أنه لا يكون كذا لكن المثل غالب إلى الرأي الباطل فهو الظنُّ الكاذب. والأول يسمى بسيطًا لما أنه ليس فيه إلا عدم الرأي فقط بخلاف الثاني فإنه عدم رأي مع وجود رأي آخر. وكما أن العلم قد يكتسب بتوسط شيء يوقع العلم فكذلك الظنُّ يكتسب بتوسط شيء يوقع الظن. والعلم في الجملة مخالف للظَّن في هيئة العقد وفي الأمور التي العلم أولى بها ولا يمكن أن يجتمع العلم والظن في شيء واحد لشخص واحد. وكذلك الظنُّ الصادق والكاذب لأنهما إذا تساويا كان شكا في الأمرين، وإذا لم يتساويا كان أحدهما ظناً وهو الراجح دون الآخر؛ إذ ذاك وهم بل يمكن أن يكون لشخص علم بشيء ولا جزء ظن بذلك الشيء.
Regarding the existent that is such-and-such and it is not in its nature that it not be except such-and-such, or regarding the existent that is such-and-such and it is not in its nature that it not be such-and-such; each of these two is called opinion. The first of them is a true opinion composed with opposite ignorance, if the thing in itself is as he believed it and it is in itself possible that it not be such. And believing it with affirmation and certainty, that would be knowledge not opinion. If the thing in itself is such and cannot not be such, it is an opinion composed with ignorance opposite to knowledge. If the thing in itself is as he believed it, but his belief that it can not be such is a permission from the aspect that the thing he supposes such might not be such, then it is a true opinion composed with simple ignorance, which is absence of knowledge. Ignorance is simple and compound: simple is that there is no view in the soul that it is such; compound is that there is no true view in the soul with the occurrence of a false view opposite to knowledge in a definitive manner. If with the permission that it not be such, but the inclination is strong towards the false view, it is false opinion. The first is called simple because it contains only absence of view, unlike the second which is absence of view with existence of another view. Just as knowledge can be acquired by means of something that produces knowledge, similarly opinion is acquired by means of something that produces opinion. Knowledge in general is different from opinion in the form of belief and in matters where knowledge is more appropriate, and knowledge and opinion cannot coexist in one thing for one person. Similarly, true and false opinion cannot coexist, because if they are equal, it is doubt about the two matters, and if they are not equal, one of them is opinion—the predominant one—and the other not; that would be illusion, but it is possible for a person to have knowledge of something and no part of opinion about that thing.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage is a definitional analysis of opinion (zann) and ignorance (jahl). The author begins by describing two types of belief about a thing that is not necessarily always that way: one is true opinion composed with opposite ignorance, and the other is false opinion. He then explains that if the belief is held with certainty and the thing cannot be otherwise, it is knowledge, not opinion. He distinguishes simple ignorance (lack of belief) from compound ignorance (holding a false belief contrary to truth). He also notes that knowledge and opinion cannot coexist about the same thing for the same person. True and false opinions also cannot coexist; if they are equal, it is doubt; if one outweighs, it is opinion, and the lesser is called illusion (wahm). This is a precise taxonomy of epistemic states.

Key Terms
ظن
جهل مضاد
جهل بسيط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes a true opinion from a false opinion?
A) True opinion is certain, false opinion is not.
B) True opinion matches reality, false opinion does not.
C) True opinion is possible to be otherwise, false opinion is not.
D) True opinion involves ignorance, false opinion does not.

Key Takeaway
Opinion is a belief that lacks certainty; true opinion matches reality but could be false, while false opinion contradicts reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a believer who holds that a particular act is recommended (mandub) based on a hadith that is not definitively authentic. He acknowledges that the act might not be recommended. Classify his belief as true or false opinion, and identify the type of ignorance involved.
?
Explain why the following statement is false: 'If I believe something and it is true, then I have knowledge, not opinion.' Use the passage to correct this error.

Ignorance (Jahl): Simple and Compound
Author defines simple and compound ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate simple ignorance from compound ignorance based on the presence or absence of a false belief.

Core Idea
Compound ignorance is a false belief held with certainty, while simple ignorance is merely not knowing.

After discussing opinion, the passage turns to ignorance (jahl). It describes two kinds: simple ignorance, which is having no opinion at all, and compound ignorance, which is having a false belief that opposes the truth. This distinction is crucial for understanding the epistemology of error in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of simple ignorance
The passage says: 'simple is that there is no view in the soul that it is such.' This means the person has no belief about the matter at all. Identify this phrase in the Arabic.
Look for 'البسيط هو أن لا يكون في النفس رأي'.
2
Locate the definition of compound ignorance
Compound ignorance is defined as 'not having the true view while holding a false view opposite to knowledge in a definitive manner.' This means the person has a false belief and is certain of it.
Look for 'والمركب هو أن لا يكون في النفس الرأي الحق مع حصول رأي باطل مضاد العلم حصولاً قطعياً'.
3
Compare the two types
Simple ignorance is just absence of belief. Compound ignorance is absence of true belief plus presence of a false belief. The passage emphasizes that compound ignorance is 'absence of view with existence of another view'.
Compound ignorance is more dangerous because it involves a mistaken certitude.
4
Identify why the first is called 'simple'
The passage explicitly states: 'The first is called simple because it contains only absence of view, unlike the second which is absence of view with existence of another view.' This explains the terminology.
Simple ignorance has only one element (lack), compound has two (lack of truth + presence of falsehood).

Doubt and Illusion: The Extremes of Opinion
Author explains doubt and illusion as they relate to opinion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish doubt (shakk) from illusion (wahm) based on the balance of evidence between two opposing beliefs.

Core Idea
Doubt is equal probability; illusion is the weaker belief when opinion is stronger.

The final part of the passage discusses how knowledge and opinion relate, and introduces doubt and illusion. When two opposing beliefs have equal strength, it is doubt. When one is stronger, it is opinion, and the weaker side is called illusion. Understanding these states helps us map the full spectrum of human belief from certainty to falsehood.

Guided Path
1
Find the condition for doubt
The passage says: 'if they are equal, it is doubt about the two matters.' Here 'they' refers to true and false opinion. So doubt occurs when the evidence for both sides is balanced.
Look for the phrase 'إذا تساويا كان شكا'.
2
Find the condition for opinion versus illusion
The passage continues: 'if they are not equal, one of them is opinion—the predominant one—and the other not; that would be illusion.' So opinion is the stronger belief, illusion is the weaker.
The term for illusion is 'wahm' (وهم).
3
Understand that knowledge and opinion are exclusive
The passage states: 'knowledge and opinion cannot coexist in one thing for one person.' This means that for a given proposition, you cannot simultaneously be certain and uncertain.
This reinforces the definition of opinion as lacking certainty.
4
Apply to a real example
Consider a person who is confronting two reports about a historical event. If both reports seem equally reliable, he is in doubt. If one report seems stronger, he holds an opinion, and the weaker report becomes mere illusion.
This shows how the categories apply to everyday reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Equivalence of Demands and Known Things
Author argues that demands and known things are equivalent, and discusses the order of 'what' and 'whether' questions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the 'what' (ma) and 'whether' (hal) demands and explain their interdependence in logical inquiry.

Core Idea
Every demand is for knowledge; once known, the demand ceases.

In this passage, the author explores the foundational relationship between the questions we ask and the knowledge we seek. He argues that demands (mataleb) and known things are coextensive—you only seek what you do not yet know. He then focuses on two fundamental types of demands: 'what' (ma) and 'whether' (hal), showing that the demand for essence ('what') ultimately reduces to a demand for existence or truth ('whether'). This insight is crucial for understanding how logical inquiry proceeds, especially in Islamic sciences where establishing first whether something exists precedes asking what it is.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
Read the first sentence: demands and known things are equivalent by seeking. Why? Because something is sought only to be known, so when it is known, the seeking stops. This is the key epistemological principle.
2
List the types of demands mentioned
The author refers to demands for 'what' (ma) and 'whether' (hal). Note that they are among those previously discussed. Recognize that 'what' concerns essence, and 'whether' concerns existence or truth.
3
Analyze the relationship between 'what' and 'whether'
The author states: 'the demand for 'what' regarding essence is a demand for 'whether''. This means that when you ask about the essence of something, you are implicitly asking whether that essence exists or is true. The 'what' question presupposes a 'whether' question.
This is a key insight: the 'what' demand reduces to a 'whether' demand.
4
Understand the order of inquiry
The author says that the 'what' demand precedes in name, but once both are given, the essence demand becomes clear. Also, after the 'whether' demand, a compound demand arises: what are the major and middle terms? The middle term is the cause (illa). This shows the progression from simple existence to full causal explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في أن المطالب والمعلومات بالطلب متساوية ] واعلم بأن المطالب والمعلومات بالطلب متساوية فإن الشيء إنما يطلب ليعلم فإذا علم بطل الطلب. والمطالب كما هي هي فإنها من جملة ما تقدم ذكره مثل مطلب «ما» و «هل» وغير ذلك ؛ وقد علم أن مطلب «ما» الذي بحسب الذات فهو مطلب هل لكنه قد سبق من حيث هو مطلب «ما» بحسب الاسم. فإذا أعطي ثم أعطي مطلب «هل» اتضح في الحال مقتضى مطلب بحسب الذات؛ ويتبع المطلب المركب من مطلبي الهل أيضًا على وجه حتى يكون كأنه يطلب ما الحد الأكبر وما الحد الأوسط؟ والحد الأوسط هو العلة، ويقع فيه طلب الما بعد طلب الهل على وجهين: أحدهما بالقوة. فإن طالب الهل في مثل هذا إنما يطلب عما هو مشكوك فيه؛ فيقتضي طلب هل أنه يطلب
Chapter: On that demands and known things are equivalent by seeking. Know that demands and known things are equivalent by seeking, for a thing is only sought to be known, so when it is known the seeking ceases. The demands are as they are, for they are among what was previously mentioned, such as the demand for 'what' and 'whether' and others. It is known that the demand for 'what' regarding essence is a demand for 'whether', but it preceded insofar as it is a demand for 'what' according to name. So when it is given, then the demand for 'whether' is given, the requirement of the demand regarding essence becomes clear immediately. And the compound demand from two 'whether' demands also follows, in a manner such that it becomes as if it seeks what the major term and what the middle term are. The middle term is the cause. The demand for 'what' after the demand for 'whether' occurs in two ways, one of them by potential. For the seeker of 'whether' in such a case seeks about what is doubtful; so the demand for 'whether' implies that he seeks...
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter on the equivalence of demands (mataleb) and known things. The author asserts that demands and known things are coextensive because something is sought only to be known; once known, the demand ceases. The demands are among those previously mentioned, like 'what' (ma) and 'whether' (hal). He clarifies that the demand for 'what' concerning essence is actually a demand for 'whether', but it precedes in name. Once both are given, the requirement of the essence-based demand becomes clear. Then a compound demand arises from two 'whether' demands, as if seeking what the major term and middle term are. The middle term is the cause. The demand for 'what' after 'whether' occurs in two ways, one potentially. The seeker of 'whether' in such cases seeks about what is doubtful, which implies seeking 'whether'. The text is cut off at the bottom of the page, but the argument is about the interconnection of these two types of demands in the process of inquiry.

Key Terms

هل
ما
حد أوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the relationship between the demand for 'what' (ma) and the demand for 'whether' (hal)?
A) They are completely independent and should be sought separately
B) The demand for 'what' regarding essence is actually a demand for 'whether'
C) The demand for 'whether' is more fundamental than 'what'
D) They are equivalent because both are demands for knowledge

Key Takeaway
The demand for 'what' regarding essence is a hidden demand for 'whether', so the answer to 'whether' is necessary for any further inquiry into essence.

?
In a usul al-fiqh inquiry, a mujtahid first asks 'Is this act obligatory or not?' (hal) and then 'What is the definition of obligation?' (ma). Show how the order follows the logical priority described in the passage.

How Abstraction from Sense Yields First Principles
First way senses aid intellect via abstraction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the process by which the intellect abstracts universal concepts from particular sense perceptions to arrive at self-evident propositions.

Core Idea
Sense provides particulars; intellect abstracts universals and judges self-evident truths.

In this lesson, we examine the first and most fundamental way the senses aid the intellect: through abstraction. The author explains that sense perception only grasps individual objects, but when these are stored in the imagination, the intellect strips away accidental features and composes universal propositions. Some of these become self-evident (awwaliyat) without needing a middle term. Understanding this process is essential for grasping the foundation of all demonstrative knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the roles of sense and intellect
Note that the author says sense only perceives particulars (al-shakhsi). The intellect then performs abstraction (tajrid) from quantity, quality, location, etc.
2
Trace the composition of universal propositions
After abstraction, the intellect combines concepts through affirmation and negation, leading to self-evident judgments that are assented to by themselves.
These are the awwaliyat.
3
Distinguish between self-evident and mediated knowledge
Some propositions are clear without a middle term; others require a middle term for demonstration. The first principles are the starting points.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعتبرت الذاتية والعرضية بين الأوصاف والموصوفات في هذه المعاني الكلية فتجرَّدت لها الفصول والأجناس والأنواع والأعراض اللازمة والمفارقة. ثم أخذت في أنحاء التركيب بعضها على التركيب الخاص بالقول الشارح لمعنى الشيء كالحد والرسم، وبعضها بالتركيب الخاص بالقول الجازم فيما لم يتوقف في الحكم إليه بعد هذا التركيب كان أوليا. وما لم يتوقف فيه كان ذلك محتاجاً إلى بيان توسط : فهذا وجه من وجوه إعانة الحس في حصول الأوليات؛ وهو إعانة على سبيل العرض؛ إذ الحس لا يدرك إلا الشخصي لكن الشخصيات إذا استقرت في الخيال متأدية إليه من الحسّ أقبل العقل على تجريدها من الكم والكيف والأين وغير ذلك، ثم ألفها بعد ذلك بالإيجاب / والسلب فلاح له ما يجب أن يصدق به بذاته، وتوقف فيما ليس كذلك إلى حصول الوسط. وقد يستعين العقل بالحس والأوليات بطريق الاستقراء أيضًا كما مرَّ من قبل، وقد يعينه بطريق التجربة لا في الأوليات بل فيما لا يحصل إلا بالتجربة، وقد يعينه بطريق الحدس أيضًا علة ما عرف، فهذه وجوه إعانة الحس للعقل في الأوليات وغيرها. واعلم بأن مبدأ البرهان كما لا يكتسب بالبرهان فكذلك مبدأ العلم لا يوجد بقوة العلم؛ ولم تبق قوة تصلح لهذا إلا العقل، فهذه القوة هي قوة العقل النظري.
Then the essential and accidental [relations] between attributes and described objects were considered in these universal meanings, and from them were abstracted the differentiae, genera, species, and necessary and separable accidents. Then they were taken in the modes of composition: some in the special composition of the explanatory statement of the meaning of a thing, like definition (hadd) and description (rasm); and some in the special composition of the decisive statement regarding what does not depend on judgment after this composition — that would be primary. And what does depend on it requires the clarification of a middle term. This is one of the ways the sense aids in acquiring first principles; it is an aid in an accidental manner, for sense only perceives the particular. But when particulars settle in the imagination conveyed to it from sense, the intellect undertakes to abstract them from quantity, quality, location, and so on, then composes them afterward by affirmation and negation, and then there becomes clear to it what must be assented to by itself, and it stops short in what is not like that until the middle term is obtained. And sometimes the intellect uses sense and first principles by way of induction as has passed before; and sometimes it is aided by way of experience, not in first principles but in what is only obtained by experience; and sometimes it is aided by way of intuition as well as the cause of what is known. These are the ways the sense aids the intellect in first principles and other matters. And know that just as the starting point of demonstration is not acquired by demonstration, likewise the starting point of knowledge does not come about by the power of knowledge; and no power remains suitable for this except the intellect. This power is the power of the theoretical intellect.
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This passage explains how the senses assist the intellect in acquiring first principles (awwaliyat) and other knowledge. The author begins by describing the process of abstraction: from particular sense perceptions, the intellect extracts universal concepts like genera, species, differentiae, and accidents. This is the first mode of assistance — the sense provides particular images which the intellect then abstracts from accidental features (e.g., quantity, quality) and combines through affirmation and negation, leading to self-evident propositions. This aid is indirect (bil-'arad) because sense only perceives particulars, but the intellect universalizes. The author then lists three additional ways: induction (istiqra'), where the intellect uses sense and first principles; experience (tajriba), which yields knowledge not obtainable otherwise; and intuition (hads), which grasps causes. Finally, the author emphasizes that the starting point of demonstration (burhan) is not itself demonstratively acquired; it is grasped by the theoretical intellect alone. Thus, the senses provide raw data, but the intellect's innate power is necessary for first principles.

Key Terms
الأوليات
الحس
التجريد
العقل النظري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
